Refuting the Futility Objection of the Mu'tazila
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila objection about giving strength to a known disbeliever.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila argument that giving strength to a known disbeliever is futile, by distinguishing divine wisdom from human analogies.

Core Idea
God's wisdom transcends human analogies; giving strength serves multiple purposes, not solely belief.

The Mu'tazila argued that it is futile for God to grant strength to someone He knows will disbelieve, as it seems like enabling sin. Imam Maturidi meets this objection head-on, showing that divine wisdom operates beyond limited human reasoning. He not only refutes the claim but also establishes a crucial principle: what appears harmful in human experience (al-shahid) does not necessarily apply to the unseen reality of God's actions (al-gha'ib).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Get a sense of Maturidi's argumentative style.
Notice the self-objection style he uses to present the Mu'tazila view.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
The Mu'tazila claim that giving strength to a known disbeliever is futile. Pinpoint the exact phrase in the passage that states this.
The claim is implicit in the objection he counters: 'المعتزلة تزعم أنه أعطى القوة ليؤمن بها'.
3
Analyze Maturidi's two responses
First, he argues that strength can be given for other benefits (like feeding a known disbeliever). Second, he distinguishes between the witnessed world (shahid) and the unseen (gha'ib), stating that what is harmful in the former does not apply to the latter.
The analogy of feeding a disbeliever who will not believe is key.
4
Reflect on the divine transcendence conclusion
Maturidi concludes that God's wisdom is beyond the understanding of a limited intellect, so the objection fails. Consider why this is a powerful theological principle.
This reinforces the incommensurability of divine and human actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : ذلك الذي قيل فيمن يفعل الحاجة. قيل : والأول أيضا قبح ممن لا يملك التقوية لو طلب منه وتضرع إليه. ثم عارض نفسه بمن يدفع إلى عبده ما يعلم يعصيه به فقال : قد يكون ذلك حكمة نحو من يعلم بخير الرسول أنه لا يؤمن، يجوز أن يطعمه ونحو ذلك مما يعلم من تأمله جهله بما عارض به نفسه؛ لأن الذي ذكره لا يجوز أن يعطيه ليؤمن بذلك بعد علمه بأنه لا يفعل، وإنما يعطيه لمنافع سوى هذا. والمعتزلة تزعم أنه أعطى القوة ليؤمن بها، وهو يعلم أنه يكفر بها، فليس ذلك مما قدر فى شيء. ثم المعارضة كانت فيمن يعصيه، فلا أحد يعد نفسه فى الحكماء إذا علم أن عبده بالذى يعطيه يعصيه ولا يكتسب رضاه بل يعمل بعداوته وشتمه، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ولكن ذا عندنه إنما قبح فى الشاهد لأن ذلك يضره ويدخل عليه الألم، وذلك لا يحتمل أمر الغائب، والله الموفق. ثم قال : لأن ليس لمن حضر أن يمتحن ونحو ذلك، فأنى له هذا بعد تقدير فعل الغائب بالشاهد على تحقيق ما يجد فيه؛ لأنه يقابل بجميع ما أنكر وادعى الخروج من الحكمة أن ذلك فى فعل من ذلك وصفه، فأما الله سبحانه وحكمته فهو متعال عن وقوف عقل مثله على حقيقة ذلك، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he said: That which was said about one who does a need. It was said: And the first is also ugly from one who does not have the power to strengthen if asked and implored. Then he objected to himself regarding one who gives his servant that which he knows he will disobey with, and said: That could be wisdom, like one who knows that the messenger's goodness will not cause faith, it is permissible to feed him, and similar things upon reflection of his ignorance of what he objected to himself. Because what he mentioned is not permissible to give him so that he may believe thereby after knowledge that he will not do it; rather he gives it for benefits other than this. And the Mu'tazila claim that He gave strength so that he may believe with it, while He knows he will disbelieve with it, so that is not from what is determined at all. Then the objection was concerning one who disobeys Him, so no one counts himself among the wise if he knows that his servant, by what he gives him, disobey him and does not earn his pleasure, rather he works with enmity and revilement. There is no power except through Allah. But this ugly thing in his view is only ugly in the witnessed world because it harms him and causes him pain, and that does not bear the matter of the unseen. And Allah is the Grantor of success. Then he said: Because it is not for the one present to test and the like, so how can he have this after estimating the act of the unseen by the witnessed to ascertain what he finds in it? For it faces all that he denied and claimed to exit from wisdom, that it is in the act of the One described as such. As for Allah, the Exalted, and His wisdom, He is exalted above the understanding of an intellect like his grasping the reality of that. There is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 867

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. Maturidi addresses the Mu'tazila argument that it is futile for God to give strength to someone He knows will disbelieve. He begins by discussing a case where someone gives to a needy person for necessity, and notes that even giving to a known wrongdoer can be wise if it serves other benefits, like feeding a known disbeliever for reasons other than faith. The Mu'tazila assert that God gives strength specifically for belief, which is negated by His foreknowledge. Maturidi counters that God gives strength for various purposes, not solely for belief. He then turns to the objection from common sense: a wise master would not give a servant what will cause disobedience. The response is that what appears ugly in the witnessed world (al-shahid) is due to harm or pain, but this does not apply to the unseen (al-gha'ib). God's wisdom transcends human analogies. Thus, the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
القوة
العلم
الحكمة
الشاهد
الغائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Maturidi denies divine foreknowledge or power; he affirms them while rejecting the Mu'tazila inference of futility.
Correction: Maturidi fully affirms God's foreknowledge and power, but argues that giving strength to a known disbeliever is not futile because strength can serve multiple purposes and divine wisdom is not constrained by human analogies of harm.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi reject the Mu'tazila claim that giving strength to a known disbeliever is futile?
A) Because God does not know the future.
B) Because strength can be given for other benefits, not just belief.
C) Because the disbeliever might change his mind.
D) Because strength is not a means to action.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the notion that divine giving of strength to a known disbeliever is futile by showing that strength can serve other purposes and that human analogies of harm do not apply to the divine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever secretly judged God's wisdom when you saw someone with talents or resources who uses them for harm? What does Maturidi's argument reveal about the limits of your own understanding? Will you now trust that God's giving has purposes beyond your comprehension?
?
Think of an ability or blessing you possess that you have used for disobedience. Does this lesson change how you view God's continued granting of that ability? How will you respond to His patience and wisdom instead of assuming He should have withheld it?
?
The Mu'tazila demanded that God's actions fit their sense of fairness. When have you demanded that God explain Himself to you? Can you genuinely surrender to the fact that His wisdom is 'exalted above the understanding of an intellect'? What step will you take this week to practice that surrender?

Refuting the Misinterpretation of the Burden Verse
Maturidi refutes the misinterpretation of the burden verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the claim that the verse 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' removes obligation at the moment of action.

Core Idea
Dropping obligation at action time contradicts the verse's affirmation of capacity.

The Qur'anic verse 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' (2:286) is often invoked to discuss human ability. Some argue it means obligation is lifted at the very moment of action, implying that one is not required to perform what they cannot immediately do. Maturidi refutes this by showing that such a view actually burdens the person beyond capacity, making the obligation impossible to fulfill.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice that Maturidi states he has already explained the correct interpretation, and here he focuses on refuting a false claim.
The passage is a single sentence; pay attention to the logical flow.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent claims that at the time of action, the obligation is dropped and the ability to perform it is nullified. Find this in the Arabic: 'في إسقاط التكليف وقت الفعل وإبطال القدرة عليه'.
This is the misinterpretation Maturidi refutes.
3
Understand the consequence
Maturidi argues that this leads to the obligated person being burdened beyond capacity. In Arabic: 'فيصير في التحصيل المكلف على غير الوسع'. Ask yourself: why does dropping obligation at action time result in burdening beyond capacity?
Because if one is required to do something but the obligation vanishes exactly when they are supposed to do it, they can never fulfill it.
4
Grasp why it is ugly
Maturidi calls this claim 'ugly' (قبح). Reflect on why contradicting the verse's clear statement that Allah does not burden beyond capacity is considered ugly in theological terms.
This reinforces the correct understanding: the verse affirms capacity, it does not excuse performance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بينا تأويل قوله : لا يُكَلِّفُ اللهُ نَفْسًا إِلَّا وُسْعَهَا ، وبينا قبح قوله / في إسقاط التكليف وقت الفعل وإبطال القدرة عليه ، فيصير في التحصيل المكلف على غير الوسع .
And we have already explained the interpretation of His saying: 'Allah does not burden a soul except with its capacity,' and we have shown the ugliness of their saying in dropping the obligation at the time of action and nullifying the ability to do it, so that in effect the obligated is burdened beyond capacity.
Source pages p. 868

Commentary
This passage is a concise refutation of a specific misinterpretation of the Qur'anic verse 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' (2:286). The author states that they have already provided the correct interpretation (ta'wīl) of this verse. They then identify the opponent's error: the opponent claims that this verse means that obligation is lifted at the very moment of action, and that the ability (qudra) to perform the act is absent. The author argues that this is actually ugly (qabīḥ) because the net effect is that the obligated person is being burdened beyond their capacity. In other words, by removing the obligation at the time of action, the opponent inadvertently makes the obligation impossible to fulfill, which contradicts the verse's intent. The author's point is that the verse affirms capacity, not that it removes obligation at the moment of performance.

Key Terms
التكليف
الوسع
القدرة
الإسقاط
القبح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The burden verse means that Allah does not require actions that exceed one's immediate physical ability, so obligation is dropped at the moment of action.
Correction: Actually, dropping obligation at action time makes the obligation impossible to fulfill, burdening beyond capacity. The verse affirms capacity, not removal of obligation.

Checkpoint
What is the 'ugly' consequence of the opponent's interpretation of the burden verse according to Maturidi?
A) It makes the obligated person sinless.
B) It results in the obligated being burdened beyond capacity.
C) It denies God's mercy.
D) It contradicts the Sunnah.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that dropping obligation at the time of action actually contradicts the verse's affirmation of capacity, making the obligation impossible and thus ugly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a divine command that feels heavy, do you ever subconsciously reinterpret it to lighten your responsibility? This lesson shows such reinterpretation can make the obligation impossible. Are you willing to accept the full weight of what Allah has commanded, trusting that He has given you the capacity to fulfill it?
?
The opponents thought they were being merciful by dropping the obligation at action time. Have you ever made a choice that seemed merciful but caused greater harm? Where in your life might you be avoiding a duty now, thinking it's too much, while Allah has already ensured you have the capacity?
?
This lesson reveals a subtle contradiction: dropping obligation when action is due makes the burden impossible. How does this challenge your tendency to postpone or avoid acts of worship or good deeds until it's 'too late'? What step can you take today to act on a command you've been neglecting?

The Sword Story and the Refutation of Al-Aṣlaḥ
Maturidi argues against al-aṣlaḥ using the sword story.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the story of the sword to argue that prevention is not always better, thereby refuting the Mu'tazilite obligation of al-aṣlaḥ.

Core Idea
If prevention were always better, Allah would prevent all sin.

Maturidi employs a historical narrative to challenge a rationalist theory about divine justice. The story of a man who drew his sword on the Prophet serves as a test case for whether prevention (manʿ) is always better for a person's religion. By analyzing this example, Maturidi exposes a contradiction in the Mu'tazilite claim that Allah must always do what is best for His servants.

Guided Path
1
Read the story and the question
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi presents a specific narrated event and then poses a dilemma: was the prevention better for that person's religion, or would letting him act have been better?
The event is from a hadith where a man attempted to kill the Prophet but Allah prevented him.
2
Analyze the two possible answers
Maturidi considers two answers: 'letting go' (iṭlāq) or 'prevention' (manʿ). For each, he states the implication that follows. Identify each implication in the text.
The first answer implies Allah may not do the best; the second implies prevention can be better.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
If the opponent admits prevention is better, then every sinner who is not prevented has not had the best done for them. This contradicts the Mu'tazilite position that Allah always does the best.
Maturidi ends with a reminder of reliance on Allah's power.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Understand that this argument does not deny Allah's ability to prevent sin, but refutes the claim that He is obligated to do so. It defends divine wisdom and justice without imposing human notions of 'best'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال : المروى عن الذي روى أنه سنل سيفه على رسول الله فمنعه الله بقبض يده ، أكان ذلك المنع أصلح له في الدين وأخير له أو الإطلاق ؟ فإن قال : الإطلاق ، فقد أقرّ بأن الله قد يفعل بعباده ما كان غيره أصلح فى الدين : وإن قال : المنع ، فقد أقر أن المنع قد يكون أصلح ، فكل عاص لم يمنع عنه لم يفعل به الأصلح ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then it is said: The narrated story about the one who drew his sword upon the Messenger of Allah, and Allah prevented him by gripping his hand - was that prevention better (aṣlaḥ) for him in religion and more good (akhyar) for him, or letting go? If he says: letting go, then he has acknowledged that Allah may do with His servants what is not better in religion. And if he says: prevention, then he has acknowledged that prevention may be better. Then every sinner who was not prevented has not had the best done for him. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 868

Commentary
This passage employs a historical example to advance the argument about what is 'best' (aṣlaḥ) for a person in religion, a key topic in discussions of divine justice. The author refers to a well-known story (found in hadith) of a man who attempted to kill the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) but Allah prevented him by gripping his hand. The author presents a dilemma: was the prevention (manʿ) better for that man's religion, or would letting him act (iṭlāq) have been better? If the opponent answers that letting him act would have been better, then they admit that Allah sometimes does what is not best for His servants. If they answer that prevention was better, then they admit that prevention can be better. From this, the author draws a broader implication: if prevention is better, then every sinner who is not prevented from sinning has not been treated in the best way. This creates a difficulty for those who claim Allah always does what is best (al-aṣlaḥ), because it would imply that Allah must prevent all sins to be consistent, which is clearly not the case. The passage ends with a reminder of reliance on Allah's power.

Key Terms
المنع
الإطلاق
الأصلح
الدين
العاصي
المروي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this story proves that Allah always prevents sin or that He is unable to prevent sin.
Correction: Maturidi is not denying divine power; he is using the story to challenge the Mu'tazilite claim that Allah is obligated to do the best. The argument shows that even in this case, prevention is not clearly better, so the theory of al-aṣlaḥ is flawed.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi argue from the story of the sword?
A) That Allah always prevents sin.
B) That if prevention is better, then every sinner who is not prevented has not received the best treatment.
C) That letting the man act would have been better.
D) That the story is fabricated.

Key Takeaway
The story of the sword demonstrates that the Mu'tazilite theory of al-aṣlaḥ leads to a contradiction, thereby affirming that divine actions are not bound by human notions of what is best.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial that you see as not the 'best' for you, do you find yourself questioning Allah's wisdom? How does this lesson challenge your expectation that Allah must always give you what you consider 'best' in that moment? What can you do to surrender your own definition of 'best' to His?
?
The sword story shows that Allah prevented a man from killing the Prophet, but He does not prevent every sin. Think of a specific sin you committed that Allah allowed to happen. Does this lesson change how you view that event? Are you willing to accept that Allah's decision not to prevent it was better for you in ways you cannot see? What would it take to trust Him fully in that area?
?
If Allah is not obligated to do what is 'best' by our human standards, how does that affect your relationship with Him? What is one concrete area of your life—your career, relationships, or health—where you are clinging to your own plan of what is 'best'? Will you today consciously release that to Allah's will, even if it doesn't match your expectations?

The Objection of Resemblance in Power and Its Refutation
Objection-and-answer with a fundamental principle of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming human power does not imply resemblance to God, and the principle that power is always for an act.

Core Idea
Human power is derived from Allah; His power is unique and independent.

This passage from Imam Maturidi addresses a key objection: if humans have power, don't they resemble God? The answer lies in distinguishing created power from divine power. We'll see how the objection is refuted through two arguments and a fundamental principle that power cannot exist without an act.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial answer
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Note how the opponent’s objection is framed and the immediate response from the Muslim theologian.
The response relies on the distinction of being powerful 'by Allah' versus 'by itself'.
2
Analyze the two aspects of the question
The passage presents two aspects: uniqueness in power and independence from God. Understand how each aspect refutes the opponent’s own previous positions or leads to absurd conclusions.
The first aspect uses the opponent's own argument against the Dualists; the second shows that if human power were independent, it would negate need for God.
3
Grasp the principle of power for the act
Imam Maturidi states: 'power is impossible for it to exist not for an act.' This is a key principle in kalam. Understand what it means and how it refutes the claim that power can precede the act (takhalluf).
If power existed before the act, then God would give power for something impossible, which is corrupt.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends with 'there is no power except by Allah.' Consider how this ties the entire argument back to tawhid and dependence on God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ذكر سؤال خصمه من وجه لا يحتمل خصمه قوله ، فقال : إذ أثبتم لأنفسكم القدرة فقد أشبهتم الله بها ، فقال : لا يجب ذا ؛ لما قدرت به ، وهو لا بغيره ، كما يقال في علم به . قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : لو قدرت بالله لم يجز أن تزول بقدرتك قدرة الله ، كما إذ علمت به لم يزل بعلمك الله وبعد ، فإن السؤال من وجهين: أحدهما الانفراد بالقدرة، وبه احتججت في نقض قول الثنوية ، فيلزمك فى هذا ، والثانى أن ذلك يوجب الغنى عن الله في الفعل قبل وجوده ، ولا يجوز أن يكون الله يغنى أحدا عن نفسه . فإن قلت : يحتاج إليه فى الإبقاء ، أحلت عندك ؛ لأنها لا تحتمل ، وإن قلت : يحدث أخرى، فقد أغناه عنه فى وقت ، ولو جاز ذلك فى الوقت - مع قيام العبودة يجوز أبدا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم الأصل أن القدرة محال كونها لا للفعل ، وكذلك العجز لا عن فعل ، ثم قد يجوز أن يكون قادرا فى وقت للفعل ، يعجز فى الوقت الثانى ؛ إذ معلوم وجود مثله ، فيكون الله تعالى معطيا القوة لشيء يستحيل كونه ، وفى ذلك فساد كون القوة للفعل ، فألزم ما أوجبه العقل إنها لا تكون إلا للفعل إبطال القول بالتقدم ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then he mentioned the question of his opponent in a way that his opponent cannot bear his saying, so he said: Since you have affirmed for yourselves power, you have resembled Allah by it. He said: This is not necessary, because you are powerful by Him, and He is not by other than Him, as is said concerning knowledge by Him. Sheikh Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: If you were powerful by Allah, it would not be permissible for the power of Allah to cease by your power, just as when you know by Him, Allah does not cease by your knowledge. Then the question is from two aspects: one is uniqueness in power, and by that you argued in nullifying the saying of the Dualists, so it follows for you in this; the second is that that necessitates independence from Allah in the act before its existence, and it is not permissible that Allah should make anyone independent of Himself. If you say: he needs Him for preservation, you have made it ambiguous in your view, because it does not bear that; and if you say: He creates another [power], then He has made him independent of Him in a time, and if that were permissible in time with the establishment of servitude, it would be permissible forever, and there is no power except by Allah. Then the principle: power is impossible for it to exist not for an act, likewise inability not from an act; then it may be that he is powerful in a time for an act, but incapable in the second time, since the existence of similar is known; then Allah would be giving power to something whose existence is impossible, and in that is corruption of power being for the act; so he concluded what the intellect necessitated: that it is only for the act, nullifying the claim of antecedent [power], and there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 872

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer combined with a fundamental principle. The opponent objects: if you (Muslims) affirm power for yourselves, you resemble God in His attribute of power. The answer: no, because your power is from God (bil-lāh), while God's power is not from another. Abu Mansur elaborates: if your power were from God, it would not diminish His power. The question has two aspects. First, claiming uniqueness in power: the opponent himself used this argument against the Dualists, so it applies to him. Second, claiming independence from God: if man were powerful independently, he would not need God for the act. If one says he needs God for preservation, that is ambiguous; if he says God creates another power, that implies temporary independence. Then the principle: power cannot exist without an act (i.e., it must be related to an act); likewise, inability is not unrelated to an act. It is possible to be powerful for an act at one time and incapable at another, but power cannot precede its act. If power existed before the act, then God would give power for something impossible (since the act may never occur). Reason necessitates that power is only for the act, refuting the claim of antecedent power (takhalluf). The passage ends with 'there is no power except by Allah.'

Key Terms
القدرة (power / qudra)
الانفراد (uniqueness)
الغنى عن الله (independence from God)
الإبقاء (preservation)
الفعل (act)
العجز (inability)
التقدم (antecedence / takhalluf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think human power is an independent attribute like God's power, implying resemblance.
Correction: Human power is derived from Allah and depends on Him; it is not self-subsisting. God's power is unique and independent.

Checkpoint
What does the principle 'القدرة محال كونها لا للفعل' (power is impossible for it to exist not for an act) entail?
A) Power can exist without any act
B) Power must be related to a specific act
C) Power precedes every act
D) Power is eternal

Key Takeaway
Power is always related to an act, and human power is entirely dependent on Allah, not independent or resembling His unique power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accomplish something, do you ever feel a sense of independence from Allah? How does this lesson challenge that feeling?
?
The passage says that claiming independence from Allah is impossible for a servant. In what areas of your life do you subtly act as if you are independent, even momentarily?
?
Consider the phrase 'there is no power except by Allah.' How often does this truly echo in your heart when you face a difficult task? What would change if you lived this truth?

Divine Attributes

Power Over Faith Does Not Imply Power Over God
Maturidi presents and answers the objection that power to believe implies power over God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why having the power to believe does not imply having power over God, even for those whom God knows will not believe.

Core Idea
Power is not the act; human power does not challenge divine foreknowledge.

In theological discussions about predestination and free will, a tricky objection arises: if a person has the power to believe, does that mean they have power over God? This is especially problematic for figures like Pharaoh, whom God knows will never believe. The author presents this objection and offers a clear distinction that safeguards both divine knowledge and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the objection is stated and the immediate answer.
The objection claims power over faith implies power over God.
2
Identify the key distinction
Find where the author says 'power is not the faith'. This is the core of the answer.
3
Understand the counter-rebuttal
Notice the phrase 'if that were incumbent upon us concerning power, it would be incumbent upon you concerning the command'. This shows the author turning the argument back.
This is a common dialectical technique.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why does this distinction resolve the problem? How does it preserve both human responsibility and divine knowledge?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسه بأمر فرعين إنه لو كان يقدر على الإيمان لكان يقدر على الله ، وهذا في فرعون وكل من في علم الله أنه لا يؤمن . فأجاب بأن ذا لا يجب لأن القدرة غير الإيمان الذى هو المعلوم أنه لا يكون ، ولو لزمنا ذلك فى القوة ليلزمكم في الأمر . ثم عارض بقدرة الله على إنشاء العالم ليحال من غير أن يجوز الوصف بالقدرة على إبطال علمه ، فمثله الأول . ثم عارض حسيناً بالإطلاق أنه أطلق بينه وبين الإيمان، أتقول في أنه أطلق فى إبطال علم الله ؟ ثم قال : الله عالم أن لو كان كيف يكون ، فلو كان لم يكن يخرج من علم الله
Then he objected to himself with two branches: If he were able to believe, he would be able over Allah, and this applies to Pharaoh and everyone of whom in the knowledge of Allah that he will not believe. So he answered that this is not necessary because power is not the faith that is known that it will not occur, and if that were incumbent upon us concerning power, it would be incumbent upon you concerning the command. Then he objected by the power of Allah to create the world being impossible from such that it is not permissible to describe [Him] as able to nullify His knowledge, so it is like the first. Then he objected regarding the absolute [expression] that he made [the relation] between him and faith absolute: do you say that he made it absolute in nullifying the knowledge of Allah? Then he said: Allah knows what would be if it were, and if it were, it would not depart from the knowledge of Allah.
Source pages p. 873

Commentary
This passage is a self-objection and answer. The author raises a difficulty: if a person (like Pharaoh) has power (qudra) to believe, then he would have power over God, because believing is an act that, if performed, would contradict God's knowledge that he will not believe. The answer: this does not follow, because power is distinct from the act of faith; power is the ability, not the act itself. God's knowledge is of what will not happen, not of what is impossible. If the opponent argues that this implies power to nullify God's knowledge, the author responds by analogy: God's power to create the world does not imply power to nullify His own knowledge. Then the author deals with an objection about absolute expressions: if one says 'he is able to believe' absolutely, does that imply he can nullify God's knowledge? No, because 'ability' here is the ability to perform an act if he willed, but God's knowledge precludes that actualization. Finally, the author invokes the principle that God knows what would happen if it were to occur, and if it did, it would still be within His knowledge. This is a dense passage dealing with the relationship between divine foreknowledge and human power.

Key Terms
القدرة (power)
الإيمان (faith)
علم الله (knowledge of God)
الفرعون (Pharaoh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human power over an act means one can change God's knowledge.
Correction: Power is the ability, not the actualization; God's knowledge is of actual occurrences, not mere possibilities.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that power over faith does not imply power over God, even for those known not to believe?
A) Because God's knowledge can change
B) Because power is distinct from the act that is known not to occur
C) Because no one has power over God
D) Because faith is impossible for Pharaoh

Key Takeaway
Human power to believe does not conflict with divine foreknowledge because power is not the same as the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that because God already knows what you will do, your choices aren't truly yours? How does the distinction between power and the act in this lesson free you from that feeling? What will you do the next time that thought arises?
?
The objection in the passage uses Pharaoh to argue that he never had real power to believe. Are there areas in your life where you have excused your inaction by saying 'God didn't will it' or 'it wasn't meant to be'? How does this lesson challenge you to take responsibility for your abilities?
?
This lesson separates having the ability to do something from actually doing it. Think of a good deed you know you have the power to do but haven't done. What is holding you back from turning that ability into action? Commit to one specific step you will take today.

Nullifying Divine Knowledge and Absolute Expressions
Maturidi answers objections about nullifying divine knowledge and clarifies absolute expressions of ability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the analogy of God's power to create the world to argue against nullifying divine knowledge, and how absolute expressions of human ability are properly understood.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge is immutable; human ability is conditioned by that knowledge.

The author continues to defend the compatibility of human power and divine foreknowledge. He raises a new objection: how can we say God has power to create the world but not power to nullify His knowledge? And what about absolute statements like 'he is able'? He answers by distinguishing between types of power and emphasizing that God's knowledge encompasses all possibilities.

The objection tries to draw a parallel between human power and God's power.

Consider what 'the first' refers to from the previous lesson.
3
Examine the objection about absolute expressions

4
Grasp the final principle
The passage concludes with: 'Allah knows what would be if it were, and if it were, it would not depart from the knowledge of Allah.' Reflect on how this resolves the issue.
This reinforces God's knowledge as all-encompassing.

Refutation of the Doctrine of al-Aslah
Maturidi refutes the first argument for al-aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational refutation of the claim that God is obligated to do what is best for His creation.

Core Idea
God's sovereignty is absolute; He is not bound by human notions of 'best'.

Maturidi begins by critiquing the doctrinal claim known as al-aslah—the idea that God must always choose the optimal course for His creatures. He argues that this notion misplaces the basis of divine action, substituting human estimation for God's own declaration. In this passage, he presents a concise logical argument that undermines the entire doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without stopping. Notice the rhythm and key terms like 'الأصلح' and 'الاعتبار'.
This passage is dense; focus on the flow of the argument.
2
Understand the translation
Compare the literal translation with the Arabic. Identify the main claim and its refutation.
The phrase 'ووجه الاعتبار به ليس على ما قدر' is crucial.
3
Analyze the logical structure
Break down the argument into its components: premise, hypothetical consequence, and conclusion.
Use the guided breakdown below for clarity.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal implication
Consider why Maturidi insists that God's actions are not subject to human criteria of optimality.
This refutes a possible limitation on divine power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : ووجه الاعتبار به ليس على ما قدر ، ولكن بما يقول بالأصلح ، ومعلوم أن الله لو لم يكن ملكه على ما ملكه لم يكن ويمنع من يمنعه عن طاعة رسوله ، وكان ذلك أقل من اضلة . ليقدر أن يضل للغواية وأقرب إلى الطاعة ، فثبت أن القول بالأصلح باطل مضمحل .
Shaykh Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: The aspect of consideration concerning it is not according to what was estimated, but according to what He says concerning the optimal (al-aslah). It is known that if God did not have His ownership as He owns, He would not be, and He would prevent the one whom He prevents from obeying His messenger, and that would be less than misguidance [addala?]. So that He might cause to stray for error and bring closer to obedience. Thus it is established that the statement concerning the optimal (al-aslah) is false and nullified.
Source pages p. 874

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the doctrine of al-aslah (the optimal), which holds that God is obligated to do what is best for His creatures. Maturidi begins by stating that the proper way to consider God's actions is not based on human estimation of what is best, but on what God Himself declares. He then argues that if God's ownership were not as He has it, He would not be God, and He could prevent someone from obeying His messenger. Such prevention would be less than misguidance, implying that God's power includes the ability to lead astray and to guide. The conclusion is that the doctrine of al-aslah is false and collapses. The author follows the argument's logical order: premise (correct basis of consideration), hypothetical consequence, and final refutation.

Key Terms
الأصلح
الاعتبار
القدرة
الملك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is morally obligated to do what is best for each creature in every situation.
Correction: Maturidi argues that God's actions are not constrained by human notions of optimal; His absolute ownership and power mean He is not obligated to act according to our estimates.

Checkpoint
On what basis does Maturidi argue that the doctrine of al-aslah is false?
A) Because God's actions are judged by human reason
B) Because God's power and ownership are absolute, not constrained by human notions of optimal
C) Because God never prevents anyone from obedience
D) Because misguidance is impossible for God

Key Takeaway
God's absolute sovereignty means He is not obligated to follow human ideas of what is best.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficulty that seems pointless or unfair, do you secretly believe God should have done something 'better' for you? How does today's lesson challenge that assumption? Commit to praising Him even when His plan contradicts your own estimation of what is best.
?
Have you ever looked at someone who is disobedient and questioned why God allowed them to go astray? The lesson teaches that God's sovereignty includes the ability to cause either guidance or misguidance. Does this make you more humble about your own guidance? Thank Allah for His mercy and beg Him to keep you firm.
?
The argument of al-aslah assumes that what is 'best' for you is clear to you. But what if the worst calamity is actually the best for your afterlife? Are you willing to accept that your judgment is limited and that Allah's wisdom is far beyond your own? Make a sincere intention to submit to His decree, whatever it may be.

The Absurdity of Empowering an Enemy
Maturidi refutes the claim that empowering a disbelieving enemy is wise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's second argument against the doctrine of al-aslah, demonstrating that empowering an enemy who will never believe is irrational and leads to logical absurdity.

Core Idea
Empowering a disbelieving enemy cannot be wise—it gives him leverage to claim favor over God.

Maturidi continues his refutation of the doctrine that God must do what is optimally beneficial for creation. Here he addresses the specific case of an enemy who God knows will never believe. If God empowers such an enemy, it would be patently irrational, as even basic reason recognizes. Moreover, it would allow the enemy to make boastful claims that undermine God's lordship and wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let Maturidi's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the rhetorical shift when the enemy begins speaking.
2
Grasp the initial premise
Identify Maturidi's claim that empowering an enemy is outside the bounds of wisdom by the intuition of reason. What makes it self-evidently irrational?
Think about basic logic: giving power to someone who will use it against you is unwise.
3
Analyze the enemy's hypothetical speech
Read the enemy's words carefully. What claims does he make about his own favor over God? How does he argue that God's perfection depends on his choice?
The enemy says: 'if I wished I would have done it, then complete for You the lordship...'
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why this scenario is absurd and how it refutes the doctrine that God must do the optimal. What does this say about God's wisdom and independence?
The argument shows that such empowering would allow a creature to claim superiority, which is impossible for God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثانى أنه إذا أخبر أنه لا يؤمن بالله ، وقد علم ذلك وهو عدوه ، وإقدار العدو على تسفيه المقدر وتقويته على نقض ملكه وإبطاله ربوبيته خارج عن حد الحكمة ببديهة العقل ، مع ما فيه تمكين عدوه الأعظم مينة له عليه أن يقول لي عليك كل منة ، أو ملكنى نقض ربوبتك ، بما لا يكون ربا جاهلا ، وقويتي على إزالة حكمتك ، بما لا يكون حكيم كذوب ، وقد جعلت لى القدرة على ذلك ، وبذلك أمرتني ، وقد تعلم أنى لو شئت لفعلت فتمت لك الربوبية وسلمت لك الحكمة ، فمينتي عليك أعظم ونعمنى لديك أعم ، فبأى نعمة لك تعاقبني ، وبأى حكمة تأمرنى ، وبي تمت لك ؟ ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And the second [argument]: that when He informs that he will not believe in God – and He knows that, and he is His enemy – and enabling the enemy to belittle the Almighty and strengthen him to nullify His kingdom and invalidate His lordship is outside the bounds of wisdom by the intuition of reason, along with what is in it of empowering His greatest enemy as a favor (minna) upon him, so that he may say: 'I have all favor over You, or You gave me power to nullify Your lordship – while You would not be a ignorant lord – and You strengthened me to remove Your wisdom – while You would not be a lying wise – and You gave me the power for that, and by that You commanded me, and You know that if I wished I would have done it, then complete for You the lordship and safe for You the wisdom, so my favor (minnati) upon You is greater and my blessings (ni'ami) to You are more general, so by which blessing of Yours do You punish me, and by which wisdom do You command me, and through me was completed for You?' And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 874

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument against the doctrine of al-aslah, focusing on the case of an enemy who God knows will never believe. Maturidi argues that empowering such an enemy to mock and undermine God's kingdom and lordship is clearly outside the bounds of wisdom, as recognized by basic reason. He then elaborates a hypothetical speech from the empowered enemy, who claims that God's granting him power amounts to a favor, allowing him to boast that he could have disobeyed, and that through him God's lordship and wisdom are perfected. The enemy asks: by what blessing does God punish me, and by what wisdom does He command me, if through me His perfection is achieved? The argument concludes that this scenario is absurd, and thus the doctrine that God must do the optimal fails. The author uses a dramatic rhetorical device to highlight the contradiction.

Key Terms
الثانى
إقدار العدو
الحكمة
المنة
الربوبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That empowering a disbeliever is a favor from God that demonstrates wisdom.
Correction: Maturidi shows that such empowering would allow the enemy to claim favor over God and assert that God's perfection depends on his choice, which is absurd and contradicts divine wisdom.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's second argument, what does empowering the enemy allow him to claim?
A) That God is unjust
B) That through his obedience God's lordship is perfected
C) That through his potential disobedience God's lordship and wisdom are perfected, giving him leverage
D) That God's power is limited

Key Takeaway
Empowering a disbeliever to challenge God's lordship contradicts wisdom and leads to absurd claims of favor, refuting the doctrine of al-aslah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If you were the disbeliever in the argument, would you dare to say 'By me Your lordship is completed'? In what ways do you subtly act as if God needs your worship?

Command Implies Subjugation, Empowerment Implies Exaltation
Maturidi distinguishes command and empowerment in terms of subjugation and exaltation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi distinguishes between command and empowerment in terms of subjugation and exaltation.

Core Idea
Command demands, empowerment enables; one lowers, the other raises.

Maturidi opens by contrasting two divine acts: command (amr) and empowerment (iqdar). He argues that being commanded reveals lowliness, while possessing power reveals highness. This foundational distinction shapes his understanding of God's absolute sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening definition
Read the first few lines where Maturidi states that command shows lowliness and servitude, while power shows wealth and elevation.
Notice the immediate contrast between 'amr' and 'iqdar'.
2
Follow the argument for command
Maturidi argues that if there were no command or prohibition, concepts like belief and disbelief would lack meaning. He emphasizes that power's fruit is action, not command.
3
Observe the witnessed example
He supports his claim with the witnessed truth that a powerful person is not commanded; being commanded implies subjugation.
This is a key argument from the observable world.
4
Contrast with empowerment
Empowerment leads to elevation and highness, while command does not necessitate action.
This distinction is central to his theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
به ، يظهر ذله وعبوديته ، والقوة هى الغنى والعلو والرفعة ، فهو الوجه الذي به يبطل ربوبيته غيرا لله ، وليس فى الأمر ذلك ، على أنه لو لم يكن أمر ولا نهى كان القول. بيؤمن ويكفر ويقدر ولا يقدر لا معنى له ، ولا قوة إلا بالله و بعد ، فإن القدرة ثمرتها الفعل ، وبه يكون الذى ذكر ، لا بالأمر ؛ لذلك يصر الأمر بالأمر أمرا بالذي ذكرت ، فبالإقدار يصير ملكا غنيا مستخلفا لما إذا تم كان ربا إلها كان ربا إلها ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وبعد ، فإنا عارضنا بالذى طريق العلم به العقل من الوجه الذي ذكرت ، والأمر لا يناقض ما يوجبه العقل ، ولولا الأمر كان الأول بالعقل وحشيا ، فأما أن يعرف وجه الحكمة في الأمر أو لا يعرفه بالذي عرفناه لم يجب دفعه بما يتعذر عليه وجه الثانى ، ولا قوة إلا بالله وأيد الذي ذكرت أمر الشاهد أن كل قوى يرتفع ويجل بقوته ولا يؤمر الجليل العظيم بشيء ، ثبت أن فى الأمر ذلة واستعبادا ، فهو لا يوجب وفى الإقدار رفعه وعلوه فهو يوجب ، والله المرفق تری ذلك ، لم أنه قد نقض 10 وبعد ، فإذ لا توجد قدرة لا تضيع فوجودها يوجب الفعل الذي يقصد ، وحق الأمر اللزوم لا وجرد الفعل ، وكم من أمر به لا اثتمار هنالك ؛ فلذلك يجب به . وما ذكر فى الله فهو بقدرته ونفاذ مشيئته وجرى سلطانه ، فتمت ربوبيته واستوجب الجلال والرفعة بذاته ، لم يجز أن يكون فيه ما ذكر من الخوف ، بل به تمام الحكمة وعلو الرتبة ، وفى ايجاب ذلك لغيره نقض . ألا قول الثنوية بالذي ذكرت ، ولا يقبل قولهم بمعارضته مثله في الله أنه يقدر على أنه لا يفعل ، فإذا يقدر على نقض ربوبيته ، وبمثله جعلوه في غيره ٢٠ معارضاً موجبا ذلك ، فمثله الذي نحن فيه . وووجه آخر أنه لا يوجب لله قدرة ولا علما بقوله له قدرة بكذا وعلم بكذا لا معنى له ، وذلك متحقق في غيره ، ما علم فالمعارضة له لازمة. وأيضا أن الله إذ هو قادر بذاته ، عالم بذاته ، فمحال وضفه بالذي ذكر ؛ إذ بذلك تمت ربوبيته وجل سلطانه وألوهيته ، وقد يكون الشيء الموصوف بذاته مما إذا ثبت لغيره عليه سلطان تجرى قدرته عليه كالأعراض التي من مختلفات لأنفسهن ، والأجسام / لهن ، أو الأجسام التى من قائمات بأنفسهن والأعراض بهن ، ثم لله عليها سلطان وملك ، فلو جعلنا على الله في إبطال تقديره ونقص تدبيره وإزالة علمه ونفى الحقيقة عن خبره لكان تحت قدرة غيره وفى سلطان آخر ، فتعالى الله عن ذلك علوا كبيرا • صبح ما الإطلاق يرجع إلى الأمر ، وقد بيناه .
With it, his lowliness and servitude appear, and power is wealth, highness, and elevation. So it is the aspect by which his lordship is nullified for other than God, and that is not in the command. Had there been no command nor prohibition, the statement would have been: 'He believes and disbelieves, and He has power and has no power' has no meaning. There is no power except by God. Then, power's fruit is the act, and by it occurs what was mentioned, not by the command. Therefore the command is insisted upon by the command as an order for what you mentioned. By empowerment he becomes a king, rich, a successor, such that if completed he would be a lord, a god, he would be a lord, a god. There is no power except by God. Then, we have countered with that whose path of knowledge is reason in the aspect I mentioned. The command does not contradict what reason necessitates. Were there no command, the first would be brutish by reason. So whether one knows the aspect of wisdom in the command or does not know it by what we have known, its repulsion by that which makes the second aspect difficult for him is not necessary. There is no power except by God. And what I mentioned is supported by the witnessed fact that every powerful one is elevated and exalted by his power, and the great and mighty is not commanded. It is established that in command there is lowliness and enslavement. Thus it does not necessitate, while in empowerment there is his elevation and highness, so it necessitates. And God is the granter of success. You see that, because he has refuted. Then, since there is no power that does not perish, its existence necessitates the act intended, and the right of the command is binding, not the occurrence of the act. How many a command has no compliance there? Therefore it is necessitated by it. And what is mentioned concerning God is by His power, the penetration of His will, and the flow of His authority. So His lordship is perfected and He deserves majesty and highness by His essence. It is not permissible that what was mentioned of fear be in Him. Rather, by it is perfection of wisdom and high rank. And in necessitating that for other than Him is a refutation. Indeed, the saying of the Dualists is as I mentioned, and their saying is not accepted by countering it with the like in God: that He is able that He does not act, and thus He is able to nullify His lordship. And by the like they made in others a countering that necessitates that. So its like is what we are in. And another aspect: It does not necessitate for God power nor knowledge by saying 'He has power over such and such and knowledge of such and such' has no meaning, and that is actualized in others, what is known. So the countering is necessary for him. Also, since God is powerful by His essence, knowing by His essence, it is impossible to describe Him with what was mentioned; because by that His lordship is perfected, His authority and divinity are exalted. And something described by its essence may be such that if another has authority over it, his power runs over it, like the accidents that are different in themselves, and the bodies [that are] for them, or the bodies that are self-subsistent and the accidents through them. Then God has authority and ownership over them. If we were to place upon God nullification of His decree, deficiency of His planning, removal of His knowledge, and negation of truth from His report, He would be under the power of another and in another's authority. Far exalted is God above that, a great exaltation. In the morning, what is absolute returns to the command, and we have explained it.
Source pages p. 875

Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument by Maturidi on the nature of command (amr) and empowerment (iqdar) in relation to divine power. He begins by stating that command reveals lowliness and servitude, while power implies wealth, highness, and elevation. The point is that if God were to be subject to command from another, His lordship would be nullified. He then argues that if there were no command or prohibition, the very concepts of belief, disbelief, ability and inability would be meaningless. Power's fruit is the act itself, not the command; the command only demands, but empowerment enables. The author supports this by observing that in the witnessed world, a powerful being is elevated by his own power and is not commanded by anyone; being commanded implies subjugation. Therefore, command does not necessitate action, whereas power necessitates action when present. For God, His power, will, and authority are all by His essence; He is not subject to fear or dependence. The passage then addresses an objection from the Dualists, who claim that God can refrain from acting, which would imply He can nullify His own lordship. Maturidi refutes this by saying that such a characterization does not apply to God, as He is powerful and knowing by His essence. To claim that God has power over something in the sense that He can be limited would be meaningless. God cannot be under any other's power—if we were to say that His decree could be nullified or His knowledge removed, that would place Him under another's power, which is impossible. The passage concludes by affirming that all absoluteness returns to the command, which has been explained.

Key Terms
أمر (command)
إقدار (empowerment)
قدرة (power)
ذلة (lowliness)
عبودية (servitude)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's command is like human command and implies that He is dependent or subjugated.
Correction: Maturidi argues that in the witnessed world, the great and mighty are not commanded; command implies lowliness, so it cannot apply to God.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does command (amr) imply about the one commanded?
A) It implies power and exaltation
B) It implies lowliness and servitude
C) It implies wisdom and justice
D) It implies mercy and compassion

Key Takeaway
Command implies subjugation, while empowerment implies exaltation; thus, God's command is not like human command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear God's command in the Quran or through the Prophet, do you feel a sense of lowliness and servitude, or do you resist it? What does your reaction reveal about your heart?
?
You have been given abilities, wealth, and status—these are forms of empowerment from God. Do you use them to seek elevation in His sight by serving Him, or do they make you prideful and forgetful of the One who empowered you?
?
This lesson shows that God is too great to be commanded by anyone. Yet in prayer, do you sometimes approach Him as if you are dictating terms, rather than submitting as a servant? How can you change your dua to reflect true servitude and reliance on His empowerment?

Divine Attributes

Divine Power and Refutation of Dualists
Maturidi argues that divine power is by essence and refutes Dualist objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God's power is by essence and cannot be subject to any external power, refuting the Dualists.

Core Idea
God's power is by essence, not derived; He cannot be under another's authority.

Maturidi continues his argument by examining the nature of power and command in God. He refutes the Dualists who claim God can refrain from acting, and establishes that God's power and knowledge are by essence, immune to external influence.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument about power and act
Maturidi states that power necessitates its act when it exists, unlike command which only binds without guaranteeing action.
Notice the difference: power has a necessary connection to its act.
2
Understand the nature of divine power
For God, power, will, and authority are by essence; He cannot have fear or dependence.
3
Examine the Dualist objection
The Dualists claim that God can refrain from acting, implying He can nullify His own lordship. Maturidi refutes this.
This is a key polemical point.
4
Follow the refutation
Maturidi argues that such claims would place God under another's power, which is impossible. God is powerful and knowing by essence, not by external factors.
5
Conclude the argument
The passage ends with the affirmation that all absoluteness returns to command, which has been explained.
This ties back to the first lesson.

First Issue: Does God's Power Depart When He Empowers Creatures?
Maturidi refutes the Qadariyya's first argument using analogy and the nature of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Qadariyya claim that God's power over actions diminishes when He empowers humans is self-contradictory and implies that God's power is not essential.

Core Idea
If God's power were essential, it could not depart — Maturidi refutes the Qadariyya using analogy with knowledge and the nature of accidents.

Maturidi presents the first of two arguments against the Qadariyya sect, who claimed that God's power over human actions is transferred to humans upon empowerment. This argument shows the absurdity of such a claim by appealing to the nature of essential attributes and the distinction between bodies and accidents. Read the passage carefully to see how Maturidi builds his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the Qadariyya claim
Read the Arabic and translation. The Qadariyya say that God's power over movements and stillness departs when He empowers humans.
They think power can be transferred.
2
Notice Maturidi's analogy with divine knowledge
Maturidi compares power to knowledge: God's knowledge does not go away when He teaches others, so essential power should not depart either.
3
Understand the argument from accidents and bodies
Accidents are distinct from bodies; likewise, power and knowledge are separate from their possessors. If power could depart, it would be like an accident, not an essential attribute of God.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that if power can depart and return, it is the description of bodies and accidents — impossible for God. Hence, the Qadariyya view leads to absurdity.
Essential attributes cannot be transferred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومسألتان فى القدرة على القدرية يوجبان أن الله ليس بقادر بذاته ، أحدهما أنهم قالوا : يقدر الله جل ثناؤه على حركات العباد وسكونهم ، فلما أقدرهم على تلك الحركات والسكون زالت عنه القدرة عليها ، فيكون قادرا في التحقيق بغيره ؛ إذ هو بذاته على ما كان عليه . فلو كانت تلك القدرة له بذاته لم تكن تزول عنه إذا أقدر عليها غيره. ومما يبين ذلك أنه إذ كان عالما لذاته بكل شيء ، لم يذهب علمه لما أعلم غيره ، فمثله القدرة . مع ما كانت أدلة غيرية الأعراض للأجسام هي وجود الأجسام دونها ، ومثل ذلك علة غيرية القدرة والعلم في الشاهد إنهما غير الذي له ، فكذلك القول بالذي قالوا فى الله سبحانه ، ومما يزيد هذا وضوحا أنه لو أراد أن يحركه بحركة الاضطرار ويسكنه ذلك التسكين ومعه تلك القدرة لم يقدر عليه حتى يأخذ منه تلك القدرة ، فثبت أنه بها يقدر ، وهى تزول عنه وتعود إليه ، وهذا نعت الأجسام وحقيقة الأعراض ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . التي والثانى أن الله تعالى لما أقدر عبده على إتلاف شيء ذهبت عن الله قدرة الإبقاء الذي يريد ذلك ، والإبقاء فعله ، فصار عن فعله الذي هو / في الحقيقة ا في الحقيقة
And two issues concerning power against the Qadariyya which necessitate that God is not powerful by His essence. First: They said: God, exalted be His praise, has power over the movements of servants and their stillness. When He empowered them over those movements and stillness, power over them departed from Him, so He is in reality powerful by other than Himself, while He is by His essence as He was. If that power were His by essence, it would not depart from Him when He empowered others over it. What clarifies that is that since He is knowing by His essence of everything, His knowledge did not go away when He made others know. So similarly, power. Along with that, the evidence of the otherness of accidents for bodies is the existence of bodies without them, and similar to that is the otherness of power and knowledge in the witnessed world: they are other than that which has them. So likewise the saying of what they said concerning God, exalted. And what increases this in clarity is that if He wanted to move him with a movement of compulsion and make him still with that stilling while he had that power, He would not be able to do it until He took that power from him. Thus it is established that by it He has power, and it departs from Him and returns to Him. This is the description of bodies and the reality of accidents. There is no power except by God. The second: When God, exalted, empowered His servant over the destruction of something, the power of preservation (of what He wills) departed from God. Preservation is His act, so He becomes [deprived] of His act which is in reality...
Source pages p. 876

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct arguments (مسألتان) against the Qadariyya sect that conclude that God is not powerful by His essence if their claims were true. The first issue: The Qadariyya claim that God has power over human actions, but when He gives power to humans over those actions, His own power over them departs. This would imply that God's power is not essential but acquired, because if it were essential it could not depart. Maturidi refutes this by analogy: God's knowledge is essential and does not diminish when He teaches others; similarly, essential power should not diminish. Furthermore, in the physical world, accidents (a'rad) are distinct from bodies, and power and knowledge are observed to be separate from their possessors. If God's power could depart and return, that would be the attribute of bodies and accidents, which is incompatible with God. The second issue, though incomplete in the text, states: When God empowers a servant to destroy something, the power of preservation (al-ibqa') departs from God, meaning God loses the ability to preserve that thing, which is His act. This would imply God is not powerful over His own act, which is absurd. The passage thus demonstrates the necessity that God's power is by His essence and does not transfer or diminish.

Key Terms
قدرية (Qadariyya)
قدرة (power)
ذات (essence)
علم (knowledge)
أعراض (accidents)
أجسام (bodies)
إقدار (empowerment)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power can be transferred or shared with creatures, diminishing in the process.
Correction: God's power is essential and does not diminish; empowering creatures does not reduce His power over their actions.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument of the Qadariyya that Maturidi refutes?
A) God's power increases when humans act
B) God's power over an action departs when He empowers humans over it
C) God cannot empower humans over actions
D) God's knowledge decreases when humans learn

Key Takeaway
Essential attributes of God, such as power and knowledge, cannot diminish or be transferred to creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely on your own strength or ability in a difficult situation, do you ever feel that God's power over that situation has diminished? How does Maturidi's argument challenge that assumption, and what would it look like to truly trust that God's power never departs even as He gives you ability?
?
If God's essential attributes—like His power and knowledge—never diminish or transfer, then His knowledge of your every action is complete and never wanes. How does that affect the way you live your private moments, when no one else sees? Will you let that reality change even one habit today?
?
The Qadariyya thought that God empowering creatures meant He lost power over them. Is there an area of your life where you act as if your choices have 'taken over' from God's control? What would it mean to consciously surrender that domain back to Him, acknowledging that His power remains fully present even in your free choices?

Second Issue: Does Empowering a Servant Remove God's Power of Preservation?
Maturidi presents the second argument: empowering a servant to destroy removes God's power of preservation, which is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument against the Qadariyya: that if God empowers a servant to destroy something, it would imply that God loses the power to preserve that thing, which is absurd and contradicts the essential nature of divine power.

Core Idea
God's power over His own acts cannot be removed by empowering creatures — the second argument exposes this contradiction.

The second argument, though incomplete in the surviving text, continues Maturidi's refutation of the Qadariyya. It points out that if God gives a servant the ability to destroy a thing, then God would no longer have the power to preserve that thing, which is one of God's acts. This would mean God is not powerful over His own act, which is impossible. Let's work with the available text and understand the logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the incomplete Arabic and translation
Read the passage for the second issue. Note that the text cuts off, but the core idea is clear: when God empowers a servant to destroy, the power of preservation (al-ibqa') is said to depart from God.
The text is incomplete due to manuscript damage.
2
Understand the Qadariyya implication
The Qadariyya claim would imply that God's power over preservation (an act of God) is removed when He gives power to destroy. That would mean God is not powerful over His own act.
3
Recognize the absurdity
If God loses power over preservation, He is not fully powerful. But God's power is essential and all-encompassing, so this is impossible. Therefore the Qadariyya view is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومسألتان فى القدرة على القدرية يوجبان أن الله ليس بقادر بذاته ، أحدهما أنهم قالوا : يقدر الله جل ثناؤه على حركات العباد وسكونهم ، فلما أقدرهم على تلك الحركات والسكون زالت عنه القدرة عليها ، فيكون قادرا في التحقيق بغيره ؛ إذ هو بذاته على ما كان عليه . فلو كانت تلك القدرة له بذاته لم تكن تزول عنه إذا أقدر عليها غيره. ومما يبين ذلك أنه إذ كان عالما لذاته بكل شيء ، لم يذهب علمه لما أعلم غيره ، فمثله القدرة . مع ما كانت أدلة غيرية الأعراض للأجسام هي وجود الأجسام دونها ، ومثل ذلك علة غيرية القدرة والعلم في الشاهد إنهما غير الذي له ، فكذلك القول بالذي قالوا فى الله سبحانه ، ومما يزيد هذا وضوحا أنه لو أراد أن يحركه بحركة الاضطرار ويسكنه ذلك التسكين ومعه تلك القدرة لم يقدر عليه حتى يأخذ منه تلك القدرة ، فثبت أنه بها يقدر ، وهى تزول عنه وتعود إليه ، وهذا نعت الأجسام وحقيقة الأعراض ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . التي والثانى أن الله تعالى لما أقدر عبده على إتلاف شيء ذهبت عن الله قدرة الإبقاء الذي يريد ذلك ، والإبقاء فعله ، فصار عن فعله الذي هو / في الحقيقة ا في الحقيقة
And two issues concerning power against the Qadariyya which necessitate that God is not powerful by His essence. First: They said: God, exalted be His praise, has power over the movements of servants and their stillness. When He empowered them over those movements and stillness, power over them departed from Him, so He is in reality powerful by other than Himself, while He is by His essence as He was. If that power were His by essence, it would not depart from Him when He empowered others over it. What clarifies that is that since He is knowing by His essence of everything, His knowledge did not go away when He made others know. So similarly, power. Along with that, the evidence of the otherness of accidents for bodies is the existence of bodies without them, and similar to that is the otherness of power and knowledge in the witnessed world: they are other than that which has them. So likewise the saying of what they said concerning God, exalted. And what increases this in clarity is that if He wanted to move him with a movement of compulsion and make him still with that stilling while he had that power, He would not be able to do it until He took that power from him. Thus it is established that by it He has power, and it departs from Him and returns to Him. This is the description of bodies and the reality of accidents. There is no power except by God. The second: When God, exalted, empowered His servant over the destruction of something, the power of preservation (of what He wills) departed from God. Preservation is His act, so He becomes [deprived] of His act which is in reality...
Source pages p. 876

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct arguments (مسألتان) against the Qadariyya sect that conclude that God is not powerful by His essence if their claims were true. The first issue: The Qadariyya claim that God has power over human actions, but when He gives power to humans over those actions, His own power over them departs. This would imply that God's power is not essential but acquired, because if it were essential it could not depart. Maturidi refutes this by analogy: God's knowledge is essential and does not diminish when He teaches others; similarly, essential power should not diminish. Furthermore, in the physical world, accidents (a'rad) are distinct from bodies, and power and knowledge are observed to be separate from their possessors. If God's power could depart and return, that would be the attribute of bodies and accidents, which is incompatible with God. The second issue, though incomplete in the text, states: When God empowers a servant to destroy something, the power of preservation (al-ibqa') departs from God, meaning God loses the ability to preserve that thing, which is His act. This would imply God is not powerful over His own act, which is absurd. The passage thus demonstrates the necessity that God's power is by His essence and does not transfer or diminish.

Key Terms
قدرية (Qadariyya)
قدرة (power)
إقدار (empowerment)
إتلاف (destruction)
إبقاء (preservation)
فعل (act)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power can be diminished when He delegates authority over His creation.
Correction: God's power over His own acts (like preservation) is essential and cannot be removed by empowering creatures.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's second argument, what happens when God empowers a servant to destroy something?
A) God's power increases
B) God loses the power of preservation over that thing
C) The servant becomes equal to God in power
D) Nothing changes

Key Takeaway
God's power over His own acts is essential and cannot be removed by empowerment of creatures.

?

When you sin, do you unconsciously feel that you have 'taken away' something from God's sovereignty? How does the truth that God's power is untouched by your actions change your view of His mercy and your repentance?
?
If you truly believed that God's power is never diminished by your decisions, what would change in your daily reliance on Him? Name one specific situation where you will apply this lesson.

God's Essential Attributes Cannot Be Transferred
Maturidi argues against transferring God's essential attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that God's essential power and knowledge cannot be transferred to creatures without contradicting His divinity and sovereignty.

Core Idea
God's attributes are His essence—they cannot be given away.

In this passage, Maturidi responds to an objection that God's power and knowledge might be transferred to creatures. He explains that since God is powerful and knowing by His very essence, such a transfer would imply that God becomes subject to another's authority—a logical impossibility. Let's read his argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical flow: a premise, then an analogy, then a conclusion.
2
Identify the core premise
Maturidi asserts that God is powerful by His essence (qādir bi-dhātihi) and knowing by His essence (ʿālim bi-dhātihi). This is the foundation of his argument.
These terms indicate that power and knowledge are not accidental attributes but essential to God's being.
3
Understand the analogy
He contrasts created things like accidents and bodies, which are subject to God's power, with the idea that if God's power were transferred to another, He would be under that other's authority. This is impossible.
The analogy highlights the absurdity of the opponent's position.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi ends with a declaration of God's transcendence: 'Exalted is God above that, a great exaltation.' This is the logical conclusion of his argument.
The argument is not just philosophical but also affirms tanzih (divine transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالمعارضة له لازمة. وأيضا أن الله إذ هو قادر بذاته ، عالم بذاته ، فمحال وضفه بالذي ذكر ؛ إذ بذلك تمت ربوبيته وجل سلطانه وألوهيته ، وقد يكون الشيء الموصوف بذاته مما إذا ثبت لغيره عليه سلطان تجرى قدرته عليه كالأعراض التي من مختلفات لأنفسهن ، والأجسام / لهن ، أو الأجسام التى من قائمات بأنفسهن والأعراض بهن ، ثم لله عليها سلطان وملك ، فلو جعلنا على الله في إبطال تقديره ونقص تدبيره وإزالة علمه ونفى الحقيقة عن خبره لكان تحت قدرة غيره وفى سلطان آخر ، فتعالى الله عن ذلك علوا كبيرا . صبح ما الإطلاق يرجع إلى الأمر ، وقد بيناه .
The counter-argument is necessary for him. And also that God, since He is powerful by His essence, knowing by His essence, it is impossible to describe Him with what was mentioned; for by that His lordship is complete and His authority and divinity are majestic. And sometimes the thing described by its essence may be such that if another has authority over it, his power runs over it, like the accidents which are of varying kinds in themselves, and the bodies / for them, or the bodies that are self-subsistent and the accidents in them, and then God has authority and dominion over them. So if we were to impose on God the nullification of His decree, the deficiency of His management, the removal of His knowledge, and the denial of the truth of His report, He would be under the power of another and under another's authority. Exalted is God above that, a great exaltation. Subh: what is absolute refers to the matter, and we have explained it.
Source pages p. 876

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of an argument against an opponent. It begins by asserting that the opponent's objection is self-defeating. Then it presents a second point: since God is powerful and knowing by His essence (qādir bi-dhātihi, ʿālim bi-dhātihi), it is impossible to describe Him with the limitations mentioned earlier, because those limitations would contradict the perfection of His lordship, authority, and divinity. The author draws an analogy: created things like accidents and bodies are subject to God's power. If someone claimed that God's power could be transferred to a creature, that would imply that God is under that creature's power and authority—which is impossible. The passage ends with a declaration of God's transcendence and a brief remark about the term 'al-iṭlāq' (absoluteness) being already explained.
