First Issue Against Qadariyya: God's Power Is Not Essential
Maturidi presents the first argument that God's power cannot be essential if transferable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's first argument against the Qadariyya that God's power is not essential, and why it cannot be transferred or diminished.

Core Idea
God's power is essential and cannot be transferred or diminished.

Maturidi begins by presenting the first of two issues that disprove the Qadariyya claim that God's power is transferable. He uses logical arguments and analogies to show that God's power is essential and does not leave when He empowers others. This passage lays the foundation for understanding divine attributes as inherent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very concise and logical style.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage argues that God cannot be powerful by His essence if His power is transferable. Locate the statement that expresses this conclusion.
3
Follow the analogy with knowledge
Maturidi compares power to knowledge: God's knowledge does not decrease when He grants knowledge to others. Understand why this analogy strengthens the argument.
This is a key part of the logical reasoning.
4
Examine the hypothetical of compulsion
The passage ends with a hypothetical scenario showing that if a person still possessed God-given power, God could not compel him without first removing that power. This demonstrates that power is an accident.
This final step solidifies the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومسألتان فى القدرة على القدرية يوجبان أن الله ليس بقادر بذاته ، أحدهما أنهم قالوا : يقدر الله جل ثناؤه على حركات العباد وسكونهم ، فلما أقدرهم على تلك الحركات والسكون زالت عنه القدرة عليها ، فيكون قادرا في التحقيق بغيره ؛ إذ هو بذاته على ما كان عليه . فلو كانت تلك القدرة له بذاته لم تكن تزول عنه إذا أقدر عليها غيره. ومما يبين ذلك أنه إذ كان عالما لذاته بكل شيء ، لم يذهب علمه لما أعلم غيره ، فمثله القدرة . مع ما كانت أدلة غيرية الأعراض للأجسام هي وجود الأجسام دونها ، ومثل ذلك علة غيرية القدرة والعلم في الشاهد إنهما غير الذي له ، فكذلك القول بالذي قالوا فى الله سبحانه ، ومما يزيد هذا وضوحا أنه لو أراد أن يحركه بحركة الاضطرار ويسكنه ذلك التسكين ومعه تلك القدرة لم يقدر عليه حتى يأخذ منه تلك القدرة ، فثبت أنه بها يقدر ، وهى تزول عنه وتعود إليه ، وهذا نعت الأجسام وحقيقة الأعراض ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . التي
And two issues regarding power against the Qadariyya require that God is not powerful by His essence: One of them is that they said: God, exalted be His praise, has power over the movements and rests of servants. When He made them capable of those movements and rests, the power over them left Him, so He is in reality powerful by another; for He is by His essence as He was. For if that power were His by His essence, it would not leave Him when He empowered another to it. What clarifies that is that since He is knowing by His essence of everything, His knowledge does not depart when He makes another know. So similarly is power. Also, the proofs of the essential otherness of accidents to bodies are that bodies exist without them, and similarly the cause of the otherness of power and knowledge in the witnessed is that they are other than the one who has them. So likewise is the statement regarding what they said about God, exalted. And what increases this clarity is that if He wanted to move him with a compulsory movement and make him still with that stillness while he possesses that power, He would not be able to do so unless He takes that power from him. So it is established that he is powerful by it, and it leaves him and returns to him. And this is the description of bodies and the reality of accidents. And there is no power except by God. [التي]
Source pages p. 876

Commentary
This passage introduces two issues that force the Qadariyya to admit that God's power is not essential. The first issue argues: if God gives servants power over their own actions, then His power over those actions ceases. This shows that God's power is transferable, which would mean He is powerful through something other than His essence. Using an analogy with knowledge, the author points out that God's knowledge does not decrease when He grants knowledge to others; so similarly, His power should not diminish. He further argues using the relationship between accidents and bodies—accidents are ontologically distinct from bodies, and likewise power and knowledge are distinct from the one possessing them. Finally, a hypothetical example shows that if a person still possessed God-given power, God could not compel him without first removing that power, proving that power is an accident that can leave and return.

قادر بذاته
عالم بذاته
أعراض
أجسام
علة غيرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The assumption that God's power functions like human power—transferable and accidental.
Correction: Maturidi argues that God's power is essential and does not leave Him when He empowers others, unlike human power which is accidental and transferable.

Checkpoint

A) The analogy of bodies and accidents
B) The analogy of divine knowledge
C) The analogy of motion and rest
D) The analogy of creation and destruction

Key Takeaway
God's power is essential and does not diminish when He grants power to others, proving it is not an accident.

Second Issue Against Qadariyya: God's Act Prevented
Maturidi argues that empowering a servant to destroy prevents God's own act of preservation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that empowering a servant to destroy necessarily prevents God's own act of preservation, and why this leads to an unacceptable implication about divine power.

Core Idea
Empowering a creature to destroy limits God's own power to preserve.

This passage presents the second of Maturidi's arguments against the Qadariyya, who claimed that humans create their own actions. Maturidi argues that if God grants a servant the power to destroy something, then God's own power to preserve that thing is negated. This would mean God is prevented from performing His own act, which leads to absurd consequences. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the compact, argumentative style.
2
Identify the premise
The premise is that God empowers the servant to destroy something. What happens to God's power of preservation for that thing?
The text says 'the power of preservation... left God'.
3
Understand the consequence
If preservation power leaves, God becomes prevented from His own act of preservation. Grasp why this is problematic.
Preservation is God's act; to be prevented implies limitation.
4
Grasp the implication
If God can be prevented by another, then His power is not absolute; it becomes dependent. The author points out this absurdity.
The phrase 'من احتمل المنع لغيره يحتمل الإطلاق به' points to the implication.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes by exalting God above such dependence. Reflect on why this argument refutes the Qadariyya position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثانى أن الله تعالى لما أقدر عبده على إتلاف شيء ذهبت عن الله قدرة الإبقاء الذي يريد ذلك ، والإبقاء فعله ، فصار عن فعله الذي هو في الحقيقة فعله ممنوعا ، ومن احتمل المنع لغيره يحتمل الإطلاق به ، وفى الأول إعجاز وفى الثانى إقدار ، وجبا جميعا له بغيره ، جل الله عن ذلك .
And the second: that when God, exalted, empowered His servant to destroy something, the power of preservation that He wills for that thing left God, and preservation is His act. So He becomes prevented from His act which is in reality His act. And whoever accepts prevention for another accepts that it is absolute for Him? In the first is incapacitation and in the second is empowerment, and both are together by another for Him. Exalted is God above that.
Source pages p. 876

Commentary
This passage presents the second issue against the Qadariyya. It states that when God empowers a servant to destroy something, the divine power to preserve that thing ceases. Since preservation is an act of God, this means God is prevented from performing His own act. The author then comments that if one accepts that God can be prevented by another, then one must also accept that God is absolute (or dependent) in some way. The first case (empowerment for destruction) shows incapacity (iʿjāz), while the second (empowerment for preservation) shows enabling (iqdār). Both cases ultimately imply that God's power depends on another—a notion from which God is exalted.

Key Terms
إتلاف
قدرة الإبقاء
إعجاز
إقدار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The view that God's act of preservation can be negated by granting destructive power to a creature.
Correction: Maturidi argues that if God empowers a servant to destroy, then God's own preservation power leaves, implying prevention of His act. This cannot be true because it makes God's act dependent on another.

Checkpoint

A) It remains unchanged.
B) It increases.
C) It leaves God.
D) It becomes the servant's power.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that empowering a creature to destroy prevents God from preserving, which implies a limitation on divine power, from which God is exalted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If you truly believed that God's preservation is never hindered by any creature's empowerment, would you live with more courage or more caution? Give a specific example from your life where this conviction would make a practical difference.

The Two Aspects of al-Ka'bi's Error
Maturidi identifies two errors in al-Ka'bi's objection about power and time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi argues that God's power, being solely for action, can remain inactive for multiple times without contradiction, and how divine power differs from human power regarding contradictory opposites.

Core Idea
Power exists for action; if it can rest once, it can rest many times.

Imam Maturidi responds to al-Ka'bi's objection that if God's power can be inactive for a moment, it could be inactive forever. He identifies two key errors: first, that power is only for action, and second, a confusion about time and the possibility of action. He then distinguishes divine power from human power by noting that God is not subject to contradictory opposites.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Al-Ka'bi's objection is presented first, followed by Maturidi's two-aspect response.
Notice the structure: objection → two aspects of error → conclusion about opposites.
2
Identify the two aspects of error
Maturidi states that al-Ka'bi's error is from two aspects. Find these in the text: first, that power is only for action, so if it is free for one time, it can be free for many; second, regarding the time argument about the second time not possessing power.
The second aspect is more subtle: it involves the logical consistency of allowing action in a time when power is not present.
3
Understand the distinction between divine and human power
Maturidi explains that God's actions are not subject to contradictory opposites (tadādd), unlike human actions. This distinction justifies why God's power can be inactive for multiple times while a human's cannot.
This step connects the two aspects to a deeper theological principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال الكعبى : إن قال قائل : لو جاز أن يبقى القادر وقتا لا يفعل فيه لجاز كذلك أوقاتا كثيرة، كما يوصف بذلك الله تعالى.

قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله : وقد أخطأ في التقدير، وإنما السؤال فيه من وجهين: أحدهما أن القدرة إذ ليست إلا للفعل، وقد تخلوا عنه وقتا جاز أن تخلو عنه أوقاتا، وقد حققت هذا الوصف لله. والثاني أنه للوقت الثاني من وقت القدرة ليس بواجد لها، وجاز الفعل بها، فلم لا كان للوقت العاشر كذلك؟ وإن لم يجدها، أو إذ لم يجز الفعل بها بعد فنائها بأوقات وجب أن لا يجوز بوقت الأول؟ أن الله كذلك بما لا تتضاد عليه الأفعال ويقدر على ما لا ضد له، والعبد لا يقدر على ما لا ضد له؛ لذلك لم يجز فاعل. فيقال: وما ذكرت ما قوبلت به، بل قيل لك: ما منع أن يكون من يتضاد عليه لا يجوز وجوده، ولا فعل شيء أو ضده وقتا واحدا، ومن لا يتضاد عليه يجوز، ثم يقال: للتضاد لا يجيز وقت القدرة أو له يوجب الوقت الثاني، فأي الأمرين أجاب فهو في الجالين واحد. وما قال على الله فهو فاسد: لما ليس عنده فعل الله غير خلقه، وهو متضاد كالموت والحياة، وغير ذلك. ثم أجاب بأول أحوال الجسم أنه يخلو عن الحركة والسكون، فلم لم يجب به خلاؤه عنها أوقاتا؟

قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : فنقول وبالله التوفيق: الحركة والسكون هما اسمان، فمحال وجودهما في أول أحوال الجسم لإحالة البقاء؛ إذ السكون هو القرار حيث الوجود، والحركة الانتقال عنه، والقدرة ليست إلا للفعل، ولو جاز وجودها ولا فعل وقتا واحدا لجاز أوقاتا؛ إذ هي له، والجسم ليس للحركة ولا للسكون، وهما معنيان لا يقتضيان الحال. ألا يرى لأوقات البقاء لا يخلو عنها، القدرة لا تبقى، فيجب أن لا يخلو منه عند الوجود، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وبعد، فإن مسألتنا في الفعل، ونجيز من الجسم وقت وجوده إذا لم يكن الفعل الذي هو اسم للبقاء، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then al-Ka'bi said: If someone says: If it is permissible for the powerful one to remain for a time without acting in it, then it would be permissible likewise for many times, as God is described with that. The jurist Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: He erred in estimation. The question is from two aspects: One of them is that since power is only for action, and it was free from it for a time, it is permissible for it to be free from it for times, and I have verified this description for God. The second is that for the second time from the time of power it does not possess it, and action is permissible with it, so why not for the tenth time likewise? And if it does not find it, or if action is not permissible with it after its passing by times, then it is necessary that it not be permissible for the first time? That God is thus with what does not have contradictory opposites upon it and is powerful over what has no opposite, and the slave is not powerful over what has no opposite; therefore the agent is not permissible. So it is said: You did not mention what you were confronted with, rather it was said to you: What prevents that the one upon whom opposites occur is not permissible in his existence, and he cannot do a thing or its opposite at one time, while the one upon whom opposites do not occur is permissible? Then it is said: For opposites either it does not permit the time of power or it necessitates the second time. Whichever of the two he answers, he is one in both cases. And what he said about God is corrupt: because in his view the act of God is only His creation, and it has opposites like death and life and other than that. Then he answered with the first states of the body that it is empty of motion and rest, so why did it not necessitate that it be empty of them for times? Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: We say, and with God is success: Motion and rest are two names, and their existence in the first states of the body is impossible due to the impossibility of remaining; because rest is the settling where existence is, and motion is departure from it, and power is only for action. If its existence without action for one time were permissible, it would be permissible for times, since it is for it, and the body is not for motion nor for rest, and they are two meanings that do not necessitate the state. Do you not see that for the times of remaining it is not empty of them? Power does not endure, so it must not be empty of it at the time of existence, and no strength but with God. And after that, our question is about action, and we permit from the body at the time of its existence if the action which is a name for remaining is not present, and no strength but with God.
Source pages p. 877

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection raised by al-Ka'bi regarding God's power and inactivity. The argument proceeds in two main parts: first, Imam Maturidi identifies two aspects in which al-Ka'bi erred; second, he provides a detailed theological-physical argument about motion and rest. The first aspect is that power (qudra) exists solely for action (fi'l). If it is free from action for a time, it can legitimately be free for multiple times, as God is described. The second aspect concerns the relationship between power and time: if power does not find a second time, then its action cannot be performed after its passing, yet the opponent's logic would incorrectly apply that to the first time. Imam Maturidi emphasizes that God's actions are not subject to the same restrictions as human actions because God is not subject to contradictory opposites (tadādd). Human power is limited to what has opposites, whereas divine power is not. The opponent then tries to argue from the example of a body's first state being free of motion and rest, asking why that does not imply freedom for extended times. Imam Maturidi responds by defining motion and rest as names and concepts that cannot exist in the first state of a body due to the impossibility of remaining (baqā'). He explains that rest is settling where existence is, and motion is departure from it. Since power is only for action, if it could exist without any action for a single moment, it could do so for many. But the body is not inherently subject to motion or rest; they are meanings that do not necessitate a state. The lasting times of a body are never empty of them, but power does not endure, so it must act when it exists. Finally, he clarifies that the issue at hand is about action; for a body, we allow that at the moment of its existence it may lack the action that is the name for remaining (i.e., the act of being). Thus, the refutation denies the opponent's analogy.

Key Terms
القدرة
الفعل
الوقت
التضاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God's power is inactive for a moment, it could be inactive forever, making God a 'potential' being.
Correction: Imam Maturidi shows that power's freedom from action for a time is permissible for God precisely because His power is not limited by opposites as human power is.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Maturidi, what is the first aspect of al-Ka'bi's error?
A) Power can be absent for many times if it is absent for one time.
B) Power is the same for God and humans.
C) Power is only for action, and its freedom from action for a time allows freedom for many times.
D) God's power is not subject to time.

Key Takeaway
God's power, being solely for action, can be inactive for multiple times without contradiction, as God is not subject to the limitations of contradictory opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a period where God's help seems absent, do you doubt His power? How does this lesson's distinction between God and human power affect your patience?
?
Have you ever thought that God's inactivity in answering your du'a means His power is limited? Reflect on the fact that God's power is solely for action and not constrained by time. Will you commit to trusting Him even in silence?
?
How does knowing that God is not subject to contradictory opposites change the way you relate to His attributes of life and death? Are you living in awe of a God whose power transcends all opposites?

The Body's First State and the Motion-Rest Argument
Maturidi refutes the argument from a body's first state being free of motion and rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi's refutation of al-Ka'bi's analogy between God's power and a body's first state being free of motion and rest.

Core Idea
Motion and rest are impossible in a body's first state, so the analogy fails.

Al-Ka'bi attempts to strengthen his objection by pointing out that a body in its first state is empty of motion and rest, asking why that does not imply it could remain so for many times. Imam Maturidi responds by explaining that motion and rest are names that cannot exist in the first state due to the impossibility of remaining (baqā'), and that power necessitates action in its time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read al-Ka'bi's second argument and Maturidi's detailed response. Note the definitions of motion and rest.
This passage uses physical concepts to make a theological point.
2
Understand the impossibility of motion and rest in the first state
Maturidi states that motion and rest are two names, and their existence in the first state of a body is impossible due to the impossibility of remaining (baqā'). Rest is settling where existence is, and motion is departure from it. In the first moment of existence, both are impossible.
This is a key philosophical point about the nature of beginning.
3
Relate to the main argument about power
Maturidi reiterates that power is only for action. If it could exist without action for one moment, it could for many. But unlike a body's states, power does not endure, so it must act when it exists.
The body example does not apply to divine power.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Maturidi argue that motion and rest cannot exist in the first state of a body?
A) Because motion and rest are names, not real attributes.
B) Because the body is not capable of motion or rest until it is fully formed.
C) Because their existence requires a previous state of remaining, which is impossible in the first state.
D) Because power is only for action, not for motion or rest.

Key Takeaway
The analogy between a body's first state and divine power fails because motion and rest require a prior state of remaining, and power, which does not endure, must act when it exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that power is only for action—it cannot sit idle. Where in your life do you have God-given abilities (time, wealth, influence, knowledge) that you are letting sit idle, assuming they can wait? What is one specific action you will take today to use a dormant gift, and how will you hold yourself accountable before Allah?
?
Maturidi argues that the first state of a body cannot contain motion or rest because there is no prior 'where'. Reflect on your own 'first states'—the moments when you first become aware of a duty or an opportunity. Do you sometimes delay action, waiting to 'settle in' before you serve? What would it look like to act immediately upon conviction, trusting that power is tied to action?
?
The analogy between the created body and the Creator fails because of fundamental differences. Are there ways you unintentionally compare God to created things—expecting Him to act like a human, or measuring His patience by human standards? Commit to catching one such thought today and replacing it with the truth that His power and knowledge are unlike any created thing.

The Power Argument: Refuting the Objection
Maturidi refutes the initial objection about power and its use.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the objection that God's granting power for an action implies God is responsible for the action's outcome.

Core Idea
Power is a favor from God for obedience; misuse is not attributed to God.

Maturidi encounters an objection: if God knows how power will be used and grants it, does that not make Him responsible for misuse? In this passage, he explains that power in human context is not given for specific outcomes, and God's grant is a favor, not a commission. The objector's analogy fails because God is not bound by human categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector argues that when God knows how power will be used, He still grants it, similar to how humans grant strength for a known purpose. This would imply God approves the misuse.
Notice the phrase 'في الشاهد يوصف بالتقوية عليه'.
2
Identify Maturidi's response
Maturidi responds that the analogy is invalid because God is not described like humans ('والله لم يوصف بمثله'). The power is given as a favor ('امتنان'), not as a commission for a specific act.
The term 'امتنان' is key: power is a favor, not a mandate.
3
Understand the logical consequence
Since power admits only one use but the servant chooses, the use is not predetermined by the grant. The repulsion of the objection is not based on that argument.
Maturidi concludes that the objection is false because it assumes power dictates action.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The servant's act is by a power sought from God, not by God's own power. Thus, disobedience does not originate from God.
This sets up the next lesson on the specific disobedience objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال أن الله إذ علم أنه فيم يستعمل يستعمل ، وفى مثله فى الشاهد يوصف بالتقوية عليه ، والله لم يوصف بمثله ، فمثله في الخلق مع ما يقال بالأول ، لكنه طلب منه ، واختيار " ذلك فيه لا بها ، ولا يوضع في ذلك حرف الإعطاء ؛ لأنه امتنان ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . نوع . وما عارض فاسد ؛ لاحتماله أن لا يستعمل في الوجهين ، فلم يكن الدفع لوجه من ذلك ، والقوة لا تحتمل إلا أحدهما ، ولا يجوز أن يخلو عن وقوع أحدهما بها ، وقد علم بذلك ، فلا يحتمل القول بالدفع لغير ذلك . ثم قال : فإن قلت : العاصى إذ يفعل بقدرة الله لم لا قلت : إن المعصية من الله ؟ الله : وقد أخطأ قال الشيخ رحمه من وجهين : أحدهما أن خصومه لا يقولون في المعصية إنها من الله ، والثانى لا يقال فعل العبد بقدرة الله ، ولكن بقدرة طلبها .. من الله . ثم أجاب في ذلك بمثل جوابه فى الأول : إنه أعطى ليطيع وأتم هذا ، وقد بينا الوجه في الأول وخطأ [ه] في هذا السؤال.
The question: that God, when He knows that he will use [power] in something, uses it; and in the like of this in the witnessed [world] one is described as granting strength for it, but God is not described with its like. So its like in creation is with what is said in the first [case], but it is sought from Him, and choice in that is not by it, and the letter of giving is not placed in that; because it is a favor, and there is no power except by God. A type. And what opposes is corrupt because it is possible that he does not use it in either of the two ways, so the repulsion was not for a way of that, and power admits only one of them, and it is not permissible that it be empty of the occurrence of one of them by it, and that is known. So the statement of repulsion for other than that is not admissible. Then he said: If you say: The disobedient, when he acts by the power of God, why do you not say that disobedience is from God? ... And the Sheikh, may God have mercy on him, said: He erred in two respects: one is that his opponents do not say that disobedience is from God, and the second is that it is not said that the act of the servant is by the power of God, but by a power he sought from God. Then he answered in that with a similar answer to the first: He gave [power] to obey, and completed this, and we have explained the aspect in the first and his error in this question.
Source pages p. 879

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection by redefining the relation of power to human actions. The objector argues that if God knows how power will be used, and in human experience the one who grants strength is praised for the good use, then since God is not described with such human attributes, the power must be created for one specific use. The counter-objection about disobedience is refuted on two grounds: (1) opponents do not attribute disobedience to God, and (2) the servant's act is not by God's power directly but by a power he seeks from God. The power is given for obedience, and the servant's misuse does not revert to God.

Key Terms
قدرة
امتنان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if God grants power for an act, He necessarily approves or causes that act.
Correction: Maturidi explains that power is a favor given for obedience, not a mandate. The choice of use belongs to the servant.

Checkpoint
What is Maturidi's main reason for rejecting the analogy between God and humans in granting power?
A) Humans do not grant power.
B) God is not described with human attributes.
C) Power is not really granted by God.
D) The analogy is not mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
God's grant of power is a favor for obedience, not an endorsement of misuse; the servant's choice determines the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own abilities, talents, and opportunities—all gifts from God—do you truly see them as favors for obedience, or do you sometimes feel entitled to use them however you wish? What would change in your daily choices if you genuinely lived today knowing that each power you have is a trust from Him?
?
Is there a pattern in your life where you blame God—even silently—for giving you certain capacities that you've misused? How does this lesson's truth that 'power is a favor, not a commission' free you to take full responsibility for your choices rather than shifting blame?
?
What would it cost you to stop before every action today and ask: 'Am I using this power—my strength, time, speech, influence—as a favor from God for obedience, or as my own right?' Will you hold yourself accountable now?

The Disobedience Objection and Two Errors
Maturidi identifies two errors in the objection about disobedience and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the objection that the disobedient person's act being by God's power implies disobedience is from God, and the two errors in that objection.

Core Idea
The act is by a power sought from God, not by God's power itself.

The objector poses a direct challenge: if the disobedient acts by God's power, why not say disobedience is from God? Maturidi points out two errors in this question. First, his opponents do not even claim disobedience is from God. Second, the servant's act is not by God's power, but by a power he sought from God. The power is given specifically for obedience, and misuse does not revert to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the two errors
The objector says: 'If the disobedient acts by God's power, why not say disobedience is from God?' Maturidi says this contains two errors.
Note the phrase 'العاصى إذ يفعل بقدرة الله'.
2
Understand the first error
The opponents themselves do not attribute disobedience to God. So the question is based on a false premise.
Maturidi says: 'خصومه لا يقولون في المعصية إنها من الله'.
3
Understand the second error
The servant's act is not by God's power directly, but by a power he sought from God. So the premise 'by God's power' is incorrect.
Maturidi says: 'لا يقال فعل العبد بقدرة الله، ولكن بقدرة طلبها من الله'.
4
See the final refutation
Maturidi reiterates: God gave power for obedience. The act of disobedience is a misuse by the servant, not an act attributable to God.
This completes the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال أن الله إذ علم أنه فيم يستعمل يستعمل ، وفى مثله فى الشاهد يوصف بالتقوية عليه ، والله لم يوصف بمثله ، فمثله في الخلق مع ما يقال بالأول ، لكنه طلب منه ، واختيار " ذلك فيه لا بها ، ولا يوضع في ذلك حرف الإعطاء ؛ لأنه امتنان ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . نوع . وما عارض فاسد ؛ لاحتماله أن لا يستعمل في الوجهين ، فلم يكن الدفع لوجه من ذلك ، والقوة لا تحتمل إلا أحدهما ، ولا يجوز أن يخلو عن وقوع أحدهما بها ، وقد علم بذلك ، فلا يحتمل القول بالدفع لغير ذلك . ثم قال : فإن قلت : العاصى إذ يفعل بقدرة الله لم لا قلت : إن المعصية من الله ؟ الله : وقد أخطأ قال الشيخ رحمه من وجهين : أحدهما أن خصومه لا يقولون في المعصية إنها من الله ، والثانى لا يقال فعل العبد بقدرة الله ، ولكن بقدرة طلبها .. من الله . ثم أجاب في ذلك بمثل جوابه فى الأول : إنه أعطى ليطيع وأتم هذا ، وقد بينا الوجه في الأول وخطأ [ه] في هذا السؤال.
The question: that God, when He knows that he will use [power] in something, uses it; and in the like of this in the witnessed [world] one is described as granting strength for it, but God is not described with its like. So its like in creation is with what is said in the first [case], but it is sought from Him, and choice in that is not by it, and the letter of giving is not placed in that; because it is a favor, and there is no power except by God. A type. And what opposes is corrupt because it is possible that he does not use it in either of the two ways, so the repulsion was not for a way of that, and power admits only one of them, and it is not permissible that it be empty of the occurrence of one of them by it, and that is known. So the statement of repulsion for other than that is not admissible. Then he said: If you say: The disobedient, when he acts by the power of God, why do you not say that disobedience is from God? ... And the Sheikh, may God have mercy on him, said: He erred in two respects: one is that his opponents do not say that disobedience is from God, and the second is that it is not said that the act of the servant is by the power of God, but by a power he sought from God. Then he answered in that with a similar answer to the first: He gave [power] to obey, and completed this, and we have explained the aspect in the first and his error in this question.
Source pages p. 879

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection by redefining the relation of power to human actions. The objector argues that if God knows how power will be used, and in human experience the one who grants strength is praised for the good use, then since God is not described with such human attributes, the power must be created for one specific use. The counter-objection about disobedience is refuted on two grounds: (1) opponents do not attribute disobedience to God, and (2) the servant's act is not by God's power directly but by a power he seeks from God. The power is given for obedience, and the servant's misuse does not revert to God.

Key Terms
قدرة
معصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if the servant acts using God-given power, the act is directly from God.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that the act is by a power the servant sought from God, and the power is given for obedience, not for specific acts.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the second error in the objector's question?
A) The objector assumes the act is by God's power, but it is by a power sought from God.
B) The objector denies that God gives power.
C) The objector claims that disobedience is not from God.
D) The objector forgets to mention the power.

Key Takeaway
The servant's act is by a power he sought from God, not by God's own power, so disobedience is not attributable to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever feel that your weakness or circumstances made it inevitable? How does this lesson's distinction between God's power and the power you seek challenge that feeling of inevitability?
?
Think of a specific disobedience you committed. Did you, at that moment, recognize that you were using a power you had sought from God for the purpose of obedience? How does that awareness—or lack of it—affect your repentance?
?
If you truly believed that every act of obedience or disobedience is done by a power you personally sought from God, would you pray differently for strength before a temptation? What would you ask for?

Refuting the Objection of al-Ghani: Power and Persistence
Maturidi refutes an objection using al-Ghani and explains permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the objection that God's attribute al-Ghani makes Him independent of needing to create power.

Core Idea
Power is for action, not persistence; only God's preservation sustains existence.

In this passage, Maturidi tackles a subtle objection: if God is al-Ghani, the Self-Sufficient, how can He give power to creatures without becoming dependent? The objector uses analogies of sword and dirham. Maturidi's answer clarifies the nature of power and persistence, showing that the objection actually backfires.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the argumentative structure: the objector raises a concern, and Maturidi responds.
Pay attention to the phrase 'there is no power except by God' which frames the discussion.
2
Identify the objection
The objector says 'God forbid' because God is al-Ghani, and uses the analogy of a sword and dirham. Understand what this objection implies: that giving power would imply need.
3
Understand Maturidi's counter-argument
Maturidi explains that power does not admit persistence. Power is for initiating action, not for sustaining it. Necessity (need) arises from persistence, not from power.
This distinction is key: existence does not perpetuate benefit; persistence does.
4
See the conclusion
The objection backfires: the objector's own logic forces him to admit that God must preserve and sustain, which is consistent with His being al-Ghani.
The phrase 'there is no power except by God' is repeated to emphasize creaturely dependence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسه بالغنى ، فقال : معاذ الله ؛ لأنه المغنى . وعارض بالسيف والدرهم . قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وقد حاد عن ذلك ؛ إذ القوة لا تحتمل البقاء ، وبها أوجب الحاجة ، فإذا استحال ذلك لزم ما عورض به عن الغنى ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وما قابله به فالوجود لا يديم النفع ، بل يديمه البقاء ، وله الحاجة إلى الإبقاء ، وليس ذلك فى القوة ؛ فلذلك لزمك الذى تعوذت منه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then he objected to himself with al-Ghani (the Rich), saying: God forbid, because He is al-Ghani. And he objected with the sword and the dirham. The Sheikh, may God have mercy on him, said: He deviated from that, because power does not admit persistence, and by it necessity is occasioned; so when that is impossible, what was objected with concerning al-Ghani becomes necessary. And there is no power except by God. And what he countered with is that existence does not perpetuate benefit, rather persistence perpetuates it, and He has need for preservation, and that is not in power; therefore that from which you sought refuge becomes necessary for you. And there is no power except by God.
Source pages p. 879

Commentary
In this passage, Maturidi addresses a self-objection invoking God's attribute al-Ghani (the Self-Sufficient) and the analogy of sword and dirham. The objector claims that if power is created for one use, then God's giving power would make Him dependent, which is impossible for al-Ghani. Maturidi replies that this objection misses the point: power (qudra) does not entail permanence (baqa'). The existence of a thing does not guarantee its continued benefit; only persistence (baqa') does, and that requires preservation from God. Power is only for initiating action, not for sustaining existence. Therefore, the objection backfires: the critic is forced to admit that God must preserve, which is consistent with His being al-Ghani. The phrase 'there is no power except by God' closes each section.

Key Terms
غنى
بقاء
إبقاء
قوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's attribute al-Ghani prevents Him from being the source of power for creatures because giving power would imply need.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that power is for action, not for self-sufficiency. God's giving power does not imply need because need arises from persistence, not from the act of giving.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi argue that the objection using al-Ghani fails?
A) Because al-Ghani means God has no needs, but power does not imply need.
B) Because power does not ensure persistence; persistence requires preservation from God.
C) Because the sword and dirham analogy is faulty.
D) Because the objector misunderstands the meaning of al-Ghani.

Key Takeaway
Power does not persist; only God's preservation sustains existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you woke up this morning, did you assume your own power will carry you through the day? How might your prayers change if you truly believed that no power of yours lasts a moment without God's preservation?
?
You often ask God for things, but do you also thank Him for the very persistence of your existence right now? If not, what is one specific way you can acknowledge His preservation tonight?
?
If someone denies that God is actively preserving every atom of creation, have you ever been that person in practice—living as if your own efforts sustain you? What is one step you can take today to shift that belief into action?

Divine Command, Prohibition, and Permission
Maturidi argues that divine commands are not coercive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that divine command and prohibition are absolute in matters of good but conditional in evil, and that they do not imply coercion or incapacity.

Core Idea
God's commands for good are absolute; for evil, conditional, and none compel human action.

Maturidi tackles a fundamental question: Does God's commanding or prohibiting something mean He forces humans to act or that humans lack the ability to choose? In this passage, he establishes a nuanced rule: divine commands are absolute when it comes to good, but only conditional regarding evil. He then defends against the objection that such commands entail coercion, citing a Quranic verse and a practical example from daily prayer to illustrate that divine prohibition does not remove human capacity.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the basic rule
Read the first part of the passage that states command, prohibition, and permission are absolute in good and conditional in evil. Notice that this applies only to good and evil respectively.
Maturidi is distinguishing between moral categories.
2
Examine the objection and response
Identify the objection that divine prohibition implies coercion. Then see how Maturidi responds using the Quranic verse 'so let them their way' and the contrast between two supplications.
The dua example highlights that seeking strength is praised, but seeking removal of obligation is not.
3
Reflect on power and action
Consider the final point about power ceasing at the time of action. Maturidi argues that God does not remove permission at that moment, indicating that command remains valid even when human power is absent.
This reinforces that divine command does not depend on human ability in a coercive way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمنع أو الأمر به أو الإباحة ، وذلك كله فى الخير مطلق وفى الشر لا إلا مقيدا ، لم يعسر ولم يجبر ، وإذا كان كذلك فمعارضته بالذي ذكر فاسدة . وما إنه أجاب عنا بالمنع فحق قال الله تعالى في قوله : فَخَلُّوا سَبِيلَهُمْ بعد ذكر المنع وما يحمد من قول الناس : اللهم قونا على طاعتك ، ولا يحمد : اللهم خل بيننا وبين طاعتك ، ثبت أن لأحدهما حالا ليس للآخر. وكذلك هو يقول بالفعل عن وقت فناء القدرة، ولا قدرة معه، ولا يقول بارتفاع الإطلاق والتخلية وقت الفعل ليعلم بذلك بعده فيما قدر، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And prohibition, command, or permission: all that is in good absolutely, and in evil only conditionally. He did not make it difficult nor compel. And when it is so, then opposing it with what was mentioned is corrupt. And what he answered us with prohibition is true; Allah said in His verse: 'so let them their way' after mentioning prohibition. And what is praised from people's saying: 'O Allah, strengthen us for Your obedience', and not praised: 'O Allah, let (free) between us and Your obedience', it is established that one has a state that the other does not. And likewise He says about the act at the time of the expiration of power, and there is no power with it, and He does not say about the removal of release and letting at the time of the act so that one may know thereby afterwards what is decreed. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 883

Commentary
This passage defines the rule of divine command, prohibition, and permission in relation to good and evil. The author first states that these judgments are absolute (mutlaq) in matters of good, but in evil they are only conditional (muqayyad), meaning that God does not command evil absolutely. He then emphasizes that this does not make things difficult for humans nor does it involve coercion (jabr). After establishing this principle, Maturidi argues that any objection based on the idea that God's command or prohibition implies compulsion is invalid. He supports this by citing the Quranic verse 'so let them their way' (9:5) after mentioning prohibition, implying that prohibition does not compel. He then gives a practical example: people praise the prayer 'O Allah, strengthen us for Your obedience' but do not praise 'O Allah, let between us and Your obedience', showing that there is a difference between asking for strength to obey and asking for removal of obligation. Finally, he discusses the timing of power: at the moment of action, one's power ceases, yet God does not say that He removes permission at that time, indicating that the divine command remains valid even when human power is absent.

Key Terms
المنع
الأمر
الإباحة
الخير
الشر
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that divine command and prohibition imply that God compels actions or that humans have no capacity.
Correction: Maturidi shows that commands are absolute only for good and conditional for evil, and that prohibition does not remove human capacity, as evidenced by the Quranic verse and the supplication example.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how do divine command and prohibition relate to good and evil?
A) They are absolute in both good and evil.
B) They are absolute in good and conditional in evil.
C) They are conditional in good and absolute in evil.
D) They are neither absolute nor conditional.

Key Takeaway
Divine commands are absolute only for good, not evil, and they do not imply coercion or removal of human capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself struggling to obey a clear command of Allah, do you inwardly resent the command as too burdensome, or do you turn to Him asking for strength, like the praised supplication? Which response reveals what you truly believe about God's justice and your own capacity?
?
Have you ever prayed, even in your heart, for Allah to simply remove a obligation because it felt too hard, effectively asking for 'takhliya' rather than 'taqwiyah'? What does your prayer pattern tell you about whether you acknowledge your own responsibility?
?
If you truly internalized that Allah's commands are never coercive and that He never commands evil absolutely, how would it change the way you view your own sins and the sins of others? Would you be quicker to take ownership of your actions instead of blaming circumstances or even God?

The Dialectical Argument on Divine Power and Human Capacity
Maturidi presents a dialectical argument about divine power and human capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dialectical argument Maturidi uses to critique inconsistent views on God's granting of power for sin and obedience.

Core Idea
The allocation of divine power must be consistent for friends and enemies alike.

Maturidi engages a hypothetical opponent in a series of pointed questions. The goal is to expose a logical flaw in the opponent's stance on how God apportions power to perform acts of obedience or sin. This passage is a classic example of kalam dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The initial line contains an unclear phrase likely corrupted in transmission.
2
Identify the first question about prophets
The argument begins by asking whether God ever allowed a prophet to persist in sin or informed him of it. Whatever the answer, it sets a precedent for the rest of the argument.
This establishes a baseline for divine intervention in human actions.
3
Analyze the symmetry argument about friends and enemies
Maturidi then asks whether God favored any friend by preventing the power of enmity. The response is used to derive a symmetrical principle regarding the power for sin and obedience.
If the opponent says yes to the friend, the same logic applies to enemies.
4
Consider the final question on the basis of friendship/enmity
The argument concludes by asking whether divine friendship or enmity is based on acts one is able to perform. This forces the opponent into a position that is either consistent or unreasonable.
The final formula 'there is no power except with God' underscores the theological point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي ذلك ما أنكرها قوشم : إنه وهو يعارضك فيقول : هل راقب الله نبى من الأنبياء في ابقاء معصية عامها منه أو أخبرها عنه ؟ فمها أجاب فى شيء فمثله الأول . ثم يقال : هل تفضل الله على أحد من أوليائه يمنع قدرة عداوته ؟ فإن قال : نعم ، نقول : إن الله لم يعط أوليائه قدرة معاصيه، فدايه فى أعدائه أيضا أنه لم يعطهم قوة طاعته، و إن قال : لا ، زعم أنه أعطى أولياءه قرة عداوته . ومن : إنه لم يعط أعداءه قوة العداوة، فالآن صار إلى أن أعطى أولياءه قوتها وذلك عظيم . ثم يقال : هل أعطى الله وليا قوى على تلك الطاعة حين الطاعة ؟ فإن قال : لا ، فالوحشة فى طاعة لم يقو عليها ليست بدونها في اجتناب معصية لم يقو عليها، بل قوى على ترك المعصية . وعندهم لم يقو على الطاعة، وهذا أوحش . ثم يقال : هل والى الله ولى أو عاداه، بفعل قوى عليه ! فإن قال : أقر بالقوة مع الفعل، وإن قال : لا ، زعم أن العداوة والولاية بما لا يقوى عليه، وذلك بعيد، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And regarding that, what [؟Qusham?] denied: he [the opponent] and he contradicts you saying: Did God watch over any prophet from the prophets in preserving a sin from him generally, or inform him about it? Whatever he answers in something, the first is similar to it. Then it is said: Did God favor anyone from His friends by preventing the power of his enmity? If he says: Yes, we say: God did not give His friends the power of their sins, and similarly with His enemies, that He did not give them the power of His obedience. And if he says: No, he claims that He gave His friends the [؟coolness?] of His enmity. And from it: He did not give His enemies the power of enmity, so now it comes to that He gave His friends its power, and that is great. Then it is said: Did God give a friend power over that obedience at the time of obedience? If he says: No, then the unease in obedience he is not able for is not less than the unease in avoiding a sin he is not able for, rather he is able to abandon the sin. And according to them he is not able for obedience, and this is more uneasy. Then it is said: Did God befriend a friend or befriend an enemy by an act he is able for? If he says: He affirmed power with the act, and if he says: No, he claims that friendship and enmity are by what one is not able for, and that is far, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 884

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical argument (objection and response) concerning God's granting of power for sin and obedience to His friends and enemies. The structure is a series of questions and answers that the Maturidi author (or his opponent) uses to expose a contradiction. The opponent initially denies something (likely that God's power is justly apportioned). The argument begins by asking whether God ever allowed a prophet to persist in sin or informed him of it; whatever the answer, it sets a precedent. Then it asks whether God specifically favored any friend by preventing the power of enmity (i.e., making them unable to oppose Him). If the opponent says yes, then it follows that God also does not give enemies the power of obedience, implying symmetry. If the opponent says no, then he claims that God gives friends the 'coolness' (possibly meaning ability or satisfaction) of enmity, which is contradictory. The argument then shifts to whether God gives a friend the power to obey at the moment of obedience. If not, then being powerless in obedience is no less disturbing than being powerless to avoid sin, but in fact the friend has power to abandon sin, which is inconsistent. Finally, it asks whether God's friendship or enmity is based on an act that one is able to perform. If the opponent affirms power with the act, that is one position; if not, then he claims that divine favor or disfavor is based on what one cannot do, which is unreasonable. Each step aims to force the opponent into a position that is either internally inconsistent or theologically problematic. The passage concludes with the formula 'there is no power except with God.'

Key Terms
قدرة (power)
معصية (sin)
طاعة (obedience)
ولي (friend/ally)
عداوة (enmity)
قوة (strength/power)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the argument denies all human capacity or that Maturidi holds that God gives power for sin.
Correction: The argument does not deny human capacity outright but critiques a specific theological opponent's inconsistent view about divine allocation of power. Maturidi's point is about logical consistency, not about absolute predestination.

Checkpoint
What does the argument ask about God's treatment of prophets regarding sin?
A) Did God command them to sin?
B) Did God watch over them in preserving a sin or inform them about it?
C) Did God punish them for sins?
D) Did God prevent all sins from them?

Key Takeaway
The dialectic shows that any claim about God's allocation of power must be symmetrical between friends and enemies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a sin you feel powerless to avoid, do you secretly believe that God has not given you the strength to overcome it? How does the argument's insistence on symmetry between friends and enemies challenge that feeling, and what will you do the next time that feeling arises?
?
The dialectic ends with 'there is no power except with God.' Have you ever lived a day with that truth at the center of your mind? What would change if you started each difficult task by whispering these words?
?
If God gave you the power to obey in a past moment, why do you doubt that He will give it again in the next? What argument from this lesson can you hold onto when you feel weak, and how will you act on it right now?

The Logical Connection Between Divine Will and Creation of Acts
Maturidi establishes the conditional relationship between divine will and creation of acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical connection between affirming divine will and affirming that God creates human acts, and why denying one leads to problematic implications.

Core Idea
Divine will and creation of acts stand or fall together.

Maturidi opens by examining the relationship between two key theological questions: divine will and the creation of human acts. He argues that accepting that God creates acts necessarily implies a certain understanding of His will, while denying creation leads to contradictions. In this lesson, we explore his logical reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and its translation carefully. Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then...'.
2
Identify the two scenarios
Maturidi presents two possibilities: if creation of acts is established, then will is established as a creative attribute; if not, then will is nullified in its real meaning.
The real meaning of will in the witnessed realm is repulsion of compulsion and forgetfulness.
3
Consider alternative interpretations
Note that 'will' can be misunderstood as wish, command, pleasure, etc. Maturidi dismisses these for God.
These interpretations do not apply to God in all cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة الإرادة يمكن أن تلحق بمسألة خلق الأفعال من الوجه الذي لو ثبت خلقها، والله مختار مريد لما يكون منه، ثبت القول بالإرادة من الوجه الذي يوصف بالخلق، وإن لم تثبت تبطل من الوجه الذي أريد بالارادة في الأفعال دفع الغلبة والسهو؛ إذ ذلك معنى حقيقة الإرادة فى الشاهد، إلا أن يراد بالإرادة التمنى أو الأمر والدعوى أو الرضا ونحو ذلك مما يجوز أن لا يوصف الله ببعض ذلك في كل شيء وينقض ذلك فى شىء البتة، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ويمكن أن تفردا عن تلك بما أفردها أهل الكلام، وإن كان الحق الأول. على أن فى إيجاب القول بالإرادة في كل شيء إيجاب القول بخلق الأفعال. قال الله تعالى: "فَمَنْ يُرِدِ اللهُ أَنْ يَهْدِيَهُ يَشْرَحْ صَدْرَهُ لِلإِسْلَامِ" إلى قوله: "كَأَنَّمَا يَصْعَدُ فِي السَّمَاءِ"، أخبر يريد هداية قوم بأفعالهم بهدايته، وإضلال قوم يجعل قلوبهم ضيقة حرجة. وقال عز وجل: "مَنْ يَشَا اللهُ يُضللهُ، وَمَنْ يَشَأْ يَجْعَلْهُ عَلَى صِرَاطٍ مَسْتَقِيمٍ"، ففرق بين القوم بالمشيئتين، فدلت الآيات على أن الله شاء لكل فريق بما علم أن يكون منهم، ودل على أن المشيئة فى هاتين الآيتين ليست بأمر ولا رضا. وقال تعالى: "وَلَوْ شِئْنَا لَآتَيْنَا كُلَّ نَفْسٍ هُدَاهَا"، وقال: "وَلَوْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ لَجَعَلَكُمْ أُمَّةً وَاحِدَةً"، وقال: "فَلَوْ شَاءَ لَهَدَاكُمْ أَجْمَعِينَ"، ولا يحتمل أن يكون هذه المشيئة رضا أو أمرا لما قد كانا، ثبت أنه أراد به المشيئة التي يكون عندها فعل لا محالة، ولا يحتمل أن تكون قد كانت وهو يقول: لو كان ليكون كذا، وفى تحقيق الكون دون الموعود به كذب، جل الله عن ذلك. ولا يحتمل تأويل القسر لأوجه: أحدها أن الله قد علمهم كيفية الهدى، ومائية دينه، وما به وجود حقيقته، فلا يحتمل أن يريد بهذا ضد ذلك أن غير يتقدم الإعلام في إحتمال هذا الاسم ضد الذى هو اسمه فى الحقيقة عندهم بتعليم الله ذلك لهم إلا بالله. والثانى أن طريق معرفة وحدانية الله والايمان به وبرسله طريق الاجتهاد، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The question of the will can be attached to the question of the creation of acts from the aspect that if the creation of acts is established, and God is choosing and willing for what comes from Him, then the statement of the will is established from the aspect that He is described with creation; and if it is not established, it is nullified from the aspect that by the will in acts is meant the repulsion of compulsion and forgetfulness, for that is the meaning of the reality of will in the witnessed realm, unless by will is meant wish, or command and claim, or pleasure, and the like, of which it is permissible that God not be described with some of that in everything, and that is nullified absolutely, and there is no power except with God. And it is possible that they (the two questions) be separated from that by what the people of Kalam separated them, although the truth is the first. However, establishing the statement of will in everything necessitates establishing the statement of the creation of acts. God Almighty said: 'Whoever God wills to guide, He expands his breast to Islam' up to His saying: 'as if he were climbing up to the sky.' He informed that He wills the guidance of a people through their actions by His guidance, and the misguidance of a people He makes their hearts narrow and constrained. And He, exalted, said: 'Whoever God wills, He leads astray, and whoever He wills, He places him on a straight path.' So He differentiated between the people by the two willings, and the verses indicated that God willed for each group according to what He knew would be from them, and indicated that the will in these two verses is not command nor pleasure. And He, exalted, said: 'And if We had willed, We would have given every soul its guidance.' And He said: 'And if God had willed, He would have made you one nation.' And He said: 'So if He had willed, He would have guided you all.' And it is not possible that this will is pleasure or command since they (pleasure and command) have occurred (in other contexts). It is established that He meant by it the will at which the act necessarily occurs. And it is not possible that it had been (in the past) and He says: 'If it were, it would be such,' because affirming the existence without the promise is false; exalted is God above that. And the interpretation of compulsion is not possible for two reasons: first, that God has taught them the manner of guidance, the essence of His religion, and that by which the existence of its reality occurs, so it is not possible that He intends by this the opposite of that, because the (act of) informing precedes the possibility of this name (i.e., 'compulsion') being the opposite of what is its name in reality according to them, except through God's teaching of that to them. Second, that the path to knowing God's oneness and faith in Him and His messengers is the path of effort (ijtihad). And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 885

Commentary
This passage is a theological discussion on the nature of divine will (al-irada) and its relationship to the creation of human acts. Maturidi begins by stating that the question of the will can be linked to the question of the creation of acts. He argues that if the creation of acts is affirmed, then God's will must also be affirmed in the sense that He is described as creating. Conversely, if the creation of acts is denied, then the will is nullified in the sense that it would no longer serve to repel compulsion and forgetfulness, which are the real meanings of will in the observable world (al-shahid). However, Maturidi notes that the term 'will' might be used for wish, command, claim, or pleasure, but God cannot be described with some of these in all cases; such interpretations are invalid. He then presents a second view: the two issues (will and creation) can be separated as the mutakallimun have done, but the first view (that affirming the will necessitates affirming creation) is the truth. 

Maturidi then provides Quranic evidence to support his position. He cites verse 6:125 about God expanding breasts for Islam or constricting them, explaining that God wills guidance or misguidance through human actions. Another verse (6:39) shows God differentiating between people by two willings, indicating that God's will is distinct from command or pleasure. Verses like 32:13, 5:48, and 6:149 are used to show that God's will is effective and would have guided all if He had so willed. Maturidi argues that interpreting these verses as pleasure or command is impossible because those would have required prior conditions. He also refutes the interpretation of compulsion (al-qasr) with two reasons: first, God has taught humanity the true nature of guidance and religion, making it impossible for Him to intend the opposite; second, the path to faith is through ijtihad (effort), not compulsion.

Key Terms
إرادة (irada)
خلق الأفعال (khalq al-af'al)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine will is merely a command or wish, and not necessarily connected to creation.
Correction: Maturidi argues that if creation of acts is affirmed, then will must be understood as a creative attribute; otherwise, the real meaning (repulsion of compulsion) is lost.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, if the creation of acts is not established, what happens to the concept of divine will in the context of acts?
A) It becomes a mere command.
B) It is nullified because it no longer serves to repel compulsion and forgetfulness.
C) It remains unchanged.
D) It becomes wish.

Key Takeaway
The affirmation of divine will and the creation of acts are logically interdependent in Maturidi's argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you raise your hands in dua, do you truly believe that God's will is creative and effective, or is it just a wish? How does that belief (or lack of it) affect your certainty that He will respond?
?
If you deny that God creates your actions, what does His will become in your mind? Is it reduced to a passive approval or a mere wish? Confront this: does your concept of God's will actually empower you to trust Him with your choices?
?
Take a moment: are you living as though your actions are created by God, or do you act independently? How would your reliance on God change if you fully internalized that His will and your acts are inseparably linked?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Quranic Evidence for Effective Divine Will
Maturidi uses Quranic proofs to affirm effective divine will and refute misinterpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Quranic verses demonstrate that divine will is effective and distinct from command or pleasure, and why the interpretation of compulsion is invalid.

Core Idea
Quranic will is effective; it is not command, pleasure, or compulsion.

After establishing the logical link between will and creation, Maturidi turns to Quranic evidence to show that God's will is effective and necessarily brings about actions. He refutes those who interpret divine will as mere command or pleasure, and also rejects the idea of compulsion. This lesson examines his scriptural arguments and reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verses
Read the verses cited by Maturidi and their interpretation. Note how each verse distinguishes the will of God from command or pleasure.
2
Maturidi's conclusion from the verses
He concludes that these verses show an effective will that would necessarily produce the act if exercised.
Especially the 'if...would' constructions.
3
Refutation of compulsion
Maturidi gives two reasons against interpreting these verses as compulsion: God's prior teaching and the path of ijtihad.
Both reasons protect human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة الإرادة يمكن أن تلحق بمسألة خلق الأفعال من الوجه الذي لو ثبت خلقها، والله مختار مريد لما يكون منه، ثبت القول بالإرادة من الوجه الذي يوصف بالخلق، وإن لم تثبت تبطل من الوجه الذي أريد بالارادة في الأفعال دفع الغلبة والسهو؛ إذ ذلك معنى حقيقة الإرادة فى الشاهد، إلا أن يراد بالإرادة التمنى أو الأمر والدعوى أو الرضا ونحو ذلك مما يجوز أن لا يوصف الله ببعض ذلك في كل شيء وينقض ذلك فى شىء البتة، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ويمكن أن تفردا عن تلك بما أفردها أهل الكلام، وإن كان الحق الأول. على أن فى إيجاب القول بالإرادة في كل شيء إيجاب القول بخلق الأفعال. قال الله تعالى: "فَمَنْ يُرِدِ اللهُ أَنْ يَهْدِيَهُ يَشْرَحْ صَدْرَهُ لِلإِسْلَامِ" إلى قوله: "كَأَنَّمَا يَصْعَدُ فِي السَّمَاءِ"، أخبر يريد هداية قوم بأفعالهم بهدايته، وإضلال قوم يجعل قلوبهم ضيقة حرجة. وقال عز وجل: "مَنْ يَشَا اللهُ يُضللهُ، وَمَنْ يَشَأْ يَجْعَلْهُ عَلَى صِرَاطٍ مَسْتَقِيمٍ"، ففرق بين القوم بالمشيئتين، فدلت الآيات على أن الله شاء لكل فريق بما علم أن يكون منهم، ودل على أن المشيئة فى هاتين الآيتين ليست بأمر ولا رضا. وقال تعالى: "وَلَوْ شِئْنَا لَآتَيْنَا كُلَّ نَفْسٍ هُدَاهَا"، وقال: "وَلَوْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ لَجَعَلَكُمْ أُمَّةً وَاحِدَةً"، وقال: "فَلَوْ شَاءَ لَهَدَاكُمْ أَجْمَعِينَ"، ولا يحتمل أن يكون هذه المشيئة رضا أو أمرا لما قد كانا، ثبت أنه أراد به المشيئة التي يكون عندها فعل لا محالة، ولا يحتمل أن تكون قد كانت وهو يقول: لو كان ليكون كذا، وفى تحقيق الكون دون الموعود به كذب، جل الله عن ذلك. ولا يحتمل تأويل القسر لأوجه: أحدها أن الله قد علمهم كيفية الهدى، ومائية دينه، وما به وجود حقيقته، فلا يحتمل أن يريد بهذا ضد ذلك أن غير يتقدم الإعلام في إحتمال هذا الاسم ضد الذى هو اسمه فى الحقيقة عندهم بتعليم الله ذلك لهم إلا بالله. والثانى أن طريق معرفة وحدانية الله والايمان به وبرسله طريق الاجتهاد، ولا قوة إلا بالله.

Key Terms
مشيئة (mashi'a)
التأويل بالقسر (ta'wil bil-qasr)
الاجتهاد (ijtihad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine will is a form of compulsion that forces people to act without responsibility.
Correction: Maturidi argues that God's will is effective but not compulsion; the path to faith is ijtihad (effort), and God's prior teaching precludes compulsion.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi reject the interpretation that the Quranic verses about will refer to pleasure or command?
A) Because pleasure and command have already occurred in other contexts.
B) Because they are not mentioned in the verses.
C) Because they would imply that God changes His mind.
D) Because they contradict human free will.

Key Takeaway
The Quran establishes that divine will is effective and necessarily brings about the act, distinct from command, pleasure, or compulsion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you hide behind the thought 'it was God's will' to avoid responsibility? How does this lesson's insistence on ijtihad and God's teaching of guidance confront your own excuses? Commit to one specific action today that requires your effort without blaming God's will.
?
The passage says God's will necessarily brings about the act. If you truly believed that, would you pray more earnestly for guidance, knowing that His will is effective? Examine your du'a: do you expect real change, or are you just going through motions? Resolve to call upon God with full conviction that His will transforms.
?
God taught the manner of guidance and the essence of religion. Have you taken the time to learn that teaching, or are you content with ignorance? This lesson says the path to faith is ijtihad—effort. What is one step you will take this week to learn more about your faith, relying on your own effort while trusting in God's will?

Divine Attributes
beginner
5 min
The Attribute 'Fa'al lima Yurid' and Refutation of Mu'tazili Claim
Maturidi refutes Mu'tazili by arguing from God's attribute 'Fa'al lima Yurid.'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the attribute 'Fa'al lima Yurid' to refute the Mu'tazili claim that God wills good but does not accomplish it.

Core Idea
God always does what He wills; Mu'tazili contradictions undermine trust in His promises.

Imam Maturidi presents a powerful argument against the Mu'tazila by focusing on God's attribute 'Doer of what He wills.' The Mu'tazila assert that God wills countless good deeds from creation but many never occur, implying that God fails to carry out His will. Maturidi exposes the logical and theological problems in this view, showing how it contradicts divine perfection and undermines trust in God's promises and threats.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the rhetorical questions.
The passage uses strong rhetorical style to challenge the Mu'tazili position.
2
Understand the Mu'tazili claim
Identify what the Mu'tazila believe about God's will: they claim He wills many good deeds from creation but they are not actualized. Why would God praise Himself for doing what He wills if He fails?
3
See how Maturidi uses the verse
Notice that Maturidi begins with the Quranic verse 'فَعَالٌ لِمَا يُرِيدُ' and argues that God praises Himself for this attribute. The Mu'tazili view contradicts this praise.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider the rhetorical questions: If God does not fulfill His will, how can we trust His promises or fear His threats? This undermines the very foundation of religious trust.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الله عز وجل: "فَعَالٌ لِمَا يُرِيدُ"، امتدح جل ثناؤه بالفعل لما يريد، وعند المعتزلة ما يريد من الخيرات التي تكون من الخلق مما لو اجتمع الخلق على إحصائها لم يبلغوا جزءاً من ألف جزء مما أراد فلم يفعل وهو عز وجل تمدح به: ثم من عظيم قولهم إن عند الله مشيئة جبراً لو كانت لكان الخلق على ما قال، فمن يصدقهم على هذا الدعوى أن له هذه القدرة أو المشيئة التي تعمل هذا العمل أن ظهر للخلق كل هذا الخلف في وعده وهذا العجز في فعله، أو متى يؤمن بوعد من وعد الخلق أن يفعل ما يريد أكثر مما يبلغه حساب الخلق ثم يخلف، ومن يثق بعد هذا بوعده: أو متى يخاف وعيده: وهذا محله عندهم. فإن أراد عندهم بعد أن يظهر عجزه وخلفه وما لا يليق بوصف الحكمة فأي شيء كان يبدي لتعلم به هذا على مذهب الاعتزال، جل ربنا وتعالى عن ذلك.
And Allah, mighty and majestic, said: 'Doer of what He wills.' He praised Himself, exalted be His praise, with action for what He wills. And according to the Mu'tazila, what He wills of the good deeds that come from creation, if creation were to gather to count them, they would not reach a part of a thousand parts of what He willed, yet He did not do it, and He, mighty and majestic, praised Himself with that. Then from the greatness of their statement is that with Allah is a will of coercion; if it were, the creation would be as He said. So who can believe them in this claim that He has this power or will that does this action, while all this breach of promise in His promise and this incapability in His action has appeared to creation? Or when will one trust a promise of one who promises creation that He will do what He wills more than what the reckoning of creation reaches, then He breaks? And who will trust after this in His promise? Or when will one fear His threat? And this is their position. If He intended according to them after He showed His incapability and breach of promise and what does not befit the description of wisdom, then what thing would He show to make known by it? This is according to the Mu'tazilite school. Exalted is our Lord and high above that.
Source pages p. 888

Commentary
This passage is an argument from the divine attribute 'فعال لما يريد' (Doer of what He wills) to refute the Mu'tazili claim that God wills many good things but does not accomplish them. Maturidi states that God praises Himself for doing what He wills. He points out that the Mu'tazila claim God wills countless good deeds from creation but they never occur, yet He praises Himself for that. He then exposes a contradiction: if God had a coercive will, all creation would be as He said, yet the Mu'tazila see much breach of promise and incapability. He questions how anyone can trust God's promises or fear His threats if He fails to do what He wills. He concludes that the Mu'tazili view attributes to God what is unworthy of wisdom, and exalts God above that.

Key Terms
فعال لما يريد
مشيئة جبر
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God wills good deeds but does not accomplish them, implying a limitation in His will or power.
Correction: God's attribute 'Fa'al lima Yurid' means He always does what He wills; He cannot will something and fail to do it, as that would contradict His perfection and make His promises unreliable.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi use the verse 'فعال لما يريد' to refute the Mu'tazila?
A) It shows God only wills good
B) It proves God always accomplishes what He wills, so He cannot have unfulfilled intentions
C) It indicates God's will is limited to what is possible
D) It means God does not will evil

Key Takeaway
God's attribute 'Fa'al lima Yurid' ensures He always fulfills His will; the Mu'tazili claim otherwise contradicts His perfection and undermines trust in His promises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where it seems God's promise is delayed or not fulfilled, do you find yourself doubting His ability to do what He wills? How does this lesson challenge you to examine the source of that doubt? Commit to reminding yourself of His attribute 'Fa'al lima Yurid' daily this week.
?
Think of a time you intended to do a good deed but didn't follow through. Whose will did you think was the deciding factor—yours or God's? Based on this lesson, how should you view your own failures in light of God's absolute sovereignty? Resolve to lower yourself in humility and ask for His help, knowing He is the one who gives success.
?
The Mu'tazili claim leads to questioning trust in God's promise and threat. Where in your own heart have you placed conditions on God's trustworthiness based on your expectations? Take one specific promise of Allah (e.g., Paradise for the believers) and ask yourself if you live in certainty of its fulfillment. If not, what step will you take today to strengthen that certainty?
