God's Will is Active and Unrestricted
Maturidi refutes Mu'tazilite restriction of divine will by exposing contradictions in their position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that God's will is limited to good and that God does not actually bring about what He wills.

Core Idea
God's will is not limited; He is the Doer of what He wills.

Maturidi engages the Mu'tazilite position that God wills only good and does not will evil, yet fails to bring about much of what He wills. He points out the logical and theological contradictions in this view, especially regarding God's promise and threat. This passage shows that God's attribute of will is tied to actual creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Note the rhetorical style and the Quranic quotation.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claims
The passage refutes two key Mu'tazilite claims: that God does not will evil, and that He wills good but does not bring it about. Find where these are stated or implied.
3
Follow Maturidi's argument
Trace the argument: from the Quranic verse affirming God as 'Doer of what He wills', to the inconsistency of claiming He wills but does not act, to the implications for trust in His promise and threat.
Notice how Maturidi uses hypothetical questions to expose absurdity.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes by exalting God above the Mu'tazilite description. Reflect on why this matters for tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنه أمر أن يقول كذا ولم يكن كذا ، وإن كان شرا لا يشاء [ه] ، فذكره عندهم لا معنى له ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وقال الله عز وجل : فَعَالُ لِمَا يُرِيدُ ، امتدح جل ثناؤه بالفعل لما يريد، وعند المعتزلة ما يريد من الخيرات التي تكون من الخلق مما لو اجتمع الخلق على إحصائها لم يبلغوا جزءا من ألف جزء مما أراد فلم يفعل وهو عز وجل تمدح به : ثم من عظيم قولهم إن عند الله مشيئة جبراً لو كانت لكان الخلق على ما قال ، فمن يصدقهم على هذا الدعوى أن له هذه القدرة أو المشيئة التي تعمل هذا العمل أن ظهر للخلق كل هذا الخلف فى وعده وهذا العجز في فعله ، أو متى يؤمن بوعد من وعد الخلق أن يفعل ما يريد أكثر مما يبلغه حساب الخلق ثم يخلف ، ومن يثق بعد هذا بوعده : أو متى يخاف وعيده : وهذا محله عندهم . فإن أراد عندهم بعد أن يظهر عجزه وخلفه وما لا يليق بوصف الحكمة فأى شيء كان يبدى لتعلم به هذا على مذهب الاعتزال، جل ربنا وتعالى عن ذلك
Because he commanded to say such-and-such but it was not so, and if it is evil He does not will it, so mentioning it with them has no meaning, and there is no power except through Allah. And Allah, Mighty and Majestic, said: 'Doer of what He wills' — He praised Himself, may His praise be exalted, with doing what He wills. And with the Mu'tazilites, He wills from the good acts that occur from creation such that if creation gathered to count them they would not reach one part of a thousand parts of what He willed, but He did not do it — while He, Mighty and Majestic, praised Himself with it. Then among the enormity of their statement is that Allah has a will of compulsion; if it existed, creation would be as they said. So who believes them in this claim that He has this power or will that does this work — that there appears to creation all this failure (khulf) in His promise and this incapacity in His act? Or when will one trust a promise of one who promises creation that He will do what He wills more than what creation's reckoning reaches, then breaks it? And who trusts after this in His promise? Or when will one fear His threat? And this is their position concerning Him. Then if He intended, according to them, after manifesting His incapacity, failure, and what does not befit the description of wisdom — so what thing was He to show to be known by this? This is according to the Mu'tazilite school. Exalted is our Lord and far above that!
Source pages p. 888

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position on divine will. Maturidi begins by pointing out the inconsistency in the Mu'tazilite claim that God does not will evil: if God commands a thing but it does not happen, and if evil is not willed by God, then the command has no meaning. Then he quotes the Quranic verse 'Doer of what He wills' (فَعَالٌ لِمَا يُرِيدُ), arguing that God praises Himself with actually doing what He wills. The Mu'tazilites, however, claim that God wills only good deeds from creation, but in reality He does not bring them about—so His praise is empty. Maturidi then exposes a further contradiction: the Mu'tazilites posit a will of compulsion (mashī'a jabran) in God, but if that were the case, creation would be forced. He then asks rhetorically: who can believe that God has such power or will that results in manifest failure to fulfill His promise and incapacity in His action? If God promises to do more than creation can count and then breaks His promise, who would trust His promise or fear His threat? He concludes that the Mu'tazilite position reduces God to a being who manifests incapacity and failure, which is unbefitting of wisdom. Maturidi ends by exalting God above such descriptions.

Key Terms
العتزلة
فعال لما يريد
المشيئة
الخلف
الوعد
الوعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's will is limited to good only, and that He does not will evil.
Correction: This passage shows that God's will encompasses all that occurs; He is the Doer of what He wills, and any limitation contradicts His praise and undermines trust in His promises.

Checkpoint
What is the main contradiction Maturidi points out in the Mu'tazilite position regarding divine will?
A) They claim God wills good but does not do it.
B) They claim God wills evil.
C) They deny God's power.
D) They affirm God's will is like human will.

Key Takeaway
God is actively doer of what He wills; any limitation on His will contradicts His praise and undermines trust in His promises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems evil or unjust, do you instinctively doubt God's will or assume He is not involved? How does this passage challenge you to see His hand in all that happens? What concrete step can you take this week to trust that He is the 'Doer of what He wills' even when you don't understand?
?
Have you ever found yourself limiting God's will to only the good things that happen, while attributing evil to other causes? How might this subtle belief affect your worship and reliance on Him? What will you do today to consciously acknowledge His sovereignty over every event, good or bad?
?
If you truly believed that God never breaks His promise and always does what He wills, would you live in greater hope and fear of Him? Identify one promise from the Quran that you find hard to trust, and resolve to act on it this week.

God's Will Precedes Human Sin: Proof from 17:16
Maturidi uses verse 17:16 to prove God wills the disobedience before it occurs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses Quran 17:16 to argue that God's will for a people's destruction precedes their disobedience, establishing that divine will encompasses all human actions.

Core Idea
God's will is not reactive; it precedes and determines all events, including sin.

Maturidi now turns to a direct Quranic proof that God's will is not contingent on human choices. In Surah Al-Isra, verse 16, God declares that when He intends to destroy a town, He commands its affluent ones, and then they transgress. Maturidi highlights that the intention to destroy comes before the transgression, establishing that God's will encompasses even the sin that leads to punishment. This passage is crucial for understanding the Maturidi position on divine predestination (qadar) and justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and Maturidi's interpretation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice how Maturidi begins by quoting the verse and then immediately draws the theological point: God informs that He intends destruction before the transgression occurs.
The verse is from Surah Al-Isra (17:16).
2
Follow Maturidi's logical argument
Maturidi presents a conditional argument: if God had not willed the transgression but instead willed obedience and still destroyed them, that would be unjust. Since God is just, He must have willed what actually occurred from them.
The Arabic phrase 'فلو لم كما علم أن يكون' is difficult; it likely means 'if He did not will as He knew would be'.
3
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that it is established that God willed what was from them, or at least knew it. This affirms that God's will covers even sinful actions without making Him unjust.
This refutes the idea that God only reacts to human sin.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this understanding affects the concept of human responsibility and divine justice. How does Maturidi reconcile God's prior will with human free will?
This verse is often debated in discussions on predestination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال تعالى : وَإِذَ أَرَدْنَا أَن نُهْلِكَ فَرْيَةً أَمَرْنَا مُتْرَفِيهَا فَفَسَقُوا فِيهَا ، أخبر أنه يريد إهلاك قوم وقرية بفسق أهلها قبل أن يكون منهم الفسق ، فلو لم كما علم أن يكون ، ولكن أراد أن تكون الطاعة ويهلكهم ، كان يكون ذلك جوراً ، فثبت أنه أراد الذي كان منهم أو علم ذلك .
And He, exalted, said: 'And when We intend to destroy a town, We command its affluent ones, then they commit transgression in it.' He informed that He intends the destruction of a people and a town through the transgression of its inhabitants before transgression occurs from them. For if He did not, as He knew would be, but He willed that obedience occur and He would destroy them, that would be injustice. So it is established that He willed what was from them, or knew that.
Source pages p. 888

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the Quran that God's will encompasses all events, including the sin that leads to punishment. Maturidi cites Surah Al-Isra 17:16, where God states that when He intends to destroy a town, He commands its wealthy (who then disobey). The key point is that God's intention to destroy comes before the actual transgression. Maturidi then argues: if God did not will the transgression but instead willed obedience and still destroyed them, that would be unjust. Therefore, it is established that God willed what occurred from them—or at least knew it. The Arabic phrase 'فلو لم كما علم أن يكون' is problematic but the argument is clear: God's will is not contingent on human choice; rather, it precedes and encompasses human actions.

Key Terms
إرادة
إهلاك
فسق
جور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's interpretation of verse 17:16, when does God intend to destroy a town?
A) After the people commit transgression.
B) Before the transgression occurs.
C) Only if they disbelieve.
D) It is a conditional threat.

Key Takeaway
God's will precedes and encompasses all human actions, including sin, without any injustice on His part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will precedes every sin you have committed, then your sins are not accidental; they are part of His decree. What sin have you been excusing as 'just a mistake' that you now must take full responsibility for because it was willed by God? How will you respond to that sin today?
?
This lesson shows that God's will is not reactive—your repentance does not surprise Him. If He knew and willed your sin before you committed it, then your repentance is not a change in His plan; it was always part of His will. Are you truly repentant, or are you just hoping to change God's mind? Examine your heart: does your repentance reflect a genuine turning back to the One who willed both your sin and your return?
?
God's intention to destroy the town came before the transgression of its affluent. Reflect on your own wealth and status: Are you using them in a way that invites God's intention to destroy, or His intention to save? What specific change in your spending or priorities is necessary to align with God's will?

Noah's Statement: God's Will for Misguidance
Maturidi cites Noah's verse to refute Mu'tazilite denial of divine will for misguidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Noah's statement in the Quran demonstrates that God's will includes leading people astray, countering the Mu'tazilite position.

Core Idea
Noah's words show that God's will can encompass misguidance.

The Mu'tazilites claimed that God only wills good and never misguidance. But the prophets spoke plainly. In this lesson, we examine Noah's direct address to his people, where he explicitly connects the effectiveness of his advice to God's will for their misguidance. Maturidi uses this verse to uphold the literal meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read Noah's Quranic quote
Read the verse as Maturidi presents it: Noah tells his people that if God intends to misguide them, his advice will not benefit them.
The verse is from Surah Hud (11:34).
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite objection
Maturidi explains that the Mu'tazilites deny God's intention for misguidance and reinterpret Noah's words beyond what they can bear.
This reinterpretation is a key point of contention.
3
See Maturidi's rebuttal
Maturidi insists on the literal meaning: the verse attributes the intention of misguidance to God, and any reinterpretation is unwarranted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال نوح لقومه : وَلَا يَنْفَهُ كُمُُ نُصْحِي إِن أَرَدْتُ أَنْ أَنْصَحَ لَكُم إِنْ كَانَ اللهُ يُرِيدُ أَنْ يُغْوِيَكُم . وقلت : لا يريد ذلك ، وصرفت كلام نوح إلى ما لا يحتمله وهم البشر ، وقال موسى رَبَّنَا إِنَّكَ آتَيْتَ فِرْعَوْنَ وَمَلَأَهُ زِينَةً وَأَمْوَالا فِي الحَيَاةِ الدُّنْيَا رَبَّنَا لِيَضِلُّوا عَنْ سَبِيلِكَ ، وأنتم تقولون لم يؤتهم لذلك ولكن آنهم ليهتدوا .
And Noah said to his people: 'And my advice will not benefit you if I intend to advise you, if Allah intends to lead you astray.' And you (Mu'tazilites) say: He does not intend that, and you turned Noah's speech to what human minds cannot bear. And Moses said: 'Our Lord, You have given Pharaoh and his chiefs adornment and wealth in the worldly life, our Lord, that they may go astray from Your way.' And you say: He did not give them that for that, but rather He gave them that they might be guided.
Source pages p. 888

Commentary
This passage presents two more Quranic proofs that God's will includes misguidance and leading people astray, contradicting the Mu'tazilite claim that God only wills good. First, Maturidi quotes Noah's statement to his people (Surah Hud 11:34): '...if Allah intends to lead you astray.' Noah acknowledges that if God intends to misguide them, his advice will not benefit them. Maturidi points out that the Mu'tazilites reject this and twist Noah's words beyond what human understanding can bear. Second, he quotes Moses' prayer (Surah Yunus 10:88) where Moses asks God to give Pharaoh and his chiefs wealth and adornment so that they may go astray. The Mu'tazilites claim that God gave them these things for guidance, not for misguidance. Maturidi counters by stating the plain meaning of the prophets' words: the end goal is indeed misguidance, as the verses explicitly state.

Key Terms
إغواء
نوح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that God never wills misguidance for anyone.
Correction: Noah's statement directly contradicts this: he says 'if Allah intends to lead you astray', implying that such intention is possible.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what do the Mu'tazilites do with Noah's statement?
A) Accept it literally.
B) Reinterpret it beyond human capacity.
C) Deny its authenticity.
D) Apply it only to Noah.

Key Takeaway
Noah's words affirm that God can will misguidance, and Mu'tazilite reinterpretation is unwarranted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read Noah's words 'if Allah intends to lead you astray,' does your heart tremble at the possibility that you could be among those whom Allah intends to misguide? How does this possibility shape your dua today?
?
The Mu'tazilites rejected the plain meaning of the verse because it conflicted with their rational assumptions. Are there any verses of the Quran that you have twisted or downplayed because they made you uncomfortable or conflicted with your preconceived notions? Will you repent from that and submit to what Allah actually says?
?
Noah's advice was futile if Allah intended misguidance. Reflect on your own efforts in calling others to truth. Do you rely more on your eloquence and methods, or do you recognize that guidance and misguidance are entirely in Allah's hands? How can you balance sincere effort with complete reliance on Allah's will?

Moses' Prayer: Wealth for Pharaoh's Misguidance
Maturidi cites Moses' prayer to counter Mu'tazilite interpretation of divine gifts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Moses' prayer in the Quran shows that God grants wealth and adornment to lead some people astray, refuting the Mu'tazilite claim that God only gives for guidance.

Core Idea
Moses prayed that Pharaoh's wealth leads him astray, proving God's will includes misguidance.

The Mu'tazilites argued that God gives worldly blessings only to guide people. But Moses' prayer for Pharaoh and his chiefs explicitly asks that these gifts cause them to go astray. Maturidi highlights this verse to show that the prophets understood divine will to include misguidance.

Guided Path
1
Read Moses' prayer
Read the verse as Maturidi presents it: Moses acknowledges that God gave Pharaoh and his chiefs adornment and wealth so that they may go astray.

Maturidi notes that the Mu'tazilites say God gave these things for guidance, not misguidance.
This contradicts the clear wording of the prayer.
3

Quranic Verses Challenge Mu'tazilite Denial of Divine Will
Maturidi cites multiple Quranic verses to prove God withholds purity and guidance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses Quranic verses to prove that God's will is effective in purification and trial, refuting the Mu'tazilite claim that God cannot will evil.

Core Idea
God's will is explicitly stated in the Quran, and denying it is to claim more knowledge than Allah.

Maturidi confronts the Mu'tazilite position that God does not will purification of hearts for some or that He does not intend trials. He brings forward a series of Quranic verses that explicitly state God's will in these matters. The passage ends with a powerful rhetorical question that challenges the very basis of the Mu'tazilite reinterpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and translation once to get the flow. Notice the pattern: verse, Mu'tazilite claim, counter.
The passage is dense with Quranic references.
2
Identify each verse and its implicit refutation
List the verses: 1) 'Those are the ones for whom Allah does not intend to purify their hearts' (5:41), 2) 'whomsoever Allah intends to try' (5:41), 3) 'You will not have any authority over him' (5:41), 4) 'Let not those who disbelieve think that respite is good' (3:178), 5) 'Let not their wealth and children impress you' (9:85). For each, note what the Mu'tazilites say and how the verse contradicts them.
The first two are from the same verse.
3
Understand the concluding argument
Maturidi ends by comparing the Mu'tazilites to Jews and Christians, citing 'Are you more knowing or Allah?' (2:140). This is a sharp rebuke: they are denying clear scriptural statements.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
This lesson shows that divine will is not limited to good only; God wills both guidance and misguidance, purification and trial. The Mu'tazilite denial is based on a rationalist assumption that contradicts the Quran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أُولَئِكَ الَّذِينَ لَمْ يُرِدِ اللهُ أَنْ يُطَهِّرَ قُلُوبَهُم ، وأنتم تقولون : بل أراد الله ذلك . وقال : وَمَنْ يُرِدْ اللَّهُ فِتْنَتَهُ ، وأنتم تقولون : لم يردها . أو تقولون هذه شحنة وأنى كان رسول الله يريد أو يتمنى أن لا يكون حتى يقال له : فَلَنْ تَمْلِكَ لَهُ مِنَ اللهِ شَيْئًا . وقال الله تعالى : وَلَا يَحْسَبَنَّ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا إِنَّمَا نُمْلِي لَهُم خَيْرٌ لأنفُسِهِم . وقال تعالى : وَلَا تُعْجِبْكَ أَمْوَالُهُم وَأَوْلَادُهُمْ . أخبر ما يريد بما أعطى ، وهم يقولون : لا يريد . فما يقال لأمثال هؤلاء إلا ما قيل لليهود والنصارى : أَأَنْتُمْ أَعْلَمُ أَمْ اللَّهُ .
Those are the ones for whom Allah does not intend to purify their hearts, and you say: Rather Allah intended that. And He said: 'And whomsoever Allah intends to try (fitnah),' and you say: He did not intend it. Or you say: This is a burden, and how could the Messenger of Allah intend or wish that it not be, until it was said to him: 'You will not have any authority over him from Allah.' And Allah, exalted, said: 'And let not those who disbelieve think that We give them respite is good for themselves.' And He said: 'And let not their wealth and children impress you.' He informed what He intends by what He gives, and they say: He does not intend. So nothing is said to such people except what was said to the Jews and Christians: 'Are you more knowing or Allah?'
Source pages p. 889

Commentary
In this passage, Maturidi presents a series of Quranic verses that explicitly state God's will in matters of guidance and punishment, then contrasts them with the Mu'tazilite reinterpretations. First, Surah Al-Ma'idah 5:41: 'Those are the ones for whom Allah does not intend to purify their hearts'—the Mu'tazilites claim the opposite, that God does intend purification. Second, the same verse says 'whomsoever Allah intends to try'—they deny that. Maturidi then mentions the Prophet's inability to guide those whom God has not chosen, citing 'you will not have any authority over him' (5:41). Third, Surah Al-Imran 3:178 warns against thinking that respite is good. Fourth, Surah At-Tawbah 9:85 warns not to be impressed by wealth and children, as God intends punishment through them. The Mu'tazilites deny that God intends evil. Maturidi finally invokes the verse from Surah Al-Baqarah 2:140: 'Are you more knowing or Allah?' as the ultimate rebuke.

Key Terms
تطهير
القلب
فتنة
الإملاء
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will for purification is universal; the Quran shows it is specific and not for all.
Correction: The Quran explicitly states that Allah does not intend to purify some hearts (5:41), indicating that His will is selective.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi cite as the ultimate response to those who reinterpret Quranic verses on divine will?
A) 'Are you more knowing or Allah?'
B) 'You will not have any authority over him.'
C) 'Let not their wealth impress you.'
D) 'Those are the ones for whom Allah does not intend to purify.'

Key Takeaway
God's will is clearly expressed in the Quran, and no one can claim to know better than Allah by denying His intentions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardship or failure, do you ever find yourself blaming fate or feeling that Allah should have willed differently for you? How does this lesson's affirmation that Allah indeed wills trials change your response? What would it look like to submit to His will in that moment?
?
Are there Quranic verses that you have read but subconsciously reinterpreted to avoid their uncomfortable implications—such as that Allah guides whom He wills and misguides whom He wills? Can you name one? What would it cost you to take it literally?
?
The Prophet (peace be upon him) himself could not override Allah's will for someone (Quran 5:41). Is there someone in your life you are trying to 'fix' or guide, yet Allah has not willed it? How does this lesson humble your sense of control, and will you trust Allah's plan instead of your own effort?

God's Promise to Fill Hell Proves He Wills Disobedience
Maturidi presents a dilemma: either God wills to fulfill His promise or to break it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses a dilemma to argue that God's unconditional promise to fill Hell necessitates that He wills the disobedience of the inhabitants.

Core Idea
God's promise to fill Hell forces the conclusion that He wills sin.

This lesson unpacks a sharp logical argument from Imam Maturidi. He takes a clear Quranic promise—God will fill Hell with all jinn and humans—and asks the Mu'tazilites a question with no easy answer: does God intend to keep this promise or not? The answer reveals a fundamental truth about the divine will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: a dilemma, two branches, and a further subdivision.
The Arabic contains some textual ambiguities; the commentary will clarify.
2
Identify the two main options
Maturidi first asks whether God intends to fulfill His promise to fill Hell or to break it. Identify these two options in the text.
3
Follow the consequences of each option
Trace what happens if the Mu'tazilites choose the first option (God intends to break His promise) or the second (He intends to fulfill it). Each leads to a problem.
The second option branches again: does He fulfill with people obedient or disobedient?
4
Understand the final resolution
The only consistent answer is that God intends to fulfill His promise while the inhabitants are disobedient. This proves God wills disobedience.
This conclusion aligns with Maturidi's doctrine that all things occur by God's will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الله جل ثناؤه : الأَمْلان حقب الجنَّةِ والنَّاسِ أَجْمَعِينَ . فنقول لهم : أراد الله عز وجل أن يفي بما وعد هذا، أو أراد أن يكذب وعده ويبطل وعيده. فإن قالوا بالثاني أعظموا القول ووصفوه بإرادة السفه والكذب ، وكفى بهذا القول خزيا . وإن قالوا : أراد أن يفى به قيل : أراد أن يفى به ، وهو يريد أن يطيعوه ، فيفى وهم يطيعون له أو يعصون . فإن قال بالأول فهو جورٌ أراد ؛ لأن فعله جور ، فإرادة كونه إرادة فعل الجور أن يكون له فعلا . وقال الله تعالى : وَمَا اللهُ يُرِيدُ ظُلْمًا لِلعِبَادِ . وإن قال بالثاني أذعنوا للحق ، وقالوا بالعدل ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And Allah, Mighty and Majestic, said: 'I will fill Hell with jinn and mankind all together.' So we say to them: Did Allah, Mighty and Majestic, intend to fulfill what He promised this, or did He intend to belie His promise and nullify His threat? If they say the second, they have made the statement great and described Him with willing foolishness (safah) and lying, and this statement suffices as disgrace. And if they say: He intended to fulfill it, it is said: He intended to fulfill it, and He intends that they obey Him — so He fulfills while they obey or disobey Him. If they say the first (that He intends to fulfill while they obey), then it is injustice He willed, because His act would be injustice, so willing its occurrence is willing the act of injustice to be His act. And Allah said: 'And Allah does not intend injustice for the servants.' And if they say the second (that He intends to fulfill even if they disobey), they have submitted to the truth and spoken of justice, and there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 889

Commentary
This passage presents a crucial logical dilemma. Maturidi quotes the Quranic verse (Hud 11:119) where God swears to fill Hell with jinn and mankind. He then asks the Mu'tazilites: Does God intend to fulfill this promise or not? If they say He does not intend to fulfill it (i.e., He will break His word), that would attribute foolishness and lying to God, which is disgraceful. If they say He intends to fulfill it, then we ask: does He will the obedience or disobedience of those who enter Hell? If He wills to fulfill the promise while they are obedient (i.e., they enter Hell obediently?), that would be unjust, because punishing the obedient is injustice. But God says He does not intend injustice (Surah Ghafir 40:31). Therefore, the only consistent answer is that God intends to fulfill His promise despite their disobedience, meaning He wills their disobedience. This forces the Mu'tazilites to either accept that God wills evil or face a contradiction. The phrase 'وهم يطيعون له أو يعصون' is ambiguous but the argument is clear.

Key Terms
وعد
وعيد
جور
ظلم
إرادة
سفه
كذب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's promise to fill Hell is conditional on human disobedience, but Maturidi argues it is unconditional and forces God to will sin.
Correction: The passage shows that if God intends to fulfill an unconditional promise, then He must will the disobedience of those who end up in Hell, otherwise His act would be unjust.

Checkpoint
What is the dilemma Maturidi poses to the Mu'tazilites regarding God's promise to fill Hell?
A) Either God will fill Hell with obedient people or with disobedient people.
B) Either God intends to fulfill His promise or intend to break it.
C) Either God is just or He is merciful.
D) Either God willed creation or He did not.

Key Takeaway
God's unconditional promise to fill Hell proves that He wills the disobedience of its inhabitants, a key point in Maturidi's doctrine of qadar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Does the fact that God wills the disobedience of some people make you uneasy with His justice? Examine your heart: do you really trust that whatever God wills is just, or do you secretly believe you know better? What would it take for you to surrender that hidden judgment?
?
If God wills your obedience, yet also wills that others disobey, does that prompt gratitude or pride in you? Are you ready to face the frightening possibility that your own obedience is entirely from God's will, and not from your own merit? How does this truth humble you?
?
The Mu'tazilites could not accept that God wills evil, but this passage shows their logic leads to attributing foolishness or injustice to God. Have you ever rejected a truth about God because it was uncomfortable? Will you now submit to this difficult truth, even if it challenges your preferred image of God? What will you do to make that submission real?

God's Will Excludes None: Final Verses
Maturidi cites two Quranic verses to show God's will excludes some from the Hereafter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's will includes intentionally excluding some people from the Hereafter, as proven by two Quranic verses.

Core Idea
God's will is not limited to good for all; He intentionally excludes some from the Hereafter.

Maturidi concludes his discussion on God's will by citing two powerful verses. These verses directly refute the claim that God always intends good for everyone. Instead, they show that God's will includes both giving and withholding blessings. Read on to see how these verses settle the matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the two verses carefully
Read the Arabic verses once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the contrast between human desire and divine will.
The first verse is from Surah Al-Imran 3:176, the second from Surah Al-Anfal 8:67.
2
Understand Maturidi's explanation
After the first verse, Maturidi adds that anyone who thinks all good comes from himself is mistaken; rather, Allah grants the share in the Hereafter by His will.
This links the verse to the theme of God's sole agency.
3
Ask: What is refuted here?
Consider how these verses challenge the Mu'tazilite claim that God wills good for everyone. The verses explicitly show God intending to exclude some.
The common misunderstanding is that God's mercy guarantees a share for all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الله تعالى : يُرِيدُ اللهُ أَلَّا يَجْعَلَ لَهُمْ حَظًّا فِي الآخِرَةِ . ومن أراد أن يكون منه كل خير فقد أراد له الحظ في الآخرة . وقال تعالى : تُرِيدُونَ عَرَضَ الدُّنْيَا وَاللَّهُ يُرِيدُ الآخِرَةَ .
And Allah, exalted, said: 'Allah intends not to give them any share in the Hereafter.' And whoever intends that all good be from him has intended for him the share in the Hereafter. And He said: 'You desire the display of this world, and Allah desires the Hereafter.'
Source pages p. 889

Commentary
In this final passage, Maturidi quotes two more verses to reinforce his argument. First, from Surah Al-Imran 3:176: 'Allah intends not to give them any share in the Hereafter.' Maturidi explains that if someone intends that all good comes from himself, that person is deluded; rather, it is God who grants the share in the Hereafter by His will. The second verse, from Surah Al-Anfal 8:67 (though cut off), contrasts human desire for worldly gain with God's desire for the Hereafter. The point is that God's will is decisive and encompasses even the deprivation of blessings. The Mu'tazilite claim that God wills good for everyone is refuted by these clear statements that God intends to exclude some.

Key Terms
الآخرة
الحظ
العرض الدنيا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's mercy ensures everyone gets a share in the Hereafter.
Correction: The Quran explicitly denies this in Surah Al-Imran 3:176, where Allah says He intends not to give some a share. God's will is not constrained to always give.

Checkpoint
According to Surah Al-Imran 3:176, what does Allah intend for some people?
A) To give them a great share in the Hereafter.
B) Not to give them any share in the Hereafter.
C) To increase their wealth.
D) To guide them.

Key Takeaway
God's will includes intentionally excluding some from the Hereafter, as proven by the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that Allah could intentionally exclude you from the Hereafter, how would your daily choices change? Are you living as if your 'share' is guaranteed, or as if it depends entirely on His will?
?
In what areas of your life do you find yourself 'desiring the display of this world' instead of what Allah desires? What one action can you take today to align your desires with His?
?
Are you more concerned with your own salvation or with calling others to the truth, knowing that Allah may will to exclude some? How does this verse affect your urgency in sharing Islam with those who reject it?

Divine Attributes

All Things Occur by God's Will
Maturidi argues that all occurrences, including Iblis's will, are by God's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi establishes that every event, including the will of Iblis, is within God's will and why it is appropriate to attribute all occurrences to God.

Core Idea
Everything that occurs, even Iblis's will, is within God's will.

In this passage, Maturidi addresses a profound theological question: how can we reconcile the existence of evil and the will of Iblis with the belief that everything happens by God's will? He begins with a common understanding in people's hearts and builds a logical argument that attributes all realities to God's will, while cautioning against attributing them to Iblis or other disobedient beings. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications for our understanding of divine sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice how Maturidi starts with a statement about Iblis's will and then draws a conclusion about God's will.
Pay attention to the phrases 'بما شاء إبليس' and 'ثبت أن'.
2
Identify the logical structure
Identify the premise (what people understand about Iblis's will) and the conclusion (that all things exist by God's will). Observe how Maturidi moves from the specific case of Iblis to a general principle.
The phrase 'على العلم يكون ما لا يحصى من الشر' introduces the basis for the conclusion.
3
Understand the attribution rule
Reflect on why Maturidi says it is good to attribute events to God and bad to attribute them to Iblis. This is the practical outcome of the argument.
Notice the words 'استحسنوا' and 'استقبحوا' which show moral evaluation.
4
Contemplate the final phrase
The passage ends with 'ولا قوة إلا بالله'. Consider how this phrase reinforces the argument's conclusion about reliance on God's power.
This phrase is a reminder that all power belongs to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنظيم القول فى القلوب بأن ما شاء إبليس كان ، وما لم لا يشأ لا يكون ، على العلم يكون ما لا يحصى من الشر وخروج الخير من الكون في ذلك ، ووجود مشيئة إبليس بذلك ، ثبت أن كون حقائق الأشياء بما لله فيها مشيئة ، وامتناعها عن الكون لذلك ؛ لذلك استحسنوا إضافة ذلك إلى الله واستقبحوا إضافته إلى إبليس وغيره من العصاة ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The arrangement of the statement concerning hearts is that whatever Iblis willed, exists, and whatever he did not will, does not exist. Upon knowledge, there occurs innumerable evil and the removal of good from existence in that, and the existence of Iblis's will thereby, it is established that the existence of the realities of things is by God's will regarding them, and their non-existence is due to that; therefore they considered it good to attribute that to God and considered it bad to attribute it to Iblis and other disobedient ones. There is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 891

Commentary
This passage is an initial argument establishing that all things, including evil and the will of Iblis, occur only by God's will. It begins by stating the common understanding in hearts: whatever Iblis wills happens, and what he does not will does not happen. This observation leads to the conclusion that the existence of things is by God's will, and non-existence is due to God's will not being present. Therefore, it is appropriate to attribute all events to God, not to Iblis or other sinners.

Key Terms
مشيئة
إبليس
إضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that Iblis has an independent will that operates outside of God's will, i.e., that evil occurs without God's will or control.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that even Iblis's will exists and functions only by God's will. Therefore, all events, including evil, are within God's will and ultimately attributed to Him. Attributing them to Iblis is blameworthy because it obscures the truth of divine sovereignty.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it considered good to attribute events to God and bad to attribute them to Iblis?
A) Because Iblis is a disobedient being
B) Because the existence of Iblis's will itself is by God's will
C) Because people dislike Iblis more than God
D) Because Iblis's will is not real

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's argument establishes that every occurrence, including the will of Iblis, exists only by God's will, so all events should be attributed to God alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you instinctively attribute it to your own weakness or to God's will? How does Maturidi's argument challenge your inclination to distance God from your failures? What would change in your repentance if you truly believed your sin was only possible because God willed it?
?
Consider the evil Iblis has caused. If you truly believed that even Iblis's will exists only by God's will, would it change how you view the tragedies in your life? What specific event makes you resist this truth, and how can you surrender that resistance to God's wisdom?
?
The text says it is 'good' to attribute all events to God and 'bad' to attribute them to Iblis. Is there a recent hardship you still resent, secretly blaming someone else or even yourself? What would it look like for you to honestly attribute that event to God and thank Him for it?

Divine Attributes

Analogy from Human Experience: God's Will Is Not Weak or Compelled
Maturidi presents analogies from human experience to argue for God's will as a distinct attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational arguments from human experience that demonstrate God's will is distinct and not compelled.

Core Idea
Willing friendship with an enemy is weakness; God is beyond that.

Maturidi begins by drawing analogies from the witnessed realm. He argues that no sane person wills the friendship of an enemy, and that acts of choice require will while acts of compulsion lack it. These analogies establish that God's will is neither weak nor compelled.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: analogy then dichotomy.
2
Analyze the first analogy
Maturidi argues that willing friendship with an enemy is a sign of weakness. Apply this to God: He cannot will friendship with Iblis.
This guards against attributing imperfection to God.
3
Understand the will-compulsion dichotomy
If an act is by choice, it requires will; if by compulsion, it lacks will. If God were to cause an act without willing it, He would be compelled—impossible.
This shows that God's will is essential to His action.
4
Connect to the broader argument
These points establish that God's will is a real attribute, not reducible to creation or compulsion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن الذي عليه أمر الشاهد أن كل من أراد موالاة من يختار عداوتة ، كان يكون عن ضعف وخرف ، فلا يجوز أن يكون الله تعالى يريد مولاة إبليس والذين اختاروا عداوته ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأيضا أن شرط كل من فعله اختيار الإرادة ، وكل من فعله الاضطرار أنه غير مريد لذلك ، فلو كان الله لفعل العبد غير مريد ليكون على ما كان ، يكون مضطرا ؛ ولذلك لا يجوز أن يكون لأحد فى فعل غيره إرادة ؛ لما لا يحتمل خروجه على ما يريد ، وسموا ذلك تمنياً ، فعلى ذلك لو توهم كون شيء لم يرده الله كانت إرادته تخرج مخرج التمنى . وأيضا أنه لو جعل لنا أن نبوة نبي يقول ، يكون ذلك معصية لنا أن نريده ، من حيث يكون آية ، وإن لم يكن أن يعصى ، فمثله إذ علم الله أنه أخبر عن ذلك. وأنه أن لا يكون ، كان له أن لا يريد في الحكمة ، على أنه لا يختلف أن ليس للعبد أن يسأل الله هداية بشر يعلم علم من يعلم أنه لا يهتدى نحو إبليس لم يكن له أن يقول : اللهم اهده ؛ لما يعلم أنه لا يكون ، ثم محال أن يكون علينا إرادة ذلك ، وإذ لم يكن علينا إرادة ما أنه لا يكون لم يجز أن يقال ذلك على الله ؛ إذ كونها علينا إنما يكون إذا ا جهلنا بحاله ، ولا قوة إلا بالله ثم نسأل عمن يريد أن يكون شتم رسول الله مثل شتم إبليس في المرتبة والمأتم ، أليس هو بمتعد سفيه كافر ؟ لا بد من : بلى . فيقال : من أراد أن يكون شتم رسول الله أمرا عظيما لا يبلغه شتم أحد من الخلق أن يكون محمودا ؟ لا بد من : بلى . قيل : فمن يريد أن يكون الشتم منه كذلك ؛ إذ محال كونه أحد أعظم ولا بأصغر ولا نحو ذلك ، فلا بد أن يقول : مين كافر ، وفي ذلك جواز إرادة فعل الكفر من وجه لا يحتمل الذم من ذلك الوجه ، وبالله التوفيق . ثم الأصل الذي هو معتمد المعتزلة أن إرادة الله ليست غير خلقه وأن تأويلها على ما فسر الكعبى ليس غير أنه لم يغلب ولم يضطر في فعله ، وهذا المعنى قد أعطوه جميعا في فعل العباد ، فإنكارهم الارادة وهذا معناها بعد الإعطاء في الجملة ، والله الموفق .
Also, what is established in the witnessed realm is that whoever wills friendship with one who chooses enmity, that would be due to weakness and senility, so it is not permissible that God Most High wills friendship with Iblis and those who choose enmity with Him. There is no power except with God. Also, the condition of everyone whose act is by choice is the will, and everyone whose act is by compulsion is not willing it. So if God were to cause the servant's act without being willing for it to be as it is, He would be compelled. Therefore, it is not permissible that anyone have will in the act of another, because the act cannot come out as he wills, and they call that wishful thinking. Thus, if one imagined that something occurs that God did not will, His will would be like wishful thinking. Also, if it were made for us that a prophet's prophecy says, that would be disobedience for us to will it, in that it would be a sign, even if it is not to be disobeyed. Similarly, when God knows that He has informed about that, and that it will not be, it is wise for Him not to will it, since there is no difference that the servant may not ask God for guidance for a person whom he knows, with the knowledge of one who knows that he will not be guided like Iblis, he may not say: O God, guide him, because he knows it will not be. Then it is impossible that such willing be upon us, and since it is not upon us to will what will not be, it is not permissible to say that about God, because its being upon us is only when we are ignorant of its state. There is no power except with God. Then we ask about one who wills that the insult of the Messenger of God be like the insult of Iblis in rank and calamity: is he not a transgressor, foolish, disbeliever? There is no escape from: yes. Then it is said: one who wills that the insult of the Messenger of God be a great matter not reached by the insult of any creature, would he be praised? There is no escape from: yes. He said: then one who wills the insult to be from him likewise, since it is impossible that it be greater or smaller or the like, so he must say: who is a disbeliever? And in that is the permissibility of willing the act of disbelief in one respect that does not incur blame from that respect. And with God is success. Then the principle relied upon by the Mu'tazila is that God's will is nothing other than His creation, and its interpretation according to what al-Ka'bi explained is nothing other than that He is not overcome nor compelled in His act. And this meaning they have all granted concerning the acts of servants. Their denial of the will, this being its meaning, after granting it in general, is (inconsistent). And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 892

Commentary
This passage first continues with rational arguments: an analogy from human experience: no one wills friendship with an enemy; likewise God does not will friendship with Iblis. Another argument: an act by choice involves will, by compulsion lacks will; if God's act were without will, He would be compelled, which is impossible. Therefore, no one can will another's act, as that would be wishful thinking (تمني). If something occurred without God's will, His will would be like wishful thinking. Next, an argument about the prophet's prophecy: if we were to will a prophet's statement to be disobedience, that would be wrong, and since God knows what will not happen, He does not will it. Then the passage uses a dialectical question: consider someone who wills insult to the Prophet to be as severe as Iblis's insult; that person is a disbeliever. But if someone wills that insult to be a great matter (i.e., to magnify the Prophet's status), that is praiseworthy. The point: willing disbelief can be permissible in some respects (e.g., to show the gravity of the sin). Finally, the passage addresses the Mu'tazila principle: they claim God's will is nothing other than His creation, interpreting it as meaning He is not overcome or compelled. The author argues that they have admitted the reality of will in human acts, but then deny it for God, which is inconsistent.

Key Terms
موالاة
عداوة
اختيار
اضطرار
تمني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first analogy, why can't God will friendship with Iblis?
A) Because Iblis is a created being
B) Because it would be a sign of weakness
C) Because God does not have will
D) Because Iblis does not exist

Key Takeaway
God's will is not forced by compulsion nor tainted by weakness; it is a perfect attribute accompanying His actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see evil or suffering in the world, do you ever doubt that God fully wills what happens? How does the concept of tamanni in this passage challenge that doubt? Commit today to affirming that everything occurs exactly as God wills, even when you don't understand.
?
Examine your own desires: do you ever will something that is weak or forced, like wanting friendship with someone who opposes you? How does this passage purify your understanding of God's will as perfect and uncompelled? Resolve to never project your own flawed will onto Allah.
?
If you truly believed that God's will is never wishful thinking but always effective, how would it change the way you pray, plan, and face disappointments? Right now, make a concrete decision to trust His will more than your own plans.

Knowledge and Will: God Does Not Will What Will Not Happen
Maturidi argues that God's will is consistent with His knowledge, not wishful thinking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God does not will what He knows will not happen, and how this refutes the idea that His will is like wishful thinking.

Core Idea
God's will is grounded in knowledge; He cannot will the impossible.

Maturidi continues by discussing the relationship between knowledge and will. He uses the example of a prophet's prophecy and the impossibility of willing what is known not to occur. This argument shows that God's will is not arbitrary but aligns with His knowledge and wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if it were made for us...'
2
Understand the prophecy example
If a prophet's statement were considered disobedience, we would be wrong to will it. God knows the outcome, so He does not will what will not happen.
This distinguishes human and divine will.
3
Apply to the case of Iblis
We cannot ask God to guide Iblis because we know he will not be guided. Similarly, God does not will what He knows will not be.
4
Conclude the point
If God were to will what He knows won't happen, His will would be like human wishful thinking, which is impossible.
This refutes the Mu'tazila who claim God's will is identical to His creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن الذي عليه أمر الشاهد أن كل من أراد موالاة من يختار عداوتة ، كان يكون عن ضعف وخرف ، فلا يجوز أن يكون الله تعالى يريد مولاة إبليس والذين اختاروا عداوته ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأيضا أن شرط كل من فعله اختيار الإرادة ، وكل من فعله الاضطرار أنه غير مريد لذلك ، فلو كان الله لفعل العبد غير مريد ليكون على ما كان ، يكون مضطرا ؛ ولذلك لا يجوز أن يكون لأحد فى فعل غيره إرادة ؛ لما لا يحتمل خروجه على ما يريد ، وسموا ذلك تمنياً ، فعلى ذلك لو توهم كون شيء لم يرده الله كانت إرادته تخرج مخرج التمنى . وأيضا أنه لو جعل لنا أن نبوة نبي يقول ، يكون ذلك معصية لنا أن نريده ، من حيث يكون آية ، وإن لم يكن أن يعصى ، فمثله إذ علم الله أنه أخبر عن ذلك. وأنه أن لا يكون ، كان له أن لا يريد في الحكمة ، على أنه لا يختلف أن ليس للعبد أن يسأل الله هداية بشر يعلم علم من يعلم أنه لا يهتدى نحو إبليس لم يكن له أن يقول : اللهم اهده ؛ لما يعلم أنه لا يكون ، ثم محال أن يكون علينا إرادة ذلك ، وإذ لم يكن علينا إرادة ما أنه لا يكون لم يجز أن يقال ذلك على الله ؛ إذ كونها علينا إنما يكون إذا ا جهلنا بحاله ، ولا قوة إلا بالله ثم نسأل عمن يريد أن يكون شتم رسول الله مثل شتم إبليس في المرتبة والمأتم ، أليس هو بمتعد سفيه كافر ؟ لا بد من : بلى . فيقال : من أراد أن يكون شتم رسول الله أمرا عظيما لا يبلغه شتم أحد من الخلق أن يكون محمودا ؟ لا بد من : بلى . قيل : فمن يريد أن يكون الشتم منه كذلك ؛ إذ محال كونه أحد أعظم ولا بأصغر ولا نحو ذلك ، فلا بد أن يقول : مين كافر ، وفي ذلك جواز إرادة فعل الكفر من وجه لا يحتمل الذم من ذلك الوجه ، وبالله التوفيق . ثم الأصل الذي هو معتمد المعتزلة أن إرادة الله ليست غير خلقه وأن تأويلها على ما فسر الكعبى ليس غير أنه لم يغلب ولم يضطر في فعله ، وهذا المعنى قد أعطوه جميعا في فعل العباد ، فإنكارهم الارادة وهذا معناها بعد الإعطاء في الجملة ، والله الموفق .
Also, what is established in the witnessed realm is that whoever wills friendship with one who chooses enmity, that would be due to weakness and senility, so it is not permissible that God Most High wills friendship with Iblis and those who choose enmity with Him. There is no power except with God. Also, the condition of everyone whose act is by choice is the will, and everyone whose act is by compulsion is not willing it. So if God were to cause the servant's act without being willing for it to be as it is, He would be compelled. Therefore, it is not permissible that anyone have will in the act of another, because the act cannot come out as he wills, and they call that wishful thinking. Thus, if one imagined that something occurs that God did not will, His will would be like wishful thinking. Also, if it were made for us that a prophet's prophecy says, that would be disobedience for us to will it, in that it would be a sign, even if it is not to be disobeyed. Similarly, when God knows that He has informed about that, and that it will not be, it is wise for Him not to will it, since there is no difference that the servant may not ask God for guidance for a person whom he knows, with the knowledge of one who knows that he will not be guided like Iblis, he may not say: O God, guide him, because he knows it will not be. Then it is impossible that such willing be upon us, and since it is not upon us to will what will not be, it is not permissible to say that about God, because its being upon us is only when we are ignorant of its state. There is no power except with God. Then we ask about one who wills that the insult of the Messenger of God be like the insult of Iblis in rank and calamity: is he not a transgressor, foolish, disbeliever? There is no escape from: yes. Then it is said: one who wills that the insult of the Messenger of God be a great matter not reached by the insult of any creature, would he be praised? There is no escape from: yes. He said: then one who wills the insult to be from him likewise, since it is impossible that it be greater or smaller or the like, so he must say: who is a disbeliever? And in that is the permissibility of willing the act of disbelief in one respect that does not incur blame from that respect. And with God is success. Then the principle relied upon by the Mu'tazila is that God's will is nothing other than His creation, and its interpretation according to what al-Ka'bi explained is nothing other than that He is not overcome nor compelled in His act. And this meaning they have all granted concerning the acts of servants. Their denial of the will, this being its meaning, after granting it in general, is (inconsistent). And God is the Grantor of success.

Dialectical Refutation of the Mu'tazila on God's Will
Maturidi uses a dialectical example to refute the Mu'tazila equation of God's will with His creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's dialectical argument about the insult to the Prophet and how it exposes the inconsistency of the Mu'tazila position on God's will.

Core Idea
Willing an insult can be praiseworthy or blameworthy; God's will is beyond such contradictions.

Maturidi uses a practical example: consider someone who wills the insult of the Messenger of God to be as severe as that of Iblis. That person is a disbeliever. But one who wills the insult to be a great matter (to magnify the Prophet's status) is praised. This shows that the same act (insult) can be willed with different intentions, and the Mu'tazila rejection of a distinct will leads to contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the dialectical format: question and answer.
2
Analyze the first question
If someone wills the insult of the Prophet to be like that of Iblis in rank and calamity, that person is a transgressor, foolish, disbeliever. Everyone agrees.
3
Analyze the second question
If someone wills the insult to be a great matter (to show the greatness of the Prophet), that person is praised. So the same act can be willed differently.
4
Understand the conclusion
Willing disbelief can be permissible in some respects without blame. This opens the door to a nuanced understanding of will.
5
Relate to Mu'tazila principle
The Mu'tazila say God's will is identical to His creation. But they admit that human acts involve will. This is inconsistent because if will is just the act itself, then willing an insult would be identical to the insult, creating contradictions.
Maturidi exposes this inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

?
If God's will is beyond contradiction—neither overcome nor compelled—how does that truth confront your own tendency to excuse your disobedience by circumstances? Will you commit to examining your will in the light of His majesty and aligning it with His pleasure, starting today?

The Two Meanings of God's Command (Amr)
Maturidi explains the two meanings of God's command: creative and prescriptive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between God's creative command and His prescriptive command, and why this distinction resolves an objection about divine will and human action.

Core Idea
God's command has two meanings: one creates, the other obligates.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi responds to an objection that claims since God's command is effective and cannot be repelled, everything that happens must be morally willed by God. He clarifies that the word 'command' (amr) has two distinct meanings: a creative command that brings things into existence, and a prescriptive command that imposes obligations. Understanding this distinction is crucial for reconciling divine sovereignty with human moral responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Maturidi's initial response
Read the Arabic passage once and notice how Maturidi introduces the objection and his reply that there are two aspects.
The objector claims God's command is effective and cannot be repelled.
2
Identify the two meanings of command
Look for the two aspects: the creative command (amr al-takwin) and the prescriptive command (amr al-taklif). Understand how each is defined.
The creative command is like God saying 'Be' and it is; the prescriptive command is about obligation.
3
Understand why the objection fails
Reflect on why the prescriptive command does not imply that God wills sinful actions. Note that the agent is free (mukhtar) and has his own will (irada).
It is impermissible to say God is commanded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض بقولهم: أمر الله نافذ ولا مرد لأمره. قيل: لهذا وجهان: أحدهما أمر التكوين كقوله: إنما أمره إذا أراد شيئًا أن يقول له كن فيكون، فهذا لا مرد له، ويدخل في ذلك فعل الخلق جميعا، وهو مثل الأول. والثاني أن يراد به حقيقة حق الأمر أن لا يرد عن الوجه الذي يكون الأمر وما به الأمر فيما لم يكن، لم يخرج الأمر عن حده وتزول الإرادة؛ إذ هي المكون، والأمر ليفعل أن كل مختار في الفعل موصوف بالإرادة، ولا يجوز أن يقال: تري هو مأمور؛ لإحالة وصف الله به، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he objected with their statement: 'The command of God is effective and there is no repelling His command.' It was said: This has two aspects: One is the command of creation (amr al-takwin), as His saying: 'His command, when He wills a thing, is only to say to it "Be", and it is' [Quran 36:82]. This has no repelling, and the actions of all creation enter into it, and it is like the first. The second is that what is meant by it is the true reality of the command (haqīqat haqq al-amr) that it not be repelled from the aspect by which the command and that by which the command occurs in what was not, the command does not exceed its limit and will (irāda) ceases, for it is the constituent (al-mukawwin), and the command is for action, that every agent endowed with choice (mukhtār) in action is described with will (irāda), and it is not permissible to say: 'Do you see that he is commanded?' because of the impossibility of describing God with it. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 894

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection by distinguishing two meanings of God's command (amr). The objector claimed that since God's command is effective and cannot be repelled, then everything that happens must be willed by God in a moral sense. Maturidi replies that 'command' has two aspects. First, the creative command (amr al-takwin), like God saying 'Be' to create, which is irresistible and includes all actions of creation; this does not imply moral approval. Second, the prescriptive command (amr al-taklif), which pertains to obligation; this command is not about actual creation but about what ought to be done. The prescriptive command does not entail that God wills the sinful act, because the act is done by a free agent (mukhtār) who has his own will (irāda). It is impermissible to say that God is the one 'commanded' in the sense of being obligated. Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
أمر التكوين (amr al-takwīn)
أمر التكليف (amr al-taklīf)
إرادة (irāda)
مختار (mukhtār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's command in the Quran always implies He wills the action, so everything that happens is morally approved by God.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that the prescriptive command does not entail that God wills the sinful act; the command is about obligation, not creation.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how many meanings does God's command (amr) have in the objection about it being effective?
A) One meaning: creative command only.
B) Two meanings: creative and prescriptive.
C) Three meanings: creative, prescriptive, and punitive.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
God's command has two aspects: creative and prescriptive; the prescriptive command does not entail that God wills the sinful act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever excuse yourself by saying 'It was God's will' or 'God's command is effective'? How does the distinction between creative and prescriptive command challenge that excuse and bring you back to personal responsibility?
?
Do you live as if you are merely a puppet of God's decree, or as a mukhtār (free agent) who must use your will (irāda) to obey His prescriptive command? Which one do you truly believe, and how will that belief change your choices today?
?
The prescriptive command obligates you but does not force you. Are you grateful for this gift of free will, or do you resent the responsibility? How can you turn that resentment into a commitment to fulfill the very commands you are free to choose?

The Proper Question About God's Will and the Wise
Maturidi corrects an objection about God's will for evil and defends God's wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refines the question about God's will to preserve divine wisdom and avoid attributing evil to God.

Core Idea
The question is not whether God wills evil, but whether He wills it as evil.

In this passage, Maturidi addresses a common objection: if God wills all things, does He will evil? He shows that the opponent's framing is flawed. The proper question concerns the moral quality of the act, not its mere occurrence. This distinction is crucial for maintaining God's wisdom and justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation without analyzing. Get a sense of Maturidi's dialectical style.
2
Identify the opponent's initial argument
The opponent uses the verse to argue that if God wills rectitude, then all that occurs must be good. Maturidi rejects this reasoning.
3
See how Maturidi corrects the question
The opponent asks 'Do you intend that God be reviled?' Maturidi says the correct question is about the moral quality (ugly and wrathful) of the reviling.
4
Understand the final trap
Maturidi ends with a dilemma: if the wise does not will something that would be foolish for a liar to will, then the opponent must admit God's will is not for evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال في قوله: وَمَا تَشَاءُونَ إِلَّا أَنْ يَشَاءَ اللهُ إن الاستقامة كانت بها، وذلك فاسد؛ لأن الله قد شاء: فلا يكون، فلا يجوز فيما يكون بها إلا على قولنا إنها تكون لا محالة إذا شاء الله ثم قال: أتريد الله أن يشتم؟ وقد أخطأ في السؤال، بل حقه أن يقال: أتريد الله أن يكون فعل الشتم ممن يشتمه قبيحا مسخوطا؟ ثم قال: معاذ الله؛ لأنه نهى عنه وغضب عليه، ولا يفعل الحكيم ذلك. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: قيل: أحكيم لا يشاء ذلك مما لو كان الذي شاء كاذباً سفيها؟ فإن قال: نعم، بان جهله بالحكيم، وإن قال: لا، ألزم القول بالمشيئة؛ إذ في فوت ذلك كذبه وسفهه، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And he said regarding His saying: 'And you do not will except that God wills' [Quran 81:29] that rectitude (al-istiqāma) was by it, and that is corrupt; because God has willed and it does not happen, so it is not permissible regarding what occurs by it except on our statement that it occurs necessarily when God wills. Then he said: 'Do you intend (turīd) that God be reviled?' And he erred in the question; rather, his duty is to say: 'Do you intend that the act of reviling from the one who reviles be ugly (qabīḥ) and wrathful (maskhūṭ)?' Then he said: 'God forbid! Because He has forbidden it and is angered by it, and the wise (al-ḥakīm) does not do that.' The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: It was said: Does the wise not will that which if the one who willed were lying and foolish? If he says: Yes, then his ignorance of the Wise becomes apparent; and if he says: No, then the statement of will (mashī'a) is necessitated, for in missing that there would be his lying and foolishness. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 894

Commentary
This passage addresses another objection regarding God's will (mashī'a). The opponent quoted the verse 'You do not will except that God wills' and argued that if rectitude was by God's will, then whatever occurs must be good. Maturidi rejects this, saying that the existence of something by God's will does not imply moral rectitude; it only means it occurs necessarily when God wills. Then the opponent asked: 'Do you intend that God be reviled?' Maturidi corrects the question: the proper question is not whether God wills the reviling, but whether God wills that act to be ugly and displeasing. Since God forbids reviling and is angry with it, the wise (ḥakīm) would not will it as ugly. The opponent then might say: Does the wise not will something that, if the one willing were a liar and foolish, would be foolish? If the opponent says yes, he shows ignorance of God's wisdom; if he says no, he must admit that God does will that such acts are not ugly, which would lead to absurdity. So the opponent is trapped.

Key Terms
مشيئة (mashī'a)
حكيم (ḥakīm)
قبيح (qabīḥ)
مسخوط (maskhūṭ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God wills everything, then He wills evil in the same way He wills good.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the proper question is whether God wills the act as evil; He wills the occurrence but not the moral ugliness.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi correct the opponent's question 'Do you intend that God be reviled?'
A) He says the question is irrelevant.
B) He says the proper question is whether God wills that act to be ugly and displeasing.
C) He says God does will reviling.
D) He says the opponent is blasphemous.

Key Takeaway
God's will for events does not imply His approval of their moral quality; the Wise wills only what is good.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something evil happens in your life, do you immediately assume God approves because He willed it? How does today’s lesson challenge you to separate God’s will from His pleasure?
?
You say 'God is Wise'—but do you live as if you trust His wisdom when you face suffering or see injustice? What one concrete step can you take this week to entrust an unexplained trial to His wisdom?
?
Read again: 'the Wise does not will what He hates.' Is there a sin you are holding onto even though God hates it? Will you ask Him to give you the same hatred for it, and repent today?

God Does Not Love Evil Acts
Maturidi refutes the notion that God loves evil by distinguishing will from approval.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the crucial distinction between God's will to create evil and His love or approval of it, and why it is impermissible to say God loves Iblis or vile things.

Core Idea
God's will to create does not imply His love or approval.

In this dense passage, Maturidi continues his response to objections about God's relationship to evil. He has already established that God's prohibition and anger are not like human emotions. Now he directly addresses a major misunderstanding: the confusion between God's will (irāda) and His love (maḥabba) and pleasure (riḍā). Read carefully to see how he defends divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the flow: Maturidi first references earlier discussions, then addresses the issue of love and pleasure.
2
Identify the key distinction
Locate where Maturidi contrasts God's will with His love and pleasure. Note his emphatic statement that it is not permissible to say God loves Iblis or vile things.
The phrase 'لا يجوز أن يقال' is a strong negation.
3
Understand the reasoning behind the distinction
Maturidi argues that God's will to create Iblis and evil acts does not imply His love for them. Why? Because God's will is about power and independence, not moral approval.
Consider the Quranic quote: 'Indeed, God is independent of the worlds.'
4
Reflect on the theological importance
What would be at stake if we said God loves evil? This distinction preserves God's absolute goodness and transcendence. It is a fundamental principle in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن النهى ليس من الوجه الذي ذكرنا وكذلك الغضب، وهذا النوع مما قد ذكرنا منه الكافي في باب خلق الأفعال. وبعد، فإنه إذا أراد لما علم أن يختار هو عداوته أن يكون منه عدوا ليزول معنى الضعف ويظهر الغنى عنه وعن فعله كما قال: إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَغَنِي عَنْ الْعَالَمِينَ، على أنه أن معنى الإرادة أن لا يغلب، وقد وجد في هذا، فليقل ما شاء، فهو له في الأول جواب. وأما جوابه بالمحبة والرضا فإنه لا يجوز أن يقال: إن الله يحب إبليس ويرضى به وكذلك الخبائث والأقذار، وإن كان أراد كونهم فمثله فعل الكفر وكذا كل قبائح الصور والجواهر، والله أعلم.
Given that the prohibition (al-nahy) is not from the aspect we mentioned, and likewise anger (al-ghaḍab), and this type is among what we have already mentioned enough in the chapter on the creation of actions. Then afterwards, when He wills, because He knew that he (Iblis) would choose enmity against Him, that he become an enemy, so that the meaning of weakness disappears and He shows His independence from him and from his action, as He said: 'Indeed, God is independent of the worlds' [Quran 29:6]. Given that the meaning of will (irāda) is that He not be overcome, and this has been found in this, so let him say what he wishes; that is his answer in the first. As for his answer by love (maḥabba) and pleasure (riḍā), it is not permissible to say that God loves Iblis and is pleased with him, and likewise vile things (khabā'ith) and filths (aqdhār). Even if He willed their existence, similarly the act of disbelief (kufr) and likewise all ugly forms (qabā'ih al-ṣuwar) and substances (jawāhir). And God knows best.
Source pages p. 895

Commentary
This passage continues the response regarding God's relation to evil. Maturidi reiterates that prohibition and anger are not to be understood in the same way as human emotions. He refers back to the chapter on creation of actions. Then he explains that God's will (irāda) is such that God is never overcome. When God allowed Iblis's enmity, it was to show God's independence and lack of weakness. As for the objection based on love (maḥabba) and pleasure (riḍā), Maturidi states emphatically that it is impermissible to say that God loves Iblis or is pleased with him, or with vile things and filths. Even if God willed their existence (i.e., created them), that does not mean He loves them. The same applies to disbelief and all ugly forms and substances. God's will to create something does not imply His approval of its moral quality.

Key Terms
محبة (maḥabba)
رضا (riḍā)
إرادة (irāda)
قبيح (qabīḥ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that because God created evil acts, He must love them or approve of them.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that God's will to create something does not entail His love or pleasure in it. God can will the existence of evil for a greater purpose without loving the evil itself.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about God's love for Iblis?
A) God loves Iblis because He created him.
B) It is not permissible to say God loves Iblis or is pleased with him.
C) God loves Iblis but hates his actions.
D) God is pleased with Iblis's existence.

Key Takeaway
God's will to create evil does not imply His love or approval; denying that God loves evil preserves His absolute goodness and transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas in your life where you have attributed evil to God's approval rather than His permissive will? How does this distinction change your relationship with Him?
?
When you face trials or see evil in the world, do you ever doubt God's goodness? How does knowing that He does not love evil, even if He wills it, reshape your trust in His character?
?
If God does not love vile things or disbelief, what does that demand of you in terms of your own choices and attitudes towards sin? Are you content with things God does not love?

God's Power and Will: The Argument from Prevention
Maturidi's argument from prevention to divine will

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical argument that God's failure to prevent something indicates His will, not lack of power.

Core Idea
No prevention means God wills it.

In this lesson, we dive into a sophisticated theological argument: If God could prevent evil but doesn't, does that mean He is powerless? Maturidi turns the tables, showing that the very absence of prevention proves that God wills the evil's existence. This argument connects divine power and will in a way that preserves God's omnipotence while accounting for evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once without analysis, then its literal translation. Notice the conditional structure.
The passage uses a logical conditional: 'if... then...'.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent says: if God can prevent evil but does not, then evil is not prevented. That implies God does not will it. Find this claim in the text.
3
Understand Maturidi's reversal
Maturidi argues: if God had power and did not will the evil, He would have prevented it. Since He didn't, it shows He wills it. Trace this logic in the passage.
This is a classic kalām argument linking will and power.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument preserves God's omnipotence while explaining the existence of evil in the world.
Evil exists because God wills it for His own wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال فعله. ثم قال: إذا قدر على المنع فلم يمنع، فليس بممنوع، فيقال له: لو قدر وهو لا يريده ليمنع، فدل كونه بلا إرادة أنه لم يقدر. ومما يبين ذلك أنه لو قهرهم على الإسلام لم يكونوا مسلمين قهرا، يبين أنه لم يكن يقدر على ذلك، وذلك حق الغلبة والقهر في الشاهد، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he said: His action. Then he said: If He (God) had power to prevent and did not prevent, then it is not prevented. So it is said to him: If He had power while not willing it, He would have prevented it. So His being without will indicates that He did not have power. And among what clarifies that is that if He had compelled them to Islam, they would not have been Muslims under compulsion (qahran). This clarifies that He was not able to do that, and that is the true meaning of overwhelming (ghalaba) and compulsion (qahr) in the witnessed world. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 895

Commentary
This passage presents a key argument about divine power and will. The opponent claimed that since God has power to prevent evil but does not, evil is not prevented and thus God must not will it. Maturidi reverses the logic: if God had power and did not will the evil, He would have prevented it. But since evil occurs without prevention, that shows that God does will it (otherwise He would have prevented it). The absence of prevention indicates that God does not have power in the sense of being able to prevent something He does not will. To illustrate, Maturidi gives the example of compelling people to Islam: if God had compelled them, they would not be genuine Muslims. This shows that God does not have the kind of power that would compel faith against one's will, because that would negate the nature of faith. In the witnessed world, true overcoming (ghalaba) is not compelling against will.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra)
منع (man')
إرادة (irāda)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's failure to prevent evil shows He is powerless or indifferent.
Correction: Maturidi argues it shows His will, not His powerlessness. Power is always united with will.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's argument, why does God not prevent evil?
A) Because He is not powerful enough.
B) Because He wills it.
C) Because He is unaware of it.
D) Because evil is necessary.

Key Takeaway
God's non-prevention of evil is proof of His will, not His powerlessness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness evil or suffering in your life, do you instinctively doubt God's power or question His will? How does Maturidi's argument force you to reconsider your reaction—will you now see each instance as something God has willed for a reason beyond your understanding, and commit to trusting His will even when it hurts?
?
If God's non-prevention of evil is proof that He wills it, then what does that mean for your own struggles? Have you ever tried to escape a trial by assuming God did not will it for you? How can you use this lesson to surrender to His will rather than resist it?
?
The logic of this passage demands that you stop using evil as an excuse to doubt God's power. What specific evil in your life have you blamed on God's inability to prevent? Will you now repent of that assumption and accept that it exists by His will, not His weakness? What practical step will you take today to submit to that will?
