Divine Attributes
intermediate
4 min
Refutation of the Naming (Tasmīya) Argument
Maturidi refutes the claim that attribution is merely nominal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the Qadariyya's claim that misguidance is only attributed to Allah by way of naming, not actual creation.

Core Idea
Naming without real creation would make attribution arbitrary and meaningless.

The Qadariyya proposed a second argument: perhaps misguidance is attributed to Allah only by naming (tasmīya), not by actual creation. Maturidi refutes this on two grounds: first, we do not attribute misguidance to others even though they mislead, so naming alone is insufficient; second, verses about guidance and misguidance occur in contexts of power and authority, not mere naming. This lesson examines that rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Qadariyya's naming argument.
They claimed that when the Quran says 'Allah leads astray,' it is only a name, not a real action.
2
Follow Maturidi's first refutation.
If it were only naming, why don't we attribute misguidance to other agents like Iblis who also mislead?
3
Examine the second refutation from Quranic context.
The verse 'Whomever Allah wills He leads astray...' is in a context of power and authority, not mere naming.
Notice the terms 'wealth' and 'authority' in the Arabic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه رحمه الله : قالت القدرية : فيما أضيف إلى الله الإضلال والإزاغة و صرف القلوب فما قال : صَرَفَ اللهُ قلوبهم ، ونحو ذلك ، إن ذلك كان بالمحنة والتخلية ونحو ذلك ، وفى الخيرات بالأمر والتقوية ونحو ذلك ، ولو كان بالذى قالوا يضاف إليه الإخراج من النور إلى الظلمات كما أضيف إليه الإخراج الظلمات إلى النور عندهم بالأمر والتقوية ، إذ صارت علة الإضافة في الخير إليه الأمر والتقوية ، وذكر الهداية ، وكل ذكر يقابل ما ذكر ؛ إذ الأمر والتقوية هما المحنة وفيها التخلية ، فإذا استقام ذا ، ولم يستقم الآخر بان إن في ذا معنى ليس في الآخر. مع ما زعمت القدرية إن الشرور لا تضاف إليه ؛ لأنه نهي عنها ، فقد نهى عن الضلال والغواية والزيغ ، فليم أضيفت إليه ؟ والله الموفق. وقالوا فى الإضلال بالتسمية ، وذلك فاسد ؛ لما وجد من غيره ولم يضف إليه ، ولما ليس فى التسمية فضل حكمة يذكر فى موضع الوصف بالغنى والسلطان كقوله تعالى : " مَنْ يَشَا اللهُ يُضْلِلْهُ وَمَنْ يَشَأْ يَجْعَلُهُ عَلَى صِرَاطٍ مُسْتَقِيم" ، وذلك في موضع القوة والسلطان ، وبالله نستعين. والأصل في هذا كله عندنا أن الله إذ هو موصوف بفعله ، ومعنى فعله خلقه أولى ، متفضلا فى فعله أو عادلا ، لا يخلو وصف فعله عن هذين ، وحقيقته عن الأول، فصار بأى وجه أضيف إليه من طريق فعله محقق له معنى خلقه ، ولو ذكر ذا فى الإضلال وما ذكر في الطبع وغيره لم يحتمل شيء من تمويهات المعتزلة ، فكذلك إذ ذلك معنى فعله ، والله الموفق.
The jurist, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The Qadariyya said regarding what is attributed to Allah of misguidance, leading astray, and turning of hearts—such as His saying 'Allah turned their hearts'—that that was through trial and abandonment and the like, and regarding good deeds through command and strengthening and the like. And if it were as they claimed, then the bringing out from light into darkness would also be attributed to Him just as the bringing out from darkness into light is attributed to Him by command and strengthening, since the reason for attribution to Him of good is command and strengthening, and mention of guidance, and every mention corresponds to what is mentioned; for command and strengthening are trial, and in it is abandonment. So if this is consistent, and the other is not consistent, it is clear that in this there is a meaning not in the other. Along with the Qadariyya's claim that evils are not attributed to Him because He forbade them, and He forbade misguidance, error, and deviation, so why are they attributed to Him? And Allah is the Grantor of success. And they said regarding misguidance that it is by naming, and that is invalid; because it is found from others and is not attributed to them, and because there is no merit of wisdom in naming to be mentioned in the context of ascription of wealth and authority, as His saying: 'Whomever Allah wills He leads astray, and whomever He wills He sets on a straight path,' and that is in the context of power and authority. And Allah we seek help. The principle in all of this with us is that Allah, when He is described by His action, and the meaning of His action is His creation initially, whether He is gracious in His action or just, the description of His action is not free from these two, and its reality comes from the first. So by whichever aspect it is attributed to Him from the path of His action, it is verified for Him the meaning of His creation. And if this were mentioned regarding misguidance and what is mentioned regarding nature and other things, it would not bear any of the obfuscations of the Mu'tazila. Likewise, since that is the meaning of His action, and Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 912

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Qadariyya (Mu'tazila) argument that misguidance and evil cannot be directly attributed to Allah. The author first presents their claim: they interpret verses like 'Allah turned their hearts' as referring to trial and abandonment, not direct creation, while good things are attributed to Allah by command and strengthening. The refutation proceeds by a parallel argument: if their logic were sound, then bringing out from light to darkness (misguidance) should also be attributed to Allah by the same token, since the reason they gave for good (command and strengthening) is actually a trial that includes abandonment. Thus their reasoning is inconsistent. Then the author addresses their claim that attribution is merely by naming (tasmīya), arguing it is invalid because if it were, we would attribute misguidance to others who do it, and also the verse about guidance is in the context of power and authority. Finally, the author states the foundational principle: Allah is described by His action, and the meaning of His action is His creation – whether gracious or just. Every action attributed to Him is a true creation, which refutes the Mu'tazila obfuscations.

Key Terms
التسمية
الغنى
السلطان
القوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'naming' implies no real action by Allah.
Correction: Maturidi shows that naming would be inconsistent if no real action corresponds, and the Quranic context demands real power.

Checkpoint
What is one of Maturidi's refutations of the naming argument?
A) Naming is not used in the Quran.
B) Misguidance is found from others but not attributed to them.
C) The verse about guidance is metaphorical.
D) Naming implies actual creation.

Key Takeaway
Attribution of misguidance to Allah is not mere naming but reflects His absolute power and authority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your misguidance—every sin and failure—is ultimately willed by Allah? If so, how does that affect your sense of personal responsibility and your reliance on Him for guidance?
?
If the Qadari claim were true and misguidance were only a name, would you feel less fear of Allah? Now that you know it is real, what must change in your heart regarding your view of Allah's power and your own accountability?

The Foundational Principle: Allah's Action is His Creation
Maturidi states the foundational principle that Allah's action is His creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's central principle that Allah's action is defined as His creation, which resolves all attributions of good and evil.

Core Idea
Every act of Allah, whether gracious or just, is a true act of creation.

After refuting the Qadariyya's specific arguments, Maturidi lays down the foundational principle of his theology: Allah is described by His action, and the meaning of His action is His creation. This applies to all attributes, whether of guidance or misguidance. This principle, he argues, cuts through all the obfuscations of the Mu'tazila. This lesson presents that principle and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle: Allah's action = His creation.
Understand that for Maturidi, when we say Allah acts, it means He creates.
2
Notice the two categories: gracious and just.
Allah's actions are either gracious (mutafaddil) or just (ʿādil); both are true creations.
This covers both guidance and misguidance.
3
Understand how this principle verifies attribution.
Every action attributed to Allah from the path of His action is verified as His creation, regardless of whether it is good or evil.
4
See the conclusion: it refutes all obfuscations.
Maturidi states that if this principle were applied, the Mu'tazila's evasions would not stand.
Think about how this principle directly counters trial, abandonment, and naming arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه رحمه الله : قالت القدرية : فيما أضيف إلى الله الإضلال والإزاغة و صرف القلوب فما قال : صَرَفَ اللهُ قلوبهم ، ونحو ذلك ، إن ذلك كان بالمحنة والتخلية ونحو ذلك ، وفى الخيرات بالأمر والتقوية ونحو ذلك ، ولو كان بالذى قالوا يضاف إليه الإخراج من النور إلى الظلمات كما أضيف إليه الإخراج الظلمات إلى النور عندهم بالأمر والتقوية ، إذ صارت علة الإضافة في الخير إليه الأمر والتقوية ، وذكر الهداية ، وكل ذكر يقابل ما ذكر ؛ إذ الأمر والتقوية هما المحنة وفيها التخلية ، فإذا استقام ذا ، ولم يستقم الآخر بان إن في ذا معنى ليس في الآخر. مع ما زعمت القدرية إن الشرور لا تضاف إليه ؛ لأنه نهي عنها ، فقد نهى عن الضلال والغواية والزيغ ، فليم أضيفت إليه ؟ والله الموفق. وقالوا فى الإضلال بالتسمية ، وذلك فاسد ؛ لما وجد من غيره ولم يضف إليه ، ولما ليس فى التسمية فضل حكمة يذكر فى موضع الوصف بالغنى والسلطان كقوله تعالى : " مَنْ يَشَا اللهُ يُضْلِلْهُ وَمَنْ يَشَأْ يَجْعَلُهُ عَلَى صِرَاطٍ مُسْتَقِيم" ، وذلك في موضع القوة والسلطان ، وبالله نستعين. والأصل في هذا كله عندنا أن الله إذ هو موصوف بفعله ، ومعنى فعله خلقه أولى ، متفضلا فى فعله أو عادلا ، لا يخلو وصف فعله عن هذين ، وحقيقته عن الأول، فصار بأى وجه أضيف إليه من طريق فعله محقق له معنى خلقه ، ولو ذكر ذا فى الإضلال وما ذكر في الطبع وغيره لم يحتمل شيء من تمويهات المعتزلة ، فكذلك إذ ذلك معنى فعله ، والله الموفق.
The jurist, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The Qadariyya said regarding what is attributed to Allah of misguidance, leading astray, and turning of hearts—such as His saying 'Allah turned their hearts'—that that was through trial and abandonment and the like, and regarding good deeds through command and strengthening and the like. And Allah is the Grantor of success. According to Maturidi, what is the meaning of Allah's action?
A) His command.

?

?

?
The Mu'tazila created obfuscations to avoid attributing certain acts directly to Allah. Are there areas in your life where you try to soften or explain away Allah's direct involvement? What are they, and how will you realign your heart with this truth starting today?

Divine Will: Between Sovereignty and Creation
Maturidi presents views on divine will from al-Husayn, al-Ka'bi, and the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the debate over divine will among early theologians centered on whether it means God's lack of compulsion or an eternal will that creates acts.

Core Idea
Divine will is either God's sovereignty or an eternal attribute of creation.

Maturidi begins by reporting the definitions of divine will given by al-Husayn and al-Ka'bi. Both define it negatively as God not being overcome. But al-Husayn extends it to the creation of acts, while the Mu'tazila deny that God creates human acts. This lesson unpacks these views and reveals that the true issue is not will itself but the creation of acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of will from al-Husayn and al-Ka'bi
Identify the core meaning each gives: al-Husayn says 'not overcome' and al-Ka'bi says 'free (mukhtar) and not overcome'.
Notice both define will via negation of subjugation.
2
Observe how al-Husayn connects will to creation of acts
He argues that since acts are created by God, God willed them as He created them. The Mu'tazila deny creation of acts, shifting the issue.
This shows the debate is about creation, not will per se.
3
Reflect on the Mu'tazilite position as reported by Maturidi
They do not affirm an eternal will; they only say God became knowing after not knowing, making Him creator.
This contrasts with al-Husayn's eternal will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال حسين : معنى الإرادة فى الله سبحانه أنه لم يغلب ولم يقهر، وقد أعطت المعتزلة هذا المعنى فى جميع أفعال الخلق، إنه لم يغلب ولم يقهر، فتبطل المسألة فى الإرادة، إنما بقيت في تأويل الإرادة لا غير. الحسين : إن أفعال العباد مخلوقة، فأراد خالقها كونها على ما خلقها، ومذهب المعتزلة إنها ليست بمخلوقة لله، فتكون المسألة فى خلق الأفعال لا فى الإرادة. وقال الكعبى : الارادة معناها أنه مختار غير مغلوب، فمثله في كل شيء يلزمه، ثم المعتزلة ليست تثبت لله إلى العالم سوى أن كان، ولم يكن عالم، ثم كان عالما، فصار بذلك المعنى خالقا له، مريدا على الوجه الذي ذكر. فقال حسين في أفعال العباد : إنه إذ كان ولا هذه الأفعال، ثم كانت هذه، وكانت بإرادته التي تأويلها ما وصفه وبأن خلقها بأن كان ولم تكن هى، ولا قوة إلا بالله. على أن حسينا يجعله فى الأول مريدا لكون الخلق على ما كان، وكذلك لكون كل مخلوق على ما كان بإرادته، والمعتزلة تنفى معنى الإرادة، لا تجعل غير أن كان، ولم يكن الخلق، ثم كان، فحق ذلك فيه أوجب، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقالت المعتزلة الوعيد يأخذ من أخرجه فعله عن الإيمان، وكذلك قال حسين وجميع أهل الإرجاء إن كان من استحق بفعله زوال اسم الإيمان فهو كله في النار أبدا، ولا قوة إلا بالله. والاختلاف بين هؤلاء في ما به يخرج من الإيمان لا فى حق الوعيد، فالاحتجاج بأى الوعيد في المسألة خطأ.
Al-Husayn said: 'The meaning of will (al-irada) in Allah, exalted is He, is that He was not overcome nor subdued.' The Mu'tazila also gave this meaning for all acts of creation: that He was not overcome nor subdued. So the issue about will becomes invalid; only the interpretation of will remains, nothing else. Al-Husayn said: 'The acts of servants are created, so their Creator willed them to be as He created them.' The doctrine of the Mu'tazila is that they are not created by Allah, so the issue is about the creation of acts, not about will. Al-Ka'bi said: 'Will means that He is free (mukhtar) and not overcome.' The same applies to everything that follows from Him. Then the Mu'tazila do not affirm for Allah with respect to the world anything except that He was not, and then He was knowing, then He became knowing, so by that meaning He became its Creator, willing in the manner mentioned. So al-Husayn said concerning the acts of servants: 'He was when there were not these acts, then these acts came to be, and they came to be by His will, the interpretation of which is what He described, and by His creating them because He was and they were not.' There is no power except with Allah. Moreover, al-Husayn makes Him in the beginning willing for creation to be as it was, and likewise for every created thing to be as it was by His will. The Mu'tazila deny the meaning of will; they do not posit anything other than that He was not and creation was not, then it came to be. So that is more incumbent in that matter. And there is no power except with Allah. The Mu'tazila said: 'The threat (al-wa'id) applies to whoever is taken out of faith by his act.' Likewise al-Husayn and all the Murji'a said: 'If someone deserves by his act the removal of the name of faith, then he is entirely in the Fire forever.' There is no power except with Allah. The disagreement between these groups is about that by which one exits faith, not about the reality of the threat. So arguing based on any threat in this issue is a mistake.
Source pages p. 921

Commentary
This passage is a sustained discussion of divine will and its relation to human acts, followed by an analysis of the threat of punishment. Maturidi first reports the views of al-Husayn and al-Ka'bi. Al-Husayn defines divine will negatively as God not being overcome or subdued, and the Mu'tazila agree on that negative definition. Maturidi notes that this agreement renders the dispute over will purely verbal. Al-Husayn further states that human acts are created by God, and God wills them to be as He creates them. The Mu'tazila deny that acts are created by God, shifting the issue to the creation of acts rather than will. Al-Ka'bi defines will as God being free (mukhtar) and not overcome. Maturidi then explains the Mu'tazilite position: they affirm a minimal temporal change in God (He was not knowing, then became knowing) to account for creation. Al-Husayn counters that God existed before acts and they came to be by His will and creation. Maturidi then summarizes: al-Husayn affirms that God eternally willed creation to be as it is, while the Mu'tazila deny any eternal will and only posit temporal origination. Maturidi considers the Mu'tazilite position to be more 'incumbent' (awjab) in some sense, perhaps acknowledging its internal logic. Then the discussion turns to the threat of punishment. Both the Mu'tazila and al-Husayn (and the Murji'a) agree that one who deserves removal of the name of faith will be in Hell forever. Maturidi clarifies that the real disagreement is about what causes one to exit faith (e.g., major sins vs. denying essentials), not about the threat itself. Therefore, citing the threat (wa'id) as proof for a specific position on the status of grave sinners is a mistake, because both sides accept the threat for those who genuinely exit faith.

Key Terms
الإرادة
خلق الأفعال
المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The debate over divine will is about the meaning of the word 'will' itself.
Correction: Al-Husayn shows that if both sides accept the negative definition, the real dispute becomes whether God creates human acts, not the definition of will.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what do al-Husayn and the Mu'tazila agree on regarding the meaning of divine will?
A) That will means God creates all acts directly.
B) That will means God is not overcome or subdued.
C) That will is an eternal attribute distinct from creation.
D) That will is identical to God's knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The early debate on divine will ultimately hinges on whether God creates human acts, not on a shared negative definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the fact that every one of your acts is either created by God or not, how does that change the way you think about your own choices and responsibilities today? Are you willing to accept full divine sovereignty over your actions, or do you feel a tension with your own sense of freedom?
?
This passage shows that even early scholars struggled to define God's will without reducing it. In what areas of your life do you find yourself trying to define God's will in your own terms, rather than submitting to His absolute sovereignty? What would it cost you to let go of that definition?
?
If the real issue behind debates on divine will is whether God creates human acts, then your deeds are either God's creation or your own independent products. Do you live as if your good deeds are a gift from God or as if they are solely your achievement? How would your humility before God change if you truly believed He creates even your ability to obey Him?

The Threat of Punishment: Where the Real Disagreement Lies
Maturidi discusses the threat of punishment and clarifies the real point of disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the controversy over the threat of Hellfire is actually a dispute about what removes a person from faith, not about the threat itself.

Core Idea
Everyone agrees the threat is real—the question is who deserves it.

Maturidi turns to the threat of punishment. Both the Mu'tazila and al-Husayn and the Murji'a agree that those who lose the name of faith are in Hell forever. However, Maturidi points out that the real disagreement is about what causes one to exit faith—major sins or denial of essentials. Citing the threat as evidence for a specific position is therefore a mistake.

Guided Path
1
Read the agreement on the threat
Both groups agree that whoever deserves removal of faith is in Hell forever. Notice the phrase 'كل من استحق بفعله زوال اسم الإيمان فهو كله في النار أبدا'.
This shows consensus on the consequence.
2
Identify Maturidi's clarification
He states that the disagreement is about 'ما به يخرج من الإيمان' (what causes one to exit faith), not about the threat itself.
This is the key analytical point.
3
Understand the methodological mistake
Arguing based on the threat alone is flawed because both sides accept it; the real dispute lies elsewhere.
Maturidi explicitly says 'الاحتجاج بأى الوعيد في المسألة خطأ'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال حسين : معنى الإرادة فى الله سبحانه أنه لم يغلب ولم يقهر، وقد أعطت المعتزلة هذا المعنى فى جميع أفعال الخلق، إنه لم يغلب ولم يقهر، فتبطل المسألة فى الإرادة، إنما بقيت في تأويل الإرادة لا غير. الحسين : إن أفعال العباد مخلوقة، فأراد خالقها كونها على ما خلقها، ومذهب المعتزلة إنها ليست بمخلوقة لله، فتكون المسألة فى خلق الأفعال لا فى الإرادة. وقال الكعبى : الارادة معناها أنه مختار غير مغلوب، فمثله في كل شيء يلزمه، ثم المعتزلة ليست تثبت لله إلى العالم سوى أن كان، ولم يكن عالم، ثم كان عالما، فصار بذلك المعنى خالقا له، مريدا على الوجه الذي ذكر. فقال حسين في أفعال العباد : إنه إذ كان ولا هذه الأفعال، ثم كانت هذه، وكانت بإرادته التي تأويلها ما وصفه وبأن خلقها بأن كان ولم تكن هى، ولا قوة إلا بالله. على أن حسينا يجعله فى الأول مريدا لكون الخلق على ما كان، وكذلك لكون كل مخلوق على ما كان بإرادته، والمعتزلة تنفى معنى الإرادة، لا تجعل غير أن كان، ولم يكن الخلق، ثم كان، فحق ذلك فيه أوجب، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقالت المعتزلة الوعيد يأخذ من أخرجه فعله عن الإيمان، وكذلك قال حسين وجميع أهل الإرجاء إن كان من استحق بفعله زوال اسم الإيمان فهو كله في النار أبدا، ولا قوة إلا بالله. والاختلاف بين هؤلاء في ما به يخرج من الإيمان لا فى حق الوعيد، فالاحتجاج بأى الوعيد في المسألة خطأ.
Al-Husayn said: 'The meaning of will (al-irada) in Allah, exalted is He, is that He was not overcome nor subdued.' The Mu'tazila also gave this meaning for all acts of creation: that He was not overcome nor subdued. So the issue about will becomes invalid; only the interpretation of will remains, nothing else. Al-Husayn said: 'The acts of servants are created, so their Creator willed them to be as He created them.' The doctrine of the Mu'tazila is that they are not created by Allah, so the issue is about the creation of acts, not about will. Al-Ka'bi said: 'Will means that He is free (mukhtar) and not overcome.' The same applies to everything that follows from Him. Then the Mu'tazila do not affirm for Allah with respect to the world anything except that He was not, and then He was knowing, then He became knowing, so by that meaning He became its Creator, willing in the manner mentioned. So al-Husayn said concerning the acts of servants: 'He was when there were not these acts, then these acts came to be, and they came to be by His will, the interpretation of which is what He described, and by His creating them because He was and they were not.' There is no power except with Allah. Moreover, al-Husayn makes Him in the beginning willing for creation to be as it was, and likewise for every created thing to be as it was by His will. The Mu'tazila deny the meaning of will; they do not posit anything other than that He was not and creation was not, then it came to be. So that is more incumbent in that matter. And there is no power except with Allah. The Mu'tazila said: 'The threat (al-wa'id) applies to whoever is taken out of faith by his act.' Likewise al-Husayn and all the Murji'a said: 'If someone deserves by his act the removal of the name of faith, then he is entirely in the Fire forever.' There is no power except with Allah. The disagreement between these groups is about that by which one exits faith, not about the reality of the threat. So arguing based on any threat in this issue is a mistake.
Source pages p. 921

Commentary
This passage is a sustained discussion of divine will and its relation to human acts, followed by an analysis of the threat of punishment. Maturidi first reports the views of al-Husayn and al-Ka'bi. Al-Husayn defines divine will negatively as God not being overcome or subdued, and the Mu'tazila agree on that negative definition. Maturidi notes that this agreement renders the dispute over will purely verbal. Al-Husayn further states that human acts are created by God, and God wills them to be as He creates them. The Mu'tazila deny that acts are created by God, shifting the issue to the creation of acts rather than will. Al-Ka'bi defines will as God being free (mukhtar) and not overcome. Maturidi then explains the Mu'tazilite position: they affirm a minimal temporal change in God (He was not knowing, then became knowing) to account for creation. Al-Husayn counters that God existed before acts and they came to be by His will and creation. Maturidi then summarizes: al-Husayn affirms that God eternally willed creation to be as it is, while the Mu'tazila deny any eternal will and only posit temporal origination. Maturidi considers the Mu'tazilite position to be more 'incumbent' (awjab) in some sense, perhaps acknowledging its internal logic. Then the discussion turns to the threat of punishment. Both the Mu'tazila and al-Husayn (and the Murji'a) agree that one who deserves removal of the name of faith will be in Hell forever. Maturidi clarifies that the real disagreement is about what causes one to exit faith (e.g., major sins vs. denying essentials), not about the threat itself. Therefore, citing the threat (wa'id) as proof for a specific position on the status of grave sinners is a mistake, because both sides accept the threat for those who genuinely exit faith.

Key Terms
الوعيد
الإيمان
الإرجاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila and Murji'a disagree on whether grave sinners will be punished eternally.
Correction: Maturidi shows that both groups agree on eternal punishment for those who lose the name of faith; they differ on what causes the loss.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the real point of disagreement among the Mu'tazila, al-Husayn, and the Murji'a regarding the threat (al-wa'id)?
A) Whether the threat of Hell applies to anyone who commits a sin.
B) Whether Allah's will is eternal or temporal.
C) What specific act or condition causes a person to exit faith.
D) Whether the threat is conditional or unconditional.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that the threat of Hell is not the real point of contention; what matters is the definition of what causes someone to exit faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate your own sins, do you secretly believe that a single major sin might completely expel you from faith, or do you assume your general faith protects you regardless? How does Maturidi's clarification that the threat is real but the cause of exit is defined differently challenge your self-assessment today? Will you examine what you truly believe causes someone to leave faith?
?
Think of a recent religious disagreement you had. Was the real point of contention about consequences or about the underlying cause? How often do you argue about outcomes when you actually differ on criteria? This passage calls you to refocus the debate. What will you do next time to identify the real issue first?
?
If the threat of Hell is not the point of disagreement between these groups, then what is? And more personally, have you ever used fear of Hell to coerce others into agreeing with you on theological points, while ignoring the deeper definitional issues? How can you shift your own heart to prioritize truth over winning arguments?

Al-Ka'bi's Definition of Will and Mu'tazila's Rejection
Al-Ka'bi defines divine will and contrasts with Mu'tazila on creation by knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ka'bi's definition of divine will as the attribute of free choice and how the Mu'tazila denied a distinct attribute of will, reducing it to knowledge.

Core Idea
Divine will is God's free choice, not mere knowledge.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi reports two competing views on the nature of divine will. Al-Ka'bi defines will as the attribute by which God is a free chooser, not compelled. The Mu'tazila, however, reject this, claiming that God's will is not a separate attribute but a consequence of His knowledge. Understanding this debate is foundational for Sunni theology on the attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Identify the two main positions discussed.
2
Analyze al-Ka'bi's definition
Notice how al-Ka'bi defines will as 'mukhtar ghayr maghlub' – a chooser not overcome. This links will to freedom.
3
Contrast with Mu'tazila
See how the Mu'tazila deny a distinct will, instead saying God becomes willing through knowledge. What is the implication for God's attributes?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الكعبى : الارادة معناها أنه مختار غير مغلوب ، فمثله في كل شيء يلزمه ، ثم المعتزلة ليست تثبت لله إلى العالم سوى أن كان ، ولم يكن عالم ، ثم كان عالما ، فصار بذلك المعنى خالقا له ، مريدا على الوجه الذي ذكر . فقال حسين في أفعال العباد : إنه إذ كان ولا هذه الأفعال ، ثم كانت هذه ، وكانت بإرادته التي تأويلها ما وصفه وبأن خلقها بأن كان ولم تكن هى ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . على أن حسينا يجعله فى الأول مريدا لكون الخلق على ما كان ، وكذلك لكون كل مخلوق على ما كان بإرادته ، والمعتزلة تنفى معنى الإرادة ، لا تجعل غير أن كان ، ولم يكن الخلق ، ثم كان ، فحق ذلك فيه أوجب ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Al-Ka'bi said: The meaning of will (al-irada) is that He is a chooser (mukhtar) not overcome (maghlub), so the like of it in everything follows from Him. Then the Mu'tazila do not affirm for God except that He (the world) was not and He was not knowing, then He became knowing, so He became by that meaning a creator of it, willing in the manner mentioned. Husayn said concerning the actions of servants: That when He existed while these actions were not, then these actions existed, and they existed by His will whose interpretation is what He described, and by His creating them in that He existed while they were not, and there is no power except by God. And Husayn makes Him in the beginning willing for the existence of creation as it was, and likewise for the existence of every creature as it was by His will. And the Mu'tazila deny the meaning of will; they do not affirm except that He existed and creation was not, then it existed, so that right is more incumbent in Him. And there is no power except by God.
Source pages p. 922

Commentary
This passage presents two positions: al-Ka'bi's definition of divine will and the Mu'tazila's alternative view. Al-Ka'bi defines will (al-irada) as the attribute that makes God a free chooser (mukhtar) who is not overcome or compelled (ghayr maghlub). This meaning, he argues, necessarily applies to everything God does. The Mu'tazila, on the other hand, do not affirm a distinct attribute of will for God; they reduce it to a consequence of God's knowledge. They hold that before creation, God was not yet knowing the world, and once He became knowing, that very knowledge made Him a creator and willing. Husayn (likely al-Najjar or a similar figure) offers a different view on human actions: they exist by God's will, interpreted as His creative power, such that He existed before they existed, and they came to be by His will. Husayn affirms that God was always willing for creation to be as it is, and for each creature to be as it is by His will. The Mu'tazila, in contrast, deny any distinct meaning of will; they only assert that God existed while creation was not, then creation came to be, and that right (i.e., the fact of becoming creator) is more necessarily attributed to Him. The passage ends with the refrain 'there is no power except by God.'

Key Terms
الإرادة
المختار
المغلوب
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Ka'bi denies a real attribute of will.
Correction: Al-Ka'bi actually affirms will as a distinct attribute denoting freedom, not mere knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ka'bi, what does divine will (al-irada) mean?
A) God is a chooser not overcome
B) God's knowledge of creation
C) God's power to create
D) God's decree

Key Takeaway
Al-Ka'bi affirms divine will as a distinct attribute of free choice, while the Mu'tazila reduce it to a function of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will is a distinct attribute of free choice, then every decision you make exists because He willed it. How does this confront your feeling of autonomy? Write one decision today where you must consciously submit your will to His.
?
The Mu'tazila reduce will to knowledge—making God a passive observer of what He knows. Is there an area in your life where you treat God's knowledge as mere information without letting His will transform your actions? What would it mean to let His will interrupt your plans?
?
Al-Ka'bi says God is a free chooser not overcome. In what area of your life do you act as if God's will can be overcome by your circumstances? What would it mean to trust that His will prevails even when you feel stuck?

The Verse on Shirk and Divine Forgiveness
Maturidi refutes those who misuse the verse about shirk to deny forgiveness for major sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the verse 'Indeed, God does not forgive shirk' to argue that forgiveness for major sins is possible without repentance.

Core Idea
Even shirk can be forgiven; denying forgiveness for lesser sins is foolish.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi tackles a common misinterpretation of the Quranic verse that seems to limit forgiveness only to those who avoid shirk. He shows that the verse actually proves the opposite: forgiveness is up to God's will, not our limitations. This lesson unpacks his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the overall flow.
2
Identify the two refuted positions
The passage refutes two views: those who restrict the verse's application and those who deny forgiveness for major sins without repentance.
3
Understand the logical pivot
Note that shirk itself can be forgiven through repentance; this undermines both positions.
Repentance shows forgiveness is not absolutely denied.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that God has willed forgiveness, and rejecting that is foolish.
This upholds divine mercy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الله تعالى قال : إِنَّ اللهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَن يُشْرَكَ بِهِ ومعلوم أن الشرك قد يغفر بالتوبة ، فبطل به قول من يجعله لما قسم الكتاب ، وبطل قول من يبطل المغفرة فى الكبائر بلا توبة ؛ لأن الله جل ثناؤه جعل لنفسه مشيئة المغفرة ، وذلك فما كان فى الحكمة ، لكن دفعه سفه جل الله عن ذلك وتعالى ، ذكرت القولين جميعا .
Then God Almighty said: 'Indeed, God does not forgive that partners be associated with Him.' And it is known that shirk may be forgiven through repentance. So it invalidates by this the saying of those who make it for what the Book made clear, and it invalidates the saying of those who nullify forgiveness for major sins without repentance, because God, great and exalted, placed for Himself the will of forgiveness. And that is what was in wisdom, but its rejection is foolishness - God is exalted above that and high. I have mentioned both statements.
Source pages p. 926

Commentary
This passage refutes two positions using the verse about forgiveness of shirk. The fact that shirk itself can be forgiven through repentance shows that forgiveness is not restricted only to minor sins or only with repentance. Maturidi argues that God has willed forgiveness, and to deny that is foolish. He mentions both the view that limits the verse and the view that denies forgiveness for major sins without repentance.

Key Terms
شرك
مغفرة
توبة
مشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse means shirk is absolutely unforgivable, so other sins are easier to forgive.
Correction: Maturidi argues that if shirk can be forgiven through repentance, then other sins can also be forgiven by God's will even without repentance.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi argue using the verse about not forgiving shirk?
A) That shirk is never forgiven
B) That only shirk requires repentance
C) That shirk can be forgiven, so other sins can be forgiven by God's will
D) That all sins are forgiven automatically

Key Takeaway
The verse about not forgiving shirk actually supports the possibility of forgiveness for major sins through God's will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God can forgive your greatest sins, or do you secretly think some are too big for His mercy? What would it mean for your relationship with Him if you fully trusted His will to forgive?
?
When you sin, do you rely more on your own repentance or on God's boundless will to forgive? How does this lesson challenge you to shift your reliance?
?
Have you ever fallen into the foolishness of limiting God's forgiveness—either by thinking He only forgives certain sins or that repentance is a rigid requirement? What step will you take today to repent and trust His will completely?

The Like of Disbelief: Eternal Punishment
Maturidi argues that eternal punishment is the like of disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that eternal punishment is the proportionate 'like' for the sin of disbelief and obstinacy, and why punishing a lesser sin with the same could be unjust.

Core Idea
For the obstinate disbeliever, the appropriate 'like' is eternal punishment.

Maturidi continues his refutation of the Mu'tazila by examining the third aspect: the verse does not specify the measure of punishment for major sins. He first focuses on the sin of shirk and obstinacy, arguing that its recompense is eternal punishment based on the principle of 'like for like' from Quran 6:160. He then warns against applying this measure to lesser sins, as it would exceed justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where Maturidi discusses the lack of specified punishment for major sins and connects it to Quran 6:160.
Notice how he establishes that the 'like' of the disbeliever's sin is eternal.
2
Understand the argument for eternal punishment
Maturidi argues that if given a choice, the obstinate disbeliever would choose eternal punishment over temporary punishment with safety. This reveals the magnitude of his sin.
3
Examine the implication for lesser sins
If a lesser sin were punished with the same eternal punishment, it would be excessive and unjust. This supports Maturidi's refutation of the Mu'tazila claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث أنه لم يبين في الكبائر قدر العقوبات ، ومثل الشرك والمعاندة إنما هو معلوم أن الله نفى أن يجزى من ليس بخلد ، ولا ريب أن الله قد قال: (مَنْ جَاءَ بِالسَّيِّئَةِ فَلَا يُجْزَى إِلَّا مِثْلَهَا) ، ومعلوم أن الكافر المعاند لو جمع ما يعاقب به على شرط النجاة والراحة يحتمل ويختاره ، فثبت أن مثل سيئته هو الخلود في العذاب، فإذا عذب بمثله من كان ذنبه دونه كان جازيا أكثر مما يرتكب ، وفي الحكمة عقوبته ، وهذا مما يمنع من مثله . والثاني إنه معلوم أن الذي يقابل الجحود والمعاندة من الخير أعظم وأجل من الذي يقابل ما كان في قبوله أن يفعل من الترك على نحو ما كان الجحود والمعاندة ، فجاء بالذي هو في الخير أعظم الخير ، وفي الشر لم يبلغ نهايته ، فإذا خلده في النار أبطل ثواب أفضل الخيرات بارتكاب ما دونه من الشر ، ورد ذلك وصف الجود لا العدل، والعدل أن يزيد في ثواب ما جاء به على عقاب ما أتى به ، والله جل ثناؤه قد أخبر أنه يجزى الحسنة بعشرة أمثالها ، والسيئة بمثلها ، وفي هذا لم يبلغ المثل في الحسنة ولا قصر على المثل في السيئة ، جل الله عن ذلك وتعالى .
And the third: that He did not clarify in the major sins the measure of the punishments. And the like of shirk and obstinacy is only known that Allah negated that He recompenses one who is not eternally [punished] — and there is no doubt that Allah said: 'Whoever comes with a sin will not be recompensed except with its like.' And it is known that the obstinate disbeliever, if all punishment were gathered on condition of safety and comfort, he would accept and choose it. So it is established that the like of his sin is eternal punishment. So if one whose sin is lesser is punished with its like, then He would be recompensing more than what he commits, and in wisdom his punishment [would be greater]. And this prevents [the claim] of its like. And the second: it is known that what corresponds to denial and obstinacy from good is greater and more sublime than what corresponds to what is in its acceptance of being done from abandonment, in the manner of denial and obstinacy. So He brought what is in good the greatest good, and in evil He did not reach its extremity. So if He eternally punishes him in the Fire, He nullifies the reward of the best good deeds by committing lesser evil. And that would be a description of generosity, not justice. And justice is that He increases in reward for what one brings over punishment for what one commits. And Allah, exalted be His mention, has informed that He recompenses the good deed with ten times its like, and the evil deed with its like. And in this He did not reach the like in the good deed nor fall short of the like in the evil deed. Exalted is Allah above that and high.
Source pages p. 938

Commentary
This passage is a proof and argument passage. Maturidi presents the third aspect that refutes the Mu'tazila. He states that the verse (4:31) does not specify the measure of punishment for major sins. Referring to the obstinacy of the disbeliever, he connects it to the verse 'Whoever comes with a sin will not be recompensed except with its like' (6:160). He argues that the like of the disbeliever's sin is eternal punishment, because he would choose that over a temporary punishment if given the choice. Then he points out that if someone with a lesser sin is punished similarly, it would be unfair. He then gives a second point within this aspect: that the good corresponding to disbelief and obstinacy is much greater than the good for merely leaving an act. Thus, if Allah eternally punishes the disbeliever, it would not nullify the reward for the greatest good deeds because the disbeliever did not do those good deeds. Rather, justice requires that reward outweighs punishment, as Allah has revealed: ten times for good, one like for evil. In this [verse], the like for good is exceeded (ten times) and the like for evil is not reduced. This refutes the idea that the disbeliever's punishment is unjust.

Key Terms
الكبائر
المثل
الخلود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that eternal punishment for disbelievers violates the Quranic principle of recompense like for like.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the 'like' of disbelief is precisely eternal punishment, because the disbeliever's obstinacy is so great that he would choose eternal punishment over temporary punishment with safety.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi argue is the 'like' of the disbeliever's sin according to Quran 6:160?
A) Temporary punishment
B) Eternal punishment
C) Forgiveness
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The disbeliever's sin of obstinacy is so grave that its just recompense is eternal punishment, not a lesser penalty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were faced with the choice between temporary punishment with safety and comfort versus eternal punishment, what would your choice reveal about your true allegiance? The disbeliever would choose the eternal—what does that say about where your heart truly lies?
?
Consider a sin you have repeatedly committed: do you expect it to be punished with something 'like' it, or do you assume mercy without accountability? How does Maturidi's argument about proportionality challenge your attitude toward repentance and divine justice?
?
When you hear of people who reject faith, do you secretly think their punishment is 'too harsh'? This lesson argues it is exactly the 'like' of their sin. Does your discomfort with eternal punishment stem from a deeper denial of the gravity of disbelief in your own heart?

Justice Requires Reward to Exceed Punishment
Maturidi argues justice requires reward to outweigh punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's second argument: that justice demands reward outweighs punishment, as seen in the Quran multiplying good deeds tenfold while evil deeds receive only their like, and how this refutes the Mu'tazila claim about eternal punishment.

Core Idea
Justice requires that good deeds are rewarded more than evil deeds are punished.

Maturidi presents a second point within the third aspect: comparing the recompense for good and evil. He argues that the good corresponding to disbelief is far greater than the good for merely leaving an act, so eternal punishment does not nullify the reward for the greatest good deeds. Using Quranic verses on multiplication of rewards, he shows that justice is about reward exceeding punishment, not equality.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read from 'والثاني' to the end, where Maturidi discusses the comparison between good and evil recompense.
Notice the contrast between 'الجود' (generosity) and 'العدل' (justice).
2
Understand the comparison
Maturidi says the good that corresponds to disbelief is greater than the good for leaving an action. So the disbeliever, who did not do that greater good, does not deserve its reward. Eternal punishment does not nullify that reward.
3
Learn the Quranic principle
Allah has decreed ten rewards for a good deed and one punishment for an evil deed. This shows that justice means reward exceeds punishment.
4
Apply to the Mu'tazila claim
Since the disbeliever receives only the like of his evil (eternal punishment), but the believer would receive multiplied reward, there is no injustice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث أنه لم يبين في الكبائر قدر العقوبات ، ومثل الشرك والمعاندة إنما هو معلوم أن الله نفى أن يجزى من ليس بخلد ، ولا ريب أن الله قد قال: (مَنْ جَاءَ بِالسَّيِّئَةِ فَلَا يُجْزَى إِلَّا مِثْلَهَا) ، ومعلوم أن الكافر المعاند لو جمع ما يعاقب به على شرط النجاة والراحة يحتمل ويختاره ، فثبت أن مثل سيئته هو الخلود في العذاب، فإذا عذب بمثله من كان ذنبه دونه كان جازيا أكثر مما يرتكب ، وفي الحكمة عقوبته ، وهذا مما يمنع من مثله . والثاني إنه معلوم أن الذي يقابل الجحود والمعاندة من الخير أعظم وأجل من الذي يقابل ما كان في قبوله أن يفعل من الترك على نحو ما كان الجحود والمعاندة ، فجاء بالذي هو في الخير أعظم الخير ، وفي الشر لم يبلغ نهايته ، فإذا خلده في النار أبطل ثواب أفضل الخيرات بارتكاب ما دونه من الشر ، ورد ذلك وصف الجود لا العدل، والعدل أن يزيد في ثواب ما جاء به على عقاب ما أتى به ، والله جل ثناؤه قد أخبر أنه يجزى الحسنة بعشرة أمثالها ، والسيئة بمثلها ، وفي هذا لم يبلغ المثل في الحسنة ولا قصر على المثل في السيئة ، جل الله عن ذلك وتعالى .
And the third: that He did not clarify in the major sins the measure of the punishments. And the like of shirk and obstinacy is only known that Allah negated that He recompenses one who is not eternally [punished] — and there is no doubt that Allah said: 'Whoever comes with a sin will not be recompensed except with its like.' And it is known that the obstinate disbeliever, if all punishment were gathered on condition of safety and comfort, he would accept and choose it. So it is established that the like of his sin is eternal punishment. So if one whose sin is lesser is punished with its like, then He would be recompensing more than what he commits, and in wisdom his punishment [would be greater]. And this prevents [the claim] of its like. And the second: it is known that what corresponds to denial and obstinacy from good is greater and more sublime than what corresponds to what is in its acceptance of being done from abandonment, in the manner of denial and obstinacy. So He brought what is in good the greatest good, and in evil He did not reach its extremity. So if He eternally punishes him in the Fire, He nullifies the reward of the best good deeds by committing lesser evil. And that would be a description of generosity, not justice. And justice is that He increases in reward for what one brings over punishment for what one commits. And Allah, exalted be His mention, has informed that He recompenses the good deed with ten times its like, and the evil deed with its like. And in this He did not reach the like in the good deed nor fall short of the like in the evil deed. Exalted is Allah above that and high.
Source pages p. 938

Commentary
This passage is a proof and argument passage. Maturidi presents the third aspect that refutes the Mu'tazila. He states that the verse (4:31) does not specify the measure of punishment for major sins. Referring to the obstinacy of the disbeliever, he connects it to the verse 'Whoever comes with a sin will not be recompensed except with its like' (6:160). He argues that the like of the disbeliever's sin is eternal punishment, because he would choose that over a temporary punishment if given the choice. Then he points out that if someone with a lesser sin is punished similarly, it would be unfair. He then gives a second point within this aspect: that the good corresponding to disbelief and obstinacy is much greater than the good for merely leaving an act. Thus, if Allah eternally punishes the disbeliever, it would not nullify the reward for the greatest good deeds because the disbeliever did not do those good deeds. Rather, justice requires that reward outweighs punishment, as Allah has revealed: ten times for good, one like for evil. In this [verse], the like for good is exceeded (ten times) and the like for evil is not reduced. This refutes the idea that the disbeliever's punishment is unjust.

Key Terms
العدل
الجود
الحسنة
السيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that eternal punishment for disbelievers is excessive and violates justice.
Correction: Maturidi shows that justice requires reward to exceed punishment, and the Quranic principle of tenfold reward for good deeds and onefold for evil deeds confirms this. Eternal punishment is the like of disbelief, not excessive.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does justice require regarding reward and punishment?
A) Reward equals punishment
B) Reward exceeds punishment
C) Punishment exceeds reward
D) Both are equal

Key Takeaway
Justice means Allah rewards good deeds more than He punishes evil deeds, as seen in the Quranic multiplication of rewards.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly believe that your one good deed is worth ten times that sin? Does this truth change how you view your mistakes and your hope in Allah's mercy?
?
The good of faith is described as the greatest good. Is your faith precious enough to you that losing it would be worse than any punishment? How are you guarding your faith today?
?
Maturidi argues that justice means reward outweighs punishment. Are you living as if your good deeds matter more than your sins? What one good deed can you do right now to tip the scale?

Justice in Reward and Punishment: Proportionality and Generosity
Maturidi contrasts good opposing disbelief with lesser good, affirms justice as proportional reward.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God's justice requires reward to exceed punishment, and how this is supported by the Quranic principle of multiplying good deeds.

Core Idea
God’s justice multiplies reward and matches punishment, not vice versa.

This passage from Maturidi’s Kitab al-Tawhid presents the second point on divine justice: how God deals with those who commit the greatest evil while also having some good. It contrasts the magnitude of good opposing disbelief with that opposing omission, and concludes that true justice requires reward to outweigh punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Focus on the flow of Maturidi's argument without analyzing deeply yet.
Notice the term 'الجحود والمعاندة' (denial and stubbornness) as the greatest evil.
2
Identify the two types of good and evil
The passage contrasts the good that opposes denial/stubbornness with the good that opposes mere omission. Identify which is greater and why Maturidi highlights this contrast.
The 'greater good' corresponds to the greatest evil of disbelief.
3
Understand the conclusion about justice
Maturidi argues that if God were to punish eternally without proportion, it would be generosity, not justice. Then he defines justice: reward for good should exceed punishment for evil. Finally, he cites the Quranic example: ten-fold reward for good, single for evil.
The key move is from 'jūd' (generosity) to 'ʿadl' (justice).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني إنه معلوم أن الذى يقابل الجحود والمعاندة من الخير أعظم وأجل من الذي يقابل ما كان في قبوله أن يفعل من الترك على نحو ما كان الجحود والمعاندة ، فجاء بالذى هو في الخير أعظم الخير ، وفى الشر لم يبلغ نهايته ، امن الآخر فإذا خلده في النار أبطل ثواب أفضل الخيرات بارتكاب ما دونه من الشر ، ورد ذلك وصف الجود لا العدل، والعدل أن يزيد فى ثواب ما جاء به على عقاب ما أتى به ، والله جل ثناؤه قد أخبر أنه يجزى الحسنة بعشرة أمثالها ، والسيئة بمثلها ، وفى هذا لم يبلغ المثل / فى الحسنة ولا قصر على المثل في السيئة ، جل الله عن ذلك وتعالى .
And the second [point]: It is known that the good which opposes denial and stubbornness is greater and more magnificent than that which opposes what was [only] in its acceptance to do from omission, in the manner that denial and stubbornness were. So He came with that which in good is the greatest good, and in evil did not reach its extreme. As for the other, if He consigns him eternally in the Fire, He nullifies the reward of the best good deeds by committing a lesser evil. That would be a description of generosity, not justice. Justice is that He should increase the reward of what one brings over the punishment of what one commits. God, exalted be His praise, has informed that He rewards the good deed with ten times its like, and the evil deed with its like. In this, He did not reach the like for the good deed nor did He restrict to the like for the evil deed. Exalted is God above that and highly exalted!
Source pages p. 938

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal argument about divine justice in reward and punishment. Maturidi begins by stating the second point: it is known that the good that opposes denial (juhūd) and stubbornness (muʿānada) is greater and more magnificent than the good that opposes what was only accepted by omission (tark) — that is, sins of omission. Since denial and stubbornness are the greatest evil, the good opposite them is the greatest good. In evil, the polytheist did not reach the extreme? Actually, the text says 'in evil did not reach its extreme' — perhaps meaning the evil of the polytheist is extreme but not beyond measure. Then Maturidi argues: if God were to consign such a person eternally in the Fire, He would nullify the reward of the best good deeds (the good he may have done) by committing a lesser evil — but that would be a description of generosity (jūd), not justice. Justice requires that the reward for the good one brings should exceed the punishment for the evil one commits. God has informed in the Quran that He rewards the good deed with ten times its like, and the evil deed with its like. In this divine dispensation, He did not give the like for the good deed (He gave more), nor did He restrict to the like for the evil deed (He gave exactly the like, not more). Thus, God's justice is manifested in multiplying reward and not exceeding in punishment. This exalts God above any injustice.

Key Terms
الجحود
المعاندة
الخير
الشر
العدل
الجود
الحسنة
السيئة
مثل
ثواب
عقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think eternal punishment for polytheists contradicts paying evil with its like.
Correction: Maturidi shows that because their evil is the greatest, eternal punishment is the like, and justice lies in proportional balance of reward and punishment.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does justice (al-ʿadl) require in reward and punishment?
A) Reward and punishment should be exactly equal to deeds
B) Reward should exceed punishment for the good one brings
C) Punishment should always be greater than reward
D) Generosity should replace justice

Key Takeaway
God’s justice ensures that the reward for good deeds exceeds the punishment for evil deeds, as shown by the Quranic principle of multiplying good deeds tenfold.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah multiplies every good deed you do, even the smallest, while recording each sin only once? How does this affect your motivation to do good and your fear of sin?
?
If you were to stand before Allah today, would you be confident that your good deeds outweigh your evil deeds? What is one specific habit you can change to tip the scale toward more good?
?
The text says that justice requires reward to exceed punishment. Do you judge others with this same standard—focusing on their good and being lenient on their faults? What would it look like for you to embody this divine justice in your relationships?

The Problem of Generalizing Promise and Threat Verses
Maturidi states the principle and identifies the contradiction of generalizing both promise and threat verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why interpreting both promise and threat verses as absolutely general leads to a contradiction and why this is problematic in theological reasoning.

Core Idea
Generalizing both promise and threat verses creates a contradiction, indicating irrationality.

Maturidi begins by stating a foundational principle: God has promised rewards for good deeds and threatened punishment for evil deeds in general terms. If one takes both sets of verses as absolutely general, a direct contradiction arises because the same person could be both promised reward and threatened with punishment. This contradiction signals a flaw in reasoning, forcing theologians to seek a more nuanced interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening principle
Read the first sentence of the passage where Maturidi states the principle of general promises and threats without specifying conditions.
Notice the phrase 'min ghayri dhikr ijtinabi al-kaba'ir' - without mentioning avoidance of major sins.
2
Identify the contradiction
Focus on the sentence 'faman wafaja al-ayatayni jami'an ila al-umum alzama al-tanaqud'. Understand that if both are general, they conflict for the same individual.
3
Recognize the conclusion
Note that Maturidi calls this an indication of foolishness (ayat al-safah). Reflect on why such a conclusion is unacceptable in kalam.
This sets the stage for the discussion of specification (takhsis).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل أن الله وعد على كثير من الخيرات وعدا من غير ذكر اجتناب الكبائر معه ، وأوعد على كثير من السيئات وعيدا في مخرج العموم ، [؟] على الخيرات ، فمن وجه الآيتين جميعا إلى العموم ألزم التناقض في جميع في واحد ، وذلك آية السفه ، ثم اضطربت فى ذلك الأقاويل. فزعمت المعتزلة والخوارج أن آيات الوعيد أحق بالعموم لها ، هى أبلغ فى الزجر والموعظة ، وزعمت المرجئة أن آيات الوعد أحق فى العموم لأنه أحق بالذي عرف من صفات الله من الرحمة والعفو والغفران فتقع عن الكبائر والصغائر ما يشهد لذلك قوله : إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَنْ يُشْرَكَ . مع احتمال الوعيد للمستحلين ، والوعد لو وجب التخصيص ليجب غيره والوعيد لنفسه ، فهو أولى به بالخصوص . مع ما شرط الدوام ليقع الوعيد ، وذلك آية الخصوص. وليس ذلك في الوعد فيلزم به الصرف إلى المستحلين ، أو إلى أن ذلك جزاؤه لولا الذى معه من الحسنات فيجب لديها المقابلة ، أو أن ذلك جزاؤه والله التفضل بالعفو عن ذلك بما علم من رجائه برحمته ، وعلمه بعظيم عفوه ، فلا يحرمه ذلك ، بما ظهر من فضله وإحسانه الذى بعثه على الرجاء ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then the principle: God promised many good deeds a promise without mentioning avoidance of major sins with it, and threatened many evil deeds a threat in general expression, [؟] upon good deeds. So whoever directs both sets of verses to generality necessarily leads to contradiction in all of them in one, and that is a sign of foolishness. Then the statements became confused regarding that. So the Mu'tazila and Khawarij claimed that the verses of threat are more entitled to generality because they are more effective in deterrence and admonition. The Murji'a claimed that the verses of promise are more entitled to generality because it is more befitting to what is known from God's attributes of mercy, pardon, and forgiveness, so they cover both major and minor sins; that which witnesses to this is His saying: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be ascribed to Him' (4:48), with the possibility that the threat applies to those who consider sins lawful, while the promise – if specification were necessary, it would necessitate something else, and the threat is for itself, so it is more deserving of specification. Along with what is conditioned of perpetuity so that the threat occurs, and that is a sign of specification. And that is not in the promise, so it necessitates directing it to those who consider sins lawful, or to the fact that that is its recompense were it not for the good deeds with it, so then confrontation becomes necessary, or that that is its recompense and God has the favor of pardoning that based on what He knows of hope in His mercy and knowledge of His great forgiveness, so He does not deprive him of that, based on what appears of His bounty and beneficence which instills hope, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 941

Commentary
This passage introduces a foundational principle in kalām concerning God's promises (wa'd) and threats (wa'īd). The author states that God has promised rewards for good deeds without explicitly requiring avoidance of major sins, and threatened punishment for evil deeds in general terms. If one interprets both sets of verses as absolutely general, a contradiction arises—the same person could be promised reward and threatened with punishment simultaneously—which is a sign of irrationality. The author then presents the positions of the two opposing groups: the Mu'tazila and Khawarij argue that threat verses are more general because their primary purpose is deterrence (zajr wa maw'iẓa). The Murji'a, on the other hand, argue that promise verses are more general because God's attributes of mercy and forgiveness are more characteristic of Him, and therefore promises can cover both minor and major sins, citing the Qur'anic verse 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be ascribed to Him' (4:48) which implies that lesser sins can be forgiven. The author then presents the Murji'a's reasoning in more detail: they claim that the threat can be specified (takhṣīṣ) to those who deem sins lawful (mustaḥillūn), while the promise cannot be specified without necessitating something else. They also rely on the condition of perpetuity (dawām) for threats, which indicates specification. The author reports their argument that promise verses, lacking such specification, must be directed either to those who deem sins lawful, or to a scenario where good deeds outweigh bad, or to God's voluntary pardon. Throughout, the author is presenting the views without yet endorsing any, setting the stage for further discussion.

Key Terms
عموم
تناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If promise and threat verses contradict when both general, then one set must be false.
Correction: The contradiction does not indicate falsity but rather the need for specification of one or both sets of verses, as the sects argue.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the consequence of taking both promise and threat verses as absolutely general?
A) It leads to a contradiction.
B) It leads to consistency.
C) It proves God's mercy.
D) It supports the Murji'a position.

Key Takeaway
Interpreting both promise and threat verses as absolutely general leads to a logical contradiction, necessitating a more specific interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse of promise and a verse of threat about the same action, do you ever feel torn between hope and fear? How do you reconcile them without falling into the contradiction Maturidi warns against?
?
This passage calls the one who generalizes both sets of verses a 'fool'. Is there an area in your own understanding of Islam where you have been applying a general rule without considering specification, leading to confusion? What would it take to correct that?
?
Maturidi points out that the contradiction is a sign of foolishness in interpretation. If you have ever felt that God's revelation seems contradictory, did you blame the text or your own understanding? How does this lesson challenge you to take responsibility for your interpretation?

The Mu'tazila and Khawarij View: Prioritizing Threat Verses
Mu'tazila and Khawarij argue threat verses should be general for deterrence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila and Khawarij argued that threat verses are more entitled to general interpretation than promise verses.

Core Idea
Threats are more general because they are more effective for deterrence.

After establishing the problem of contradiction, Maturidi presents the first of two major sectarian positions. The Mu'tazila and Khawarij argue that verses of threat (wa'id) should be taken as general because their primary purpose is to deter people from sin. They believe that generalizing threats is more effective for moral admonition than generalizing promises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sect position
Read the sentence: 'faza'amat al-Mu'tazila wa al-Khawarij anna ayat al-wa'id ahaqqu bi al-umum'. Note the claim that threat verses are more entitled to generality.
2
Understand their reasoning
The reason given: 'hiya ablaghu fi al-zajr wa al-maw'iza'. Reflect on why deterrence (zajr) would require generality.
They prioritize practical effect over potential conflict with promise verses.
3
Contrast with promise verses
Consider that they do not give a similar argument for promise verses. Why would threats need to be general more than promises?
This sets up the opposing view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila and Khawarij reject promise verses altogether.

Why do the Mu'tazila and Khawarij believe threat verses are more entitled to generality?
A) Because they are more numerous.

The Mu'tazila and Khawarij prioritize threat verses as general because of their greater deterrent effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you find yourself more moved by verses of threat or promise? Which actually drives you to repent or obey more?
?

?
If you had to choose one type of verse to meditate on daily—threat or promise—which would it be? What does your choice reveal about your current relationship with Allah and your understanding of His mercy?

The Murji'a View: Prioritizing Promise Verses
Murji'a argue promise verses should be general due to God's mercy, with supporting reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Murji'a argued that promise verses are more entitled to general interpretation than threat verses, and how they supported their position with reasoning and a Quranic verse.

Core Idea
Promises reflect God's mercy, so they should be general, while threats can be specified.

Maturidi then presents the second major position, that of the Murji'a. They argue the opposite: verses of promise (wa'd) should be taken as general because God's primary attributes of mercy, pardon, and forgiveness necessitate that His promises cover all sins, major and minor. They support their view with a Quranic verse and further reasoning about the nature of threats and promises.

Guided Path
1
Read the Murji'a claim
Read the sentence starting with 'wa za'amat al-Murji'a anna ayat al-wa'd ahaqqu fi al-umum'. Note their appeal to God's mercy and forgiveness.
2
Examine their evidence
They cite Quran 4:48 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be ascribed to Him' as evidence that lesser sins can be forgiven. Understand how this supports their view.
This verse implies that anything less than shirk is potentially forgivable.
3
Analyze their reasoning on threats
The Murji'a argue that threat verses can be specified (takhsis) to those who consider sins lawful (mustahillun), or they include a condition of perpetuity (dawam) which shows specification. Promises lack such conditions.
Follow the logic: if threat is for itself, it is more deserving of specification.
4
Consider the conclusion
They conclude that promise verses must be directed to either those who consider sins lawful, or mitigated by good deeds, or subject to God's voluntary pardon. Reflect on the implications for soteriology.
This aligns with the Murji'a belief that faith alone suffices for salvation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل أن الله وعد على كثير من الخيرات وعدا من غير ذكر اجتناب الكبائر معه ، وأوعد على كثير من السيئات وعيدا في مخرج العموم ، [؟] على الخيرات ، فمن وجه الآيتين جميعا إلى العموم ألزم التناقض في جميع في واحد ، وذلك آية السفه ، ثم اضطربت فى ذلك الأقاويل. فزعمت المعتزلة والخوارج أن آيات الوعيد أحق بالعموم لها ، هى أبلغ فى الزجر والموعظة ، وزعمت المرجئة أن آيات الوعد أحق فى العموم لأنه أحق بالذي عرف من صفات الله من الرحمة والعفو والغفران فتقع عن الكبائر والصغائر ما يشهد لذلك قوله : إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَنْ يُشْرَكَ . مع احتمال الوعيد للمستحلين ، والوعد لو وجب التخصيص ليجب غيره والوعيد لنفسه ، فهو أولى به بالخصوص . مع ما شرط الدوام ليقع الوعيد ، وذلك آية الخصوص. وليس ذلك في الوعد فيلزم به الصرف إلى المستحلين ، أو إلى أن ذلك جزاؤه لولا الذى معه من الحسنات فيجب لديها المقابلة ، أو أن ذلك جزاؤه والله التفضل بالعفو عن ذلك بما علم من رجائه برحمته ، وعلمه بعظيم عفوه ، فلا يحرمه ذلك ، بما ظهر من فضله وإحسانه الذى بعثه على الرجاء ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then the principle: God promised many good deeds a promise without mentioning avoidance of major sins with it, and threatened many evil deeds a threat in general expression, [؟] upon good deeds. So whoever directs both sets of verses to generality necessarily leads to contradiction in all of them in one, and that is a sign of foolishness. Then the statements became confused regarding that. So the Mu'tazila and Khawarij claimed that the verses of threat are more entitled to generality because they are more effective in deterrence and admonition. The Murji'a claimed that the verses of promise are more entitled to generality because it is more befitting to what is known from God's attributes of mercy, pardon, and forgiveness, so they cover both major and minor sins; that which witnesses to this is His saying: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be ascribed to Him' (4:48), with the possibility that the threat applies to those who consider sins lawful, while the promise – if specification were necessary, it would necessitate something else, and the threat is for itself, so it is more deserving of specification. Along with what is conditioned of perpetuity so that the threat occurs, and that is a sign of specification. And that is not in the promise, so it necessitates directing it to those who consider sins lawful, or to the fact that that is its recompense were it not for the good deeds with it, so then confrontation becomes necessary, or that that is its recompense and God has the favor of pardoning that based on what He knows of hope in His mercy and knowledge of His great forgiveness, so He does not deprive him of that, based on what appears of His bounty and beneficence which instills hope, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 941

Commentary
This passage introduces a foundational principle in kalām concerning God's promises (wa'd) and threats (wa'īd). The author states that God has promised rewards for good deeds without explicitly requiring avoidance of major sins, and threatened punishment for evil deeds in general terms. If one interprets both sets of verses as absolutely general, a contradiction arises—the same person could be promised reward and threatened with punishment simultaneously—which is a sign of irrationality. The author then presents the positions of the two opposing groups: the Mu'tazila and Khawarij argue that threat verses are more general because their primary purpose is deterrence (zajr wa maw'iẓa). The Murji'a, on the other hand, argue that promise verses are more general because God's attributes of mercy and forgiveness are more characteristic of Him, and therefore promises can cover both minor and major sins, citing the Qur'anic verse 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be ascribed to Him' (4:48) which implies that lesser sins can be forgiven. The author then presents the Murji'a's reasoning in more detail: they claim that the threat can be specified (takhṣīṣ) to those who deem sins lawful (mustaḥillūn), while the promise cannot be specified without necessitating something else. They also rely on the condition of perpetuity (dawām) for threats, which indicates specification. The author reports their argument that promise verses, lacking such specification, must be directed either to those who deem sins lawful, or to a scenario where good deeds outweigh bad, or to God's voluntary pardon. Throughout, the author is presenting the views without yet endorsing any, setting the stage for further discussion.

Key Terms
المرجئة
رحمة
عفو
غفران
مستحلون
دوام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Murji'a deny punishment entirely.
Correction: They do not deny punishment; they restrict threat verses to specific conditions (mustahillun or perpetuity) and emphasize God's mercy for believers.

Checkpoint
What Quranic verse do the Murji'a cite to support their view that promise verses are general?
A) 3:26
B) 4:48
C) 2:284
D) 39:53

Key Takeaway
The Murji'a argue that God's mercy and forgiveness make His promises generally applicable, while His threats can be specified to certain conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read about God's mercy and forgiveness described by the Murji'a, do you find yourself taking comfort in His promise while neglecting the warnings? What specific sin in your life are you currently banking on God's mercy to cover without actively working to change? Will you commit today to confront that sin rather than assume it is automatically forgiven?
?
The Murji'a argue that threat verses can be 'specified' to avoid their full impact. In your own heart, do you ever 'specify' or minimize God's warnings to make yourself more comfortable? Where in your life are you explaining away a clear warning from Allah because you hope His mercy will override it? Are you willing to accept that both promise and threat are real and demand a response?
?
Imagine standing before Allah tomorrow, and He asks you: 'Did you take my promises as a license for sin, or did they drive you to gratitude and obedience?' Based on this lesson, are you more like the Murji'a who focus on mercy, or are you balanced? What needs to change starting today?
